Introduction: 
Bacteria, being known to the human race as unicellular since its discovery has been debatable a lot in the last decade. (Guttman, 1998) has retold the story of their discovery: They were introduced to humans as minute single celled viable creatures. These were seen as rods under the microscope by the renowned scientist Anton van Leeuwenhoek as he was examining the anthrax bacterial cells. Thus, the name bacteria emerged from the predecessor word Baktron which means a staff. He also added that these were one of the strong indications of the theory of evolution as many scientists considered these micro-organisms to have come to existence randomly and automatically after many amino acids have been collected together. This latter process has occurred without any external interference as was assumed by the author and many other microbiologists.  These bacteria have the properties of other cells except for the distinct nuclear membrane which these lack. Therefore, the classification of cells into prokaryotes and eukaryotes was introduces in 1962 by Roger Stanier and C. B. van Niel   based on the citation Édouard Chatton's 1937 book Titres et Travaux Scientifiques 
However, for the last few decades with the revolution in technology and information data accumulation showed that bacteria are in living multicellular groups of cells. Formation of these commonalities is a global characteristic of bacteria and these are called biofilm.  The later are multicellular groups of bacteria and other cells like fungi held together by an autonomically-produced extracellular matrix.  Taking in consideration and comparing the tissues of most eukaryotes like mammals and humans (which are groups of different types of cells embedded in extracellular matrix) the argument of whether these are unicellular or multicellular have emerged  . Another feature of multicellularity is apoptosis (programmed cell death) which is mostly linked to much more complex multicellular organism. In this process the community will sacrifice the most fit cells instead of the unfit ones to foster for their nutrients  . Moreover, this multicellular existence provides better protection against different adverse environmental conditions and permits better function of individual cells. These latter manifests itself in the form of cellular differentiation and the acquirement of different other properties which gives advantage for the whole population .  Other features which can be counted to multicellularity is that these biofilms develop in to three dimensional structures with pockets of differentiated cells  . Moreover, there are different types of communications between cells including genetic transfer and quorum sensing with at least some level of communalism or at least non-benefit (alism) among these cells  . Furthermore, the expression of certain genes requires Many bioﬁlm cells to be attached to a surface . Recently, futo et al. have showed according to physical structure and molecular studies that biofilm growth and development are similar to those of embryogenic stages of eukaryotes in sense that they are deeply organized and formulated in discrete stages of development  . Also, multicellular counterparts are present like cynobacteria    . Based on these and other discussions and assumptions Penesyan, A., Paulsen, I.T., Kjelleberg, S. et al. have written one of a kind and an extensive systematic review arguing whether biofilms are multicellular organisms. In this review they have postulated that biofilms are the aggregation of cells which benefits the whole community by living together. But still the debate is ongoing.  
 The current paper is meant to be an encyclopedia and a collection of features of biofilms that might not have been discussed. At the same time to serve as a mini literature review where the writer would discuss the possibility of the section to serve as multicellularity of biofilms. Due to the huge data that will be presented possible literatures will be discussed and the succeeding discussions will be short. This present paper will be published in few chapters and the last one will be the more extensive discussion.

Image. 6 multicellular cyanobacteria, taken from www.cell.com  
Mahadevan (2002) says: “One take-home message from these studies is that we may need to get rid of the implicit conviction that the bacterium, being most familiar to us as a unicellular creature, is also the biological equivalent of the physicist’s ‘independent particle’. The other is that the Petri dish remains a powerful tool to gain new insights on the wonders of the microbial world” 
 
1. Chapter one: bacteria are well known for spreading infections and outbreaks
1.1. biofilms are the causes of outbreaks 
Dolgushin & Mezentseva (2020), have discussed (page 469): It was Well known for years that bacteria were invading the human bodies in planktonic cells and were disrupting the host immune system by the multiplications of bacterial cells  . Also, Azeredo et al. (2016) discussed  (page 314): it was known that these cells would will act solely and are not bind to their counterparts of the same species.  Moreover, in recent years this was slowly turning to become a bit old as the labs showed that bacterial infections as well were caused by bacterial biofilms.  
1.2. yersinia pestis in the past that caused plague had a biofilm: the plague has been confirmed to cause deaths for many millennia 
Studies have shown that the multicellular form (biofilm) is also the existing form for Yersinia Pestis.
Hinnebusch BJ, Erickson DL. (2008) say:” Transmission by fleabite is a relatively recent evolutionary adaptation of Yersinia pestis, the bacterial agent of bubonic plague. To produce a transmissible infection, Y. pestis grows as an attached biofilm in the foregut of the flea vector. Biofilm formation both in the flea foregut and in vitro is dependent on an extracellular matrix (ECM) synthesized by the Yersinia hms gene products” .
      
Image. 7 Y. Pestis biofilm inside the flea. Image taken from http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/instance/3727414/bin/nihms489972f4.jpg
 
 Image.8 Y. Pestis inside the gut of the flea. Image taken from http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/instance/3727414/bin/nihms489972f1.gif

This in in turn shows the ability of Y. pestis to block the flea's foregut with a biofilm is a highly specialized evolutionary trait that essentially forces the flea to regurgitate the bacteria into a new host during feeding. This is a profound example of bacteria organizing structurally to manipulate a macro-organism for their own transmission.
Other Epidemics are also caused by Biofilms
In human history Several of the most devastating infectious diseases and outbreaks depend strongly on biofilm formation for their survival and transmission:
1.3. Cholera (Vibrio cholerae): 
V. cholerae thrives naturally in water systems, where it endures between disease outbreaks by attaching to the chitinous shells of zooplankton (particularly copepods). This attachment triggers the formation of intricate biofilms, a key strategy for its environmental persistence (Almagro-Moreno et al., 2015 ; Reidl, 2002 ). The highly acidic environment of the human stomach represents a formidable barrier to infection. However, the extracellular matrix encasing biofilm clusters shields the aggregated bacteria from this harsh gastric acid, granting them a significantly higher survival rate than their individual, planktonic counterparts. (He et al., 2012 ). The journey continues as the biofilm clusters pass through the stomach and arrive in the small intestine. Here, the bacteria detach from the biofilm, breach the mucosal barrier, and begin to multiply. Their subsequent release of cholera toxin causes the severe, watery diarrhea characteristic of the disease. (He et al., 2012 ).
1.4. Typhoid Fever (Salmonella enterica serovar Typhi): 
Epidemiological and clinical investigations have established a robust correlation between chronic typhoid carriage and cholelithiasis, with cholesterol gallstones present in up to 90% of carriers. Subsequent laboratory analyses utilizing scanning electron microscopy (SEM) have visually confirmed that S. Typhi colonizes these calculi, elaborating a dense extracellular polymeric substance (EPS) matrix characteristic of biofilm formation. The biofilm acts as biological armor, creating a 3D sanctuary where S. Typhi is shielded from both host immunity and pharmacological intervention. The EPS matrix blocks immune cells and antibodies while simultaneously restricting antibiotic diffusion—a dual defense that renders conventional oral or intravenous therapy almost universally ineffective in asymptomatic carriers.  
When the human host eats a meal, the gallbladder contracts to release bile into the small intestine to aid in the digestion of fats. The planktonic S. Typhi bacteria hitch a ride with this bile, survive the journey through the intestines, and are excreted in the feces. Upon localization to the gallbladder, S. Typhi is confronted with bile—a complex physiological secretion composed of bile acids, bilirubin, phospholipids, and cholesterol—each contributing to its formidable antimicrobial characteristics.  Biofilm development proceeds through a series of precisely regulated, sequential phases: initial adhesion of planktonic bacteria to a surface, subsequent elaboration of a protective, self-assembled extracellular matrix (ECM), and eventual dispersal of free-swimming cells from the established sessile community into the surrounding environment.  Currently, cholecystectomy—the surgical removal of the gallbladder—continues to be the most definitive treatment for chronic typhoid carriers who present with gallstones. However, in regions where typhoid fever is endemic and surgical procedures are financially inaccessible, there exists a critical demand for alternative therapeutic strategies. 
1.5. Legionnaires' Disease (Legionella pneumophila): 
Legionella pneumophila is a significant cause of community-acquired and nosocomial pneumonia, with outbreaks frequently linked to colonization of modern built environment water systems. The bacterium persists within engineered water systems—including cooling towers, air conditioning units, and potable plumbing—by forming robust, multi-species biofilms that confer resistance to disinfectants and environmental stressors  .Within these biofilm communities, L. pneumophila resists eradication through conventional control measures and demonstrates enhanced environmental persistence. 
A critical mechanism underlying this ecological success is the facultative intracellular parasitism of free-living amoebae (e.g., *Acanthamoeba* spp.) present within biofilms. L. pneumophila invades, replicates within, and ultimately lyses these protozoan hosts, a process that not only provides a protected niche for bacterial amplification but also selects for virulence traits effective against human alveolar macrophages. Importantly, intra-amoebal growth primes L. pneumophila for enhanced biofilm colonization by upregulating polysaccharide production and altering bacterial surface properties, thereby increasing its capacity to establish and persist within new biofilm habitats. This amoeba-enhanced biofilm state subsequently shelters the bacterium from chlorine disinfection and thermal stress.    Aerosolization of bacteria-laden vesicles or planktonic cells released from these protected biofilm reservoirs results in human inhalation and subsequent risk of Legionnaires' disease. 
1.6. Tuberculosis (Mycobacterium tuberculosis): 
Transmission of Mycobacterium tuberculosis occurs primarily via aerosolized droplets; however, upon establishing infection in the pulmonary environment, the pathogen aggregates into highly organized structures, including granulomas and pellicles that demonstrate properties analogous to biofilms (Liu et al., 2024 ; Sambandan et al., 2013 ).
 
Such structural organization renders tuberculosis exceptionally difficult to eradicate, requiring patients to undergo months of combination antibiotic therapy (Ehlers & Schaible, 2013  ; Sarangi et al., 2024 ).
1.7. Does the above support the Multicellularity Theory?
The behaviour of these pathogens strongly reinforces the concept of bacterial multicellularity. 
Pathogenic bacteria often employ a strategy of delayed virulence to evade host immune detection. Rather than initiating an attack immediately upon entry, they rely on a cell-density-dependent regulatory mechanism known as quorum sensing (QS). This process involves the constitutive release and detection of small signaling molecules (autoinducers), allowing the bacterial population to estimate its own density. Virulence factors—including toxins and tissue-degrading enzymes—are only transcribed and secreted once a critical threshold population, or "quorum," has been achieved (Rutherford and Bassler  ; de Kievit and Iglewski  ). This coordinated, simultaneous activation creates the clinical impression of a synchronized ambush. From an evolutionary perspective, this behavior prevents the premature triggering of host defenses, ensuring the bacterial colony has reached a sufficient size to overwhelm the immune response and establish a successful infection (Rumbaugh et al. ).
On the other hand, Pathogens often infect the immunocompromised, the elderly, or those with underlying conditions first. This is because a healthy human (a stable multicellular) has a robust immune system and a healthy microbiome that naturally outcompetes or destroys the invading pathogen. TB is the classic example of an epidemic exploiting immune weakness. It is estimated that latent M. tuberculosis reactivates in 5-10% of immunocompetent hosts over their lifetime, but this risk rises exponentially in HIV-coinfected individuals. The depletion of CD4+ T-cells by HIV destroys the very immune architecture required to contain M. tuberculosis within granulomas . A weakened host represents an "open ecological niche" with fewer defences, allowing the invading bacterial community to establish its biofilm easily. While the rule holds true for severity, the transmission pattern can sometimes favor healthier hosts in the early stages of a novel epidemic. Healthier, more mobile populations (e.g., young adults) often drive the initial geographic spread of a pathogen because they have higher rates of social interaction and travel. They get infected first, but they usually survive. The immunocompromised get infected later, but they are more likely to die.  
1.8. conclusion of chapter one: 
In this chapter, different microbial infections and epidemics were discussed. Specifically focusing on the concept of these bacteria being spread in a biofilm form (multicellular form) when they spread and infect. The features these biofilms exhibit that can strengthen the multicellular behavior are: Structured architecture, Extracellular matrix production, Nutrient gradients, Quorum sensing, Division of labour and Stress tolerance. The biofilms that infect in biofilm form were the plague, cholera, typhoid, legionella pneumonia and TB. Also, many other bacteria strains and infections have been found to infect in a biofilm state. These are namely, Pseudomonas aeruginosa in cystic fibrosis, otitis media infection, salmonella food borne infection, listeria food borne infection, dental plaques and many other nosocomial infections. It is becoming more evident that bacteria prefer to live and is actually living in multicellular pattern. Despite this multicellular form is less preferable if the sole interests of a single cell are being taken in consideration. On the contrary, many cells tend to sacrifice or give less interest in the survival of itself on the consequence of the health and integrity of the whole biofilm (community). Moreover, it is more evident that single cells of bacteria and biofilms mostly prefer travelling to distant places and choose less preferable conditions in the beginning or at a period of their lifecycle. It also seems to endure many more harsh or destructing conditions as a biofilm to reach a place or an organ where it may evade the host immune system and can thrive more efficiently and can be more lethal in such a place. To a distant looking multicellular organism like myself, this seems to be alluring and conspicuously clear that an entity to have such an amazing preprogrammed like journey to its final destination the organism should have many complicated tools to do so. Also, to have a tandem working cells at least one or few organisms must exist. These might have many sensory organs like light sensation, sound sensation, tactile organs and an organ that interprets these inputs and would give orders other organs to have their own reactions. The last point would be discussed in the following chapter. 
One last bit is that Whole-genome sequencing (WGS) of biofilms produced by pathogens such as Staphylococcus aureus and uropathogenic Escherichia coli (UPEC) frequently reveals the presence of a dominant genetic lineage. For instance, an investigation into biofilm-forming S. aureus isolates associated with septic arthritis identified a specific, human-adapted lineage designated ST398 (Cafiso et al., 2021).  Does this last bit mean that a single species biofilm has a single cell origin or ancestor. What about multicellular ones, what is the mechanism and are they genetically autonomous which in turn lead to an idea that they might act as cells from different organs and what are the other implications of these. This paper will dive deep into these points. 

 
2. Chapter two: characteristics of a multicellular biofilm:
Here the characteristics that a multicellular organism might possess would be discussed and compiled for biofilms:
2.1. Biofilm and microbiome: sound waves production, communication and the effects of sound waves
The first research undertaken to be recall in this specific area was Matsuhashi et al (1998) when they have discovered that bacterial cells of b. Subtilis have produced sound waves in the range that human ear can detect and in the range of ultrasonic (8-43KHZ) utilizing special sound recorder and receiver (Matsuhashi et al., 1998)  . The researchers have replaced the wave producing cells on agar to a sine wave with frequencies and power outage at the same which the b. subtilis produced. Surprisingly when this process was done another receiver strain of bacteria (b. carboniphilus) living on a non-permissive agar of KCL or antibiotic have initiated and enhanced the growth their growth . Mahdadevan, S (2002) have scientifically translated this bit that the power of these sound and ultrasonic sine waves to be less than that of a human whisper at certain frequency which were frequencies in the range of 10 kHz to 40 kHz at a power output of ~ 50 mW, as human ear do not hear all the frequency at the same sensitivity (Matsuhashi et al., 1998) . More, interestingly, in a previous research bacterial colony boosted the buildup of other bacterial cells (of same strain or different) through the emission of sonic waves. These were observed to occur even when thick plastic 2.5 mm cover or 2mm thick plate separated those two agars.  . Therefore, Mahadevan, S (2002) reported:” So, with some license, one might say that bacteria talk to each other. Note that the relevant wavelengths are in the range of 1–3 cm, which are 10 4 -fold or so larger than the length of a single bacterium. The mechanism whereby the sounds are generated is unknown; clearly, a collective phenomenon is indicated” (Mahadevan, 2002) . 
The impact of other sonic waves like music instrument on bacterial virulence, metabolism and effects of antibiotics effectiveness was tested. The results of Sarvaiya, N., Kothari, V (2015) showed positive effects on most of the strains tested . The latter is further concreted by the research undertaken by Cui B, Su D, Li W, She X, Zhang M, Wang R, Zhai Q. where they subject young SAMP8 mice to long term noise exposure. This research yielded the finding of more AB accumulation in tested mice which were compared to their elder counterparts. Furthermore, this sound pollution led to less diverse gut microbiota and change in its composition which might be accredited for astounding change. Likewise, latter changes had affected the endothelial tight junctions to be lower than normal and the inflammatory markers and neurotransmitters to be more than normal. These levels were comparable to those of old mice  . Fewer other research have found that sonically treated antibiotic eradication of biofilms   , Pseudomonas aeruginosa biofilm destruction  , and E. coli destruction   was enhanced by the manipulation with these different frequencies od sounds.
Gu S, Zhang Y, Wu Y have studied the direct impact of different audible frequencies on E. coli K12. They have acquired the facts that different parameters were change mostly on the cellular level with the most prominent to be transcriptional protein synthesis and aided rate of cell growth  .
A 2016 study featured in the Journal of Bioscience and Bioengineering revealed that applying gentle, low-frequency acoustic vibrations can substantially boost the formation of bacterial biofilms. The research showed that this physical stimulus not only increased overall cell counts but also organized their spatial arrangement, with Pseudomonas aeruginosa responding best to 800 Hz and Staphylococcus aureus showing peak formation at 1600 Hz. 
Discussion: the sound waves produced are larger 10000 times or more than the cells dimensions. Since, sound or voice are vibrations caused by the original object and need mechanical energy, the energy that would take a single cell to produce repetitive sound waves would be more than the capacity of the cell by many thousand time. Also, if bacteria to be unicellular why the collective sound waves would impact the collective growth of the other strain. Also, what would be the effect of the waves produced by bacteria on human and mammalian cells. Furthermore, if bacteria are multicellular what are the effects of these waves produced by the microbiota dwelling in the ears and other parts? Would it be able to affect the human behavior (change in their social behavior)? Also, what would be the effect of these sonic waves on the other microbes like plasmodium and fungi. Moreover, what are their impact on the behavior of their biofilm (fungi and plasmodium).
Further much important questions to be investigated if these creatures are multicellular are: first: can these act as the creature’s sensory organs consist of few cell types that are specialized for this sensation. E.g b. carboniphilus are excited or changed by the sound waves that resemble those produced by b. subtilis. Can these be part of the collective specialized cells (organ) for detection of sound waves that act like an ear. Can b. subtilis and other strains which produce sound wave can they be a part of a mechanical organ that produce sound which might resemble tongue and vocal cords in higher animals. One last home takeaway message is that if they are unicellular why would they have to put extra burden and energy on a single cell for a distant colony to fight against antibiotic beside the burden for the neighboring cells. 
2.2. Do biofilms feel? Do they have tactile sensation? Biofilms and reshaping:
On the planktonic level mechanical sensation of the stimuli is very important. (Dufrêne and Persat, 2020) discussed that Bacteria dwelling in high pressure areas in the deep waters (oceans and seas) can move and flourish in these adverse conditions of which might reach 103 of atmospheric pressure.   . This feature can be attributed to the polar and the lateral flagella by the deep sea Photobacterium profundum. Polar flagella cannot operate under extremely high pressures. Therefore, the latter flagella lead to the production of the structures of lateral flagella under the aforementioned deleterious conditions. This new system of flagella can provide motility for the bacterium inside very viscous and highly pressurized elements. This led to the conclusion that these systems are part of the mechanosensory structure of these strains   . Theoretically, these motile bacteria and strains become less motile in the transition to biofilms. This might lead to the process of inhibition of bacterial movement by these flagella  . Cairns et al., 2013 have discussed that B. subtilis production of certain genes of the biofilm matrix component occurs after adherence to a facet and impedance of flagellar rotation.  . One would argue that these flagella are utilized by single bacterium. The answer can be that losing these structures is a late process at the time lapse of biofilm maturation and growth.
In regards to the biofilm sensation Siryaporn A  et. al (2014) suggested that Pseudomonas aeruginosa sense physical signals and react based up on these.  After utilization of special technique those researchers were able to detect a change in virulence in reaction to different specific areas which gives clues that biofilm react to mechanical properties. In order for the quorum sensing to induce area-triggered virulence it should adhere to a surface. Using a domain homologous to a eukaryotic mechanosensor that lead to the activation of virulence by surfaces through the surface-exposed protein PilY1.   The examples would manifest in the E. coli where the sticking component of the kind 1 pili senses the alteration in the water dynamics pressure, consequently this leads to surge in the attachment of bacteria to surfaces (Thomas et al., 2002).  In other bacteria, getting approximation with a surface induces the production of polysaccharides that help the cells to strongly to a substrate .  
The exopolymeric matrix EPS produced by bacteria is an important mechanical perseverance factor for the attachment of cells of the biofilms together. Therefore Renner, L. D. & Weibel, D have postulated that this mechanical property of EPS leads to the regulation of distribution of cellular factors to sustain the biofilm shape and integrity. This might be deployed through feedback on matrix deterioration or synthesis   .   This can be seen in P. aeruginosa biofilm where a feedback mechanism induces matrix production when the cells perceive constituents of the matrix.  Irie, Y. et al.  . Change in viscoelasticity of the matrix under different dynamic pressures during different stage of biofilm life is another example (Jana et al., 2020) .  ONE OF THE MECHANICAL irritants that affect the spatial orientation of cells inside the biofilm is production of new bacterial cells. (Douarche et al., 2015).   . The level of collaboration or competence among different cells of multispecies biofilms is altered by the position of those cells and their social interactions. (Nadel et al., 2016).   
Bridier A, Piard JC, Pandin C, Labarthe S, Dubois-Brissonnet F, Briandet R. have illustrated Another proponent of the mechanosensitivity and the most important and obvious among the other features is the change of the shape of the biofilm from 2d to 3d structures which is called plasticity. They further elucidated that architectural changes in biofilm benefit the whole population as these diversifications make the biofilm and each bacterium adapt to various environmental stressors.    . one the illustrations of this phenomenon, is the modification of architectural framework, species content and by change in spatial construct are methods and mechanisms by which biofilms in river stream resist and adapt to adverse conditions. (Battin et al., 2016; Sabater et al., 2016).    . Another example of the of the biofilm (adaptation) reaction based on mechanosensation is the change in shape of the biofilm of Bacillus subtilis with varying levels of nutrients. This manifested in the form of interior redesigning of the biofilm to exhibit large straight folds which might act as connection between the core and the outer edges. ( Gingichashvili S, Duanis-Assaf D, Shemesh M, Featherstone JDB, Feuerstein O, Steinberg D.)  . Other illustrations of similar phenomena were stressed by Beroz F, Yan J, Sabass B, Stone HA, Bassler BL, Wingreen NS, Meir Y.   (Habimana et al., 2014; . Decho, 2016;   Lennon and Lehmkuhl, 2016 ). 
Discussion: 
What is in favor of these sensations to be regarded as features of biofilm multicellularity is the collective and complex reaction of the whole biofilm cells to different physical stimuli. Besides, what also aid multicellularity concept is different responses of same species dominant biofilms under diverse conditions. One of the very elicit indications and example of the sensory organ that most journals and scientific article would not tackle is the Streptococcus mitis. NASA has recorded that “Space historians will recall that the journey to the stars has more than one life form on its passenger list: the names of a dozen Apollo astronauts who walked on the moon and one inadvertent stowaway, a common bacterium, Streptococcus mitis, the only known survivor of unprotected space travel. As Marshall astronomers and biologists met recently to discuss biological limits to life on Earth, the question of how an Earth bacterium could survive in a vacuum without nutrients, water and radiation protection was less speculative than might first be imagined”.  The quick inquiry is: how this adaptation has happened so quickly if the bacteria did not have a complex sensory and response system (nervous system). Also, if this process is not on the multicellular level, then only those cells which were able to sense the change would’ve survived.
 If the above concept is true then there are some answers to some of these queries: first: can these pilY and flagella and other undiscovered apparatus in bacteria be the analogue of Pacinian or meissner's corpuscles in higher multicellular organisms. Second: can bacterial biofilm sense different kinds of physical stimuli separately and react based on it (pain, heat, pressure, vibration etc.). Third: what is the role of skin microbiota in pain sensation of humans? Also, is there any relationship between skin microbiota and tactile induce analgesia? Fourth: can this mechanosensation and quick response in biofilms be governed by a higher nervous like system that has neurons like appendages and or a brain like structure?
2.3. Bacteria can sense light:
Seeing or visualization of objects by humans is the sensory process by which light is sensed by the eyes (sensory organs). These eyes consist of millions of specialized cells which begin with focusing light then sensing colors and transferring the image to the brain via nerves where the image will be processed. The whole process involves different lines of cells for which each type of process involved in the steps of image processing. Can different bacteria possess specialized cells for the different stages of image processing?
In the beginning: the photoreception ability among different bacterial strains should be investigated. In this regards cyanobacterium Synechocystis sp. PCC 6803 after estimation of the magnitude of light it becomes motile with type IV pili.  The investigators have shown that these bacteria anticipate and measure the direction of the light origin. These researchers have attributed this phenomenon to be due to the fact that these bacteria act as spherical microlenses that permit passing the light rays to pass through the cell membrane and cell wall of the bacterium. The researchers supposed that once this light passes to inside the cell is reflected on the opposite boundary of the same cell to produce a high- definition picture which in turn triggers bacterial motion. Then these authors have proposed that this is an example for the smallest unit for vision resembling a mini eye   . Well, these authors have proposed this idea without taking in consideration that this strain of bacteria can form biofilms. Kera, K., Yoshizawa, Y., Shigehara, T. et al. have stated that it is possible for this specific strain of bacteria to form collections of bacteria that are surrounded by a self-produced EPS in a reactionary response to environmental stress like very prolonged exposure to high concentration salt. However, the pure regulatory cellular mechanism behind this process is still unknown .  . The above writers have proved that among bacteria are cells that resembles some types of cells of the lenses for more advanced multicellular organisms.
On the other hand, there are different responses of variable bacterial biofilms to changing light spectrum and conditions that have been recorded. PsPto a low intensity phyllosphere opportunistic, in order for the bacterium to switch to high intensity phyllospehre infection it has to contact the leaf. This contact with the leaf is enough for the induction of the gene transcriptions necessary for this switch in virulence activity. Despite this independence to light condition for the gene transcription, but the expression of gene reprogramming is largely aided by blue light in the early stages of settlement of bacterial colonies. Besides, this blue light helped facilitate the declaration of the neurotransmitters, fatty acid, iron and certain other materials responsible for the confrontation against the immunity of the plant.  . Also, Different phototrophic bacterial behaviors have been recorded and varying responses can be displayed in different bacteria ranging from photosynthesis, chemotaxis, mobilization toward light sources and disposing waste products. Armitage and Hellingwerf, 2003; Wilde and Mullineaux, 2017), (Hoff et al., 1997; Armitage and Hellingwerf, 2003). Kraiselburd et al., 2012; Wu et al., 2013; Rio-Alvarez et al., 2014; Bonomi et al., 2016; Santamaria-Hernando et al., 2018), (Delprato et al., 2001).  The researchers have noticed an obvious deviation of the colony bacterial structure with light gradient.  Bacteria obtaining energy from sunlight like Cyanobacteria and on other likely phototrophic bacterial taxa such as Rhodobacter and Roseococcus (Yurkov, 2006) witnessed the most direct influence of light.   
In addition to these direct effects, some bacterial taxa with predominantly heterotrophic lifestyles appeared to increase with light (e.g. Candidate division TM7, Roseomonas and Rivibacter), whereas others decreased (e.g. Planctomycetes and Gemmatimonadetes). This may reflect preferences towards utilization of autochthonous and allochthonous organic matter, respectively, although it is difficult to make functional predictions based on such trends alone. (sensu Schimel and Schaeffer, 2012),. Wagner K, Besemer K, Burns NR, Battin TJ, Bengtsson MM  2015 and (Peter et al., 2011). All those researchers shed the light on the fact that enzymatic activities and multifunctionality are better sustained under high light condition.  
Another amazing reaction to light was the whole biofilm community metabolism shifting from those mixed dependent on light and dim condition to more dependent on light under high light availability.   . on the other hand, other strains show the opposite to the above reaction. Mukherjee S et al have discussed: pseudomonas aeruginosa biofilm is repressed under light conditions. This indeed means that P. aeruginosa which previously was unknown to sense light now is being popular for this through varying cellular mechanisms. . Losi, A., Gärtner, W have suggested: Another amazing reaction is that when symbiotic biofilms living with plants changes their behaviors and virulence under changing light conditions. . on the other hand, window light has the same sterilizing effect as ultraviolet for some taxa on the contrary to others... . another photo sensing features is that different bacterial taxa can be stimulated by different light color or wavelengths. E.g is brucella abortus once the virulence is activated by blue light it will not return to its previous status. 
2.3.1. Discussion 
The above research and reviews shed the light on the fact that some bacterial strains act as lens’ cells in multicellular organisms. Another, point was that the whole biofilm reaction to different spectrums of light. Does this mean that different biofilms possess an eye resembling structure. The resemblance would be in the sense that they allow or permit different light spectrums to enter that structure. Then other types of cells would interpret these input signals to be send by other specialized cells to be processed into an image. If this is true then the following questions should be investigated:
1. do these creatures possess other cells that have different specializations like photosensation and image processing?
2. what is the spectrum with which these eye resembling structures can detect light (visible, IR, UV….etc).
3. can these see in dim or in bright light condition? This means are they adapted to be better see in night or day light.
4. can these creatures actually see humans as a whole? 
5. does sensing some certain images by these eyes like structure increase the virulence of these creatures? (e.g. seeing the nose of by pseudomonas Aeruginosa dominant biofilm) and or sensing others might lead to decrease in the virulence.  
2.4 visualization of biofilms by humans? 
2.4.1 Seeing the biofilms:
 one of the points that a person would have an argument about is that if biofilms were multicellular organisms which act a single unit most humans would have seen them by the naked eye or at least under the light microscope. The answer would be in sections 
2.4.1.a. the extracellular polymeric matrix 
The extracellular polymeric matrix is transparent in most of biofilms.  N RAMAIAH. et. Al. (2000) discussed that availability of big, distinct polymeric debris that may be assimilated by bacteria that make up the component of the marine biofilms is of great importance. (Passow and Alldredge 1995, Smith et al 1995) (Alldredge et al 1993; Kumar et al 1998)”.  Also, Research from 2016 highlights that visualizing EPS components is difficult because they possess a low electron density and a high degree of molecular heterogeneity. 
The above is called transparent exopolymeric particles these are the components of the ECM.
2.4.1.b. seeing the bacterial cells directly by humans:
It is well known that They are transparent: Bacteria will show their color only if they are present in a colony in vitro. Usually, Regular bright-field microscopes will only reveal the bacterial cells if one closes the condenser iris diaphragm. This in turn leads to the fact in the visible spectrum to the human eyes their cells are transparent and are out of the limits that human eyes can sense. Moreover, to visualize most of them scientist usually stain most of these cells that are examined under microscope. Another question that would arise: can they be detected in the non-visible spectrum range. Douglas RH, Jeffery G. (2014) say: “Other mammals, however, such as hedgehogs, dogs, cats, ferrets and okapis had lenses transmitting significant amounts of UVA (315–400 nm), suggesting that they will be UV-sensitive even without a specific UV visual pigment”. . on the other hand, it is a well-documented fact that the human optical spectrum, is between 390nm-740 nm. However, the latter research reveals that dogs' lenses can transmit Ultra violet lights ranging between 315-400 nm. Moreover, this range which dogs and other animals can visualize is the range under which some kinds of bacteria can be visualized directly on the skin using wood's lamp. Also, this range of frequencies would seem transparent to humans but not those animals.   Schaller, M. et. al. (2005) has said that one of the metabolite of Propionibacteria is porphyrin. This pigment can be seen directly by human eyes on the skin when examined under wood’s lamp (ultraviolet-A 320–400 nm) . This latter research indicates the fact that bacterial cells and the pigments that their cells produce are not within the limits of human eyes ability to see (will be seen as transparent or as parts of other living or non-living things like human skin).  This in turn might raise the question if these are multicellular in which range they can be seen?
 
Image. 9. Porphyrin produced by bacteria in the armpit. Image from http://www.skinsight.com/cosmetics/woodsLampExamination.htm
2.4.2.  Vibration of Biofilms 
The recent evidences indicate the fact that bacterial cells can vibrate and oscillate. An oscillating object that moves faster than the eye can detect would be invisible to the human eyes. Also, this is evident on the community level. (Needs permission) Song, L. (2015) has stated:  there is a stagnant point around which bacterial cells have been illustrated to pulsate on a nanoscopic level which is a random process in adherent bacterial cells.  The author compared the resonating magnitude of different bacteria on a testing glass. The stiffness constant (spring) of the bonds resulting from the resonating of the bacteria were measured. He used different six varying bacterial strains for this reason. The results yielded the fact that in a decreased ionic liquid medium these vibrations were highest. On the other hand, when the liquid movement is present in the medium the vibrations were lowest against the direction flow.  The strains examined were: Streptococcus salivarius HB7 (145 nm), its bald mutant HB-C12 (76 nm), Staphylococcus epidermidis ATCC35983 (47 nm), Staphylococcus epidermidis ATCC35984 (21 nm) and two S. aureus strains. . On the biofilm level Garde, R., Ibrahim, B. & Schuster, et. al. has discovered: movement around a fixed point are suggested to be beneficial for the whole biofilm to withstand lack of nutrient and chemical attacks. These researchers have discussed a mathematical method for the vibration of Bacillus subtilis biofilms. . Also, on the biofilm level Martinez-Corral, Rosa et. al. have discussed that the congregational flexibility can increase in the biofilm under stressful events. This is achieved in one way by whole biofilm cell vibration which in turn this provides a support for the whole community. This latter process usually will start to become constant as the communities grow in size where the amplitude slowly increasing. These reactions and responses are in favor of the survival and betterment of the whole community. . … Taherzadeh D, Picioreanu C, Horn H.  have confirmed another clue to the fact that these oscillations and vibrations are beneficial for biofilms for the stream biofilms. Theses possess a non-movable base and a movable tail that swings quickly in fast streams. Their reports have confirmed that there is an increase in nutrient movement to inside due to the tail swinging movement.  . 
Those researchers have found that bacterial cells inside biofilms can actually oscillate collectively (the whole biofilm). Could the scattering of light on the oscillating object make them invisible.?
2.4.3. different sizes of biofilms? 
It is well known to most people that biofilms or bacteria are tiny and minute creatures. Therefore, most of the focus for their study is by using microscopes of different types. Bjarnsholt, Thomas & Alhede, Maria & Alhede, Morten & Eickhardt, Steffen & Moser, Claus & Kühl, Michael & Jensen, Peter Østrup & Høiby, Niels Have observed that the biofilms found inside living organisms ranged 5-200 µm in diameter. On the other hand, the catheter biofilms ranged up to 1200 µm in diameter for different reasons. These biofilm sizes are much smaller when compared to those ones found inside animal testing models which can produce several cm 2 of bacterial biofilms. .  however, since the idea of being tiny was popular, the focus was to study them under microscope. This could be diverting the attention away from them as bacterial cells were found measuring up 2 cm in size. Larkin, J.M. and Henk, M.C. have discovered elongated threads of biofilms of aggregated thousands of cells of large sulfur bacteria which may reach few centimeters found in Mexico bay.  . Also, recently a gigantic bacteria cell has been found which may measure around a couple of centimeters in length. This might in turn lead to the fact that this type of bacterial biofilms might be larger than few centimeters. This type of cell has been named Thiomargarita magnifica.  ..

Here the actual visibility of the biofilms and their bacterial cells have been discussed. Bacterial cells are mostly transparent under visible light conditions unless stained. Also, the assembled materials by biofilms that constitute the extra cellular matrix and the matrix itself are mostly transparent under visible light condition. Moreover, some biofilms and their products can be seen directly by the human eyes when altering the conditions to simulate an ultra violet dominant condition where this range can be seen by some animals. In addition, the vibration of the cells would make it more difficult to be seen by the eye if they were multicellular. The latter would be true if the oscillatory speed of the cells more than that would be detected by the human eye. The other issue would be that some biofilms would be macroscopic therefore, studying them might be better by other means like ct, xray, MRI and or other novel means. 
some conclusions if the biofilms were multicellular from this perspective :

Also, if this is true what would be the visible light effect on their cells? In addition, could their virulence increase under non- visible light condition like in the night or dark place?

If so, can they be utilized to detect some diseases? Does their sound like barking and braying of donkey a sign of one of them approaching when there is no visible object distressing these animals?

4. since they cannot be detected in the range of the visible spectrum, if a person’s eyes have special ability to detect some of the non-visible spectrum would it manifest itself as visual hallucinations?
2.5. viruses, fungi and other microorganisms and their relationship to bacterial biofilms:  
Usually, bacteria and viruses are considered to be separate entities. 
How Viruses Act as Active Parts of Biofilms
Viruses influence biofilms in three primary "active" ways:
1.	Structural Support: Certain viruses, such as the filamentous phage Pf4 found in *Pseudomonas aeruginosa*, do not immediately destroy their host. Rather, they arrange themselves into liquid-crystalline structures that reinforce the biofilm’s extracellular matrix (the protective slime layer), thereby enhancing its resilience against drying out and antibiotic treatment.
2.	Genetic Plasticity: Prophages—viruses incorporated into the bacterial genome—often harbour beneficial "fitness genes." When activated, they induce lysis in a subset of the bacterial population, releasing extracellular DNA (eDNA). This released eDNA then serves as a structural framework, promoting further biofilm development.
3. Community Defence: Emerging research (including work by Kuss et al. and Hansen et al.) indicates that viruses embedded within a biofilm's outer layers can help shield the underlying bacterial community. These trapped phages achieve this by destroying other invading bacteria or competing phage strains that attempt to penetrate the biofilm.
2.5.1. Can viruses be an active part of the biofilms?
a. Viruses can form biofilms themselves:
one of the breakthroughs in disease fighting strategies was the discovery of one probable mechanism by which viruses can propagate and have effects on different biotic tissues was the discovery of virus biofilms like structures by Thoulouze MI, Alcover A. (2011). They have proposed that human T-cell leukemia virus type 1 (HTLV-1) tether itself in an extracellular matrix like a cocoon which allows for better conservation and more efficacious transport of viruses between cells.  These bodies are found in the t-cells of the latter infected virus patients. These structures enhance viral infection, virulency and spread.  . By enveloping themselves in an extracellular matrix like material would they be able to be parts of biofilms? 
b. Moreover, viruses Not only can form biofilm, but they can be active members of the biofilms.
the discovery of viruses inside biofilms was registered in 1997. The results of the investigation of the industrial water flow sharing system yielded that poliovirus 1 associated with biofilms were more persistent and have greater numbers.  . Another research has found that some norovirus and enterovirus are encompassed within the structure of biofilms from samples retrieved from drinking water and wastewater. In this study the biofilm linked viruses were able to cast infection for up to 30 days.  . moreover, pipeline hooked enterovirus contained within biofilms were more invulnerable to disinfectant material and the mechanical pressured asserted by the flow. 
Moreover, many studies gradually concluded that viruses, fungi and microorganisms may be contained with the body of biofilms through the process of colonization. (Kuss et al., 2008) have found that biofilms may have caliciviruses, Rotavirus spp., Astrovirus spp., and hepatitis A virus along with fungi and gram-negative bacteria as their constituents  . another study yielded the researched that herpes simplex virus 1 (HSV-1) and the coxsackievirus type B5 (CVB5) inside the biofilm of candida albicans.  . These authors have discovered that even extensive washes of the highly virus susceptible biofilm produced high virus titers showing that viruses are better protected within fungal biofilms by the process of deep propagation. They have proposed some theories of how the virions were included into the biofilms eg. by sorption sites from the extracellular matrix, by the natural colloidal characteristic of viruses, or by biomolecules present on biological fluids. The Virus spread, viability and infectivity do not change even after inclusion illustrating being a part of biofilm does not limit viral capability but can improve it . Virus storage could be temporary or permanent . Vasickova and collaborators, illustrated acquired features of viruses within biofilms, such as protection against desiccation and antimicrobial agents  .
On the other hand, another phenomenon has been over reported which is that viruses disseminate in groups called “collective infectious units”  . Likewise, Tozzi and coworkers mentioned cumulative distribution of multiple SARS-CoV-2 particles based on microscopic image analysis of viruses. They have proposed collective clustering dynamics, a phenomenon that happens when the virions evacuate the infected cells then individual viruses tend to gather and form in the last stage interconnected network-like branches. Increased beta coronaviruses and Corona Virus attraction to polysaccharides and carbohydrates found on the matrix of biofilms could most likely have a role in secondary colonization.  
Von Borowski RG, Trentin DS. Have projected two designs of how a corona virus could be displayed inside biofilms whether be as viral biofilm or as a secondary colonizer of a microbial biofilm. furthermore, other studies refer that viral particles are either embedded (i) in a viral biofilm are embedded within extracellular matrix produced form the host cell micro- surroundings; or (ii) inside a microbial biofilm composed by bacterial and/or fungal cells.  
Another feature that might give the clue to the fact that viruses could be parts of some biofilms is that viral particles do not reproduce inside biofilms and continue to possess the infectious ability. This means that viruses do not cause any harm to the biofilm.  On the other hand, due to this close physical relation, the preservation of viral particles inside biofilms accelerates the coinfection process.
Importantly, these conditions may boost viral infectivity and encourage coinfections  . A Jiangsu Province retrospective investigation discovered that 242 (94.2% of 257) SARS-CoV-2-positive patients were infected with one or more infections. Bacterial coinfections predominated, with Streptococcus pneumoniae, Klebsiella pneumoniae, and Haemophilus influenzae being the most prevalent species.
c. CAN MICROBIAL BIOFILMS FAVOR VIRAL RESERVOIRS IN BATS?
Dawley and Gibson have stressed on the distinct links between viruses and other microorganisms that permit the passage of viruses to inside biofilms which leads to storage and accumulation of viruses. This in turn makes these latter creatures more resistant and persistent . Inflammatory processes are hindered by biofilm because the biofilm matrix inhibits recognition of viruses by immune system. Viruses contained within biofilms triggers the immune system less and thus active disease in bats is absent this can be explained as the viruses within biofilms cannot replicate. Therefore, there is steady rate of viruses. Another strong clue for this idea is that there are circumstances where genomic merging among several viruses occurs including coviruses which yields more stability and adaptation.  .
There is a phenomenon that can be described as genetic flexibility which is the mutual benefit between species perseverance and durability . This happens because of genomic recombination, addition and or erasing  .
One of the distinct features that biofilms can grant to the virus are those recombination incidents in the existence of extracellular matrix, enzymes and genes’ debris.  These different viruses would infect the same cells at the same time, therefore increasing the coinfection rate by viruses and their gene mutation. Therefore, Shirogane Y, et al. illustrated that Pseudogymnoascus destructans secondary infection raised covirus reproduction rate in bats.  . This gives new eras to investigate regarding dynamics and the communication between different components of biofilms.
On the other hand, viruses released from biofilms have shown a circumstance called “complex multivirion structures,” which is assembled virus dissemination.  . 
One the other hand, some researchers have found that viruses are very dynamic parts of the biofilm community. Alexandra T.Payne, Abigail J.Davidson, JinjunKan, MarcPeipoch, RavenBier, KurtWilliamson. (2020) have concluded: their findings indicate that viruses play a significant role in the biofilm communities of freshwater environments during all stages of biofilm growth that we observed in their study. preliminary assessments suggest that biofilm growth and maturation drive changes in viral communities. It follows, therefore, that viruses are active and participating members of biofilm communities, as well as having the capability to affect the abundance, composition, and functionality of communities, which may have implications for ecosystems as a whole.  
Therefore, one can assume that viruses can be active members of the biofilm communities. Besides, there are few researches that show that viruses and bacteria can be partners most of the times of infection.eg. 
Neu U, Mainou BA. (2020) have discussed that microbes and their products communicate with viruses and virions unequivocally. Till now the studies mostly focused on the bacterial part of microbiota that can influence the viral infection and not vice versa. Bacteria can increase virion stability therefore viral fitness thus eukaryotic cells infection and coinfection rate rises. The viral part of the biofilm once is attached to eukaryotic cells they promote bacterial adherence to foreign cells

- Direct and indirect impact of biofilms on viral infection has been recorded and the opposite also.
d.  viruses can used as weapon by some biofilm
Matthew C. Bond, Lucia Vidakovic, et al have discovered viruses that are encapsulated within the matrix of the biofilm can act as executioner for outside bacteria because these viruses remain infectious on the outer area. Nonetheless, if the attacking E. coli bacterium gain enough time to adhere and grow so that they become intertwined inside the invaded matrix then these arriving bacterial cells acquire immunity against further phage exposure. Such immunity can be acquired by taking advantage of the existing genomic matter indirectly by laying deep within curli -producing cells. Furthermore, Barr et al., found that phages have the possibility to kill bacteria residing in the mucosa of the host by capturing them in the mucosa. Based on their finding and the later finding by barr Et. al, Matthew C. Bond, Lucia Vidakovic, Praveen K. Singh, Knut Drescher, Carey D. Nadell have concluded that phage entanglement would benefit the mucosal environment and the biofilm community continuation. .

2.5.2. Fungi can also form biofilms:
There are plethora of researches which can confirm that there are some sort or interaction or relationship between bacterial biofilms and other microorganisms on the other hand. These interactions seem to be nonrandom as they are recurring with the same groups or genera many times. Also, most of these reactions yield constant products or results.   Whether it be antagonism, synergism or mutualism the supremacy and the superiority as in taking control of the situation seems to be for the bacterial biofilms for most of the interactions. On the other hand, can these nonbacterial cells aggregate or form a biofilm? If so, does this mean that these might be much under control of the bacterial biofilms the same as for humans and their animals.
A. Fungi can aggregate into biofilms:  
Fanning S, Mitchell AP (2012) have concluded that Aspergillus, Candida, Trichosporon, Coccidioides, and Pneumocystis are among the fungi that produce biofilms that are important to medicine.”  This research indicate that fungi can produce biofilms which means that the cells of the fungi are surrounded by ECM. 
Biofilm formation by saprophytic and pathogenic fungi is of great concern. Despite recent advances in the study of biofilms formed by species in the genus Candida, Cryptococcus, and Aspergillus, reports of biofilm formation by other species are increasing. 
B. Bacterial fungal interactions: 
direct bacterial and fungal relations and interaction have been recorded. Deveau A, Bonito G, Uehling J, Paoletti M, Becker M, Bindschedler S, Hacquard S, Hervé V, Labbé J, Lastovetsky OA, Mieszkin S, Millet LJ, Vajna B, Junier P, Bonfante P, Krom BP, Olsson S, van Elsas JD, Wick LY (2018) have explained: that usually bacterial and fungal cells are found to exist simultaneously in vast types of places.  Plants and animals benefit greatly from their interactions as they play a vital role in many ecosystem functions. Several fungal and bacterial families engage in complex interactions that are responsible for the emergence of critical behavioral shifts between the microorganisms, ranging from mutualism to antagonism. 
Furthermore, it is likely that fungal microbiomes contribute to the biology of their fungal hosts, similarly to plant and animal microbiomes, which contribute to the 'extended phenotype' of their hosts. In fact, antibiotic treatments inhibited mycelial growth, secondary metabolite production, or reproduction (Vahdatzadeh, Deveau, and Splivallo 2015; Schulz-Bohm et al.2016; Mondo et al.2017; Uehling et al.2017).  . Also, including reproduction (Partida-Martinez et al.2007; Mondo et al.2017), proliferation (Shaffer et al.2017; Uehling et al.2017; Desirò et al.2018), energy dynamics (Salvioli et al.2016; Vannini et al.2016), primary metabolism (Lastovetsky et al.2016; Salvioli et al.2016; Vannini et al.2016; Li et al.2017; Uehling et al.2017) and secondary metabolism (Rohm et al.2010; Hoffman et al.2013).  
Nonetheless, these interactions seem to repeat themselves nonrandomly. An example of a well-studied endobacterial-fungal system is the association between (Gigasporaceae, Glomeromycotina) and Candidatus Glomeribacter gigasporarum (CaGg, Burkholderiaceae). Bianciotto et al.2004 have indicated that the above-mentioned bacteria can be transmitted vertically among fungi and can have detrimental effect on the phase before the symbiosis in these fungi. In the stage preceding the symbiotic one, the previous creatures can boost ATP production, fungal capacity to utilize biological source of energy and shows responsive oxygen clearing operations (Salvioli et al.2016). Another study unveiled features of the endobacterial biology in which a toxin-antitoxin process was active (Salvioli Di Fossalunga et al.2017), also the total operon for vitamin B12 production (Ghignone et al.2012).  This may inherently refer to an imaginable metabolic benefit by this endobacterium for the fungus and plant partner. This phenomenon has also been noticed in lichen Lobaria pulmonaria, the lichen-associated bacteria aids photosynthesis by supplying B12 (Grube et al.2015). 
Another example is the relationship between Paraburkholderia rhizoxinica (formerly Burkholderia rhizoxinica) and Rhizopus microsporus is mutualistic, wherein the bacteria offer toxic substances to its host, enhancing fungal pathogenicity on rice. Remarkably, the transmission of vertical endobacteria has an impact on fungal reproduction, eliminating asexual sporulation and reducing mating significantly. (Mondo et al.2017). A new study used endobacterial regulation of fungal reproduction to find breeding genes in the Mucoromycota, a group of fungi famously resistant to genetic techniques, as well as recreate major reproductive pathways throughout the fungal kingdom. (Mondo et al.2017). Furthermore, research into the pre-symbiotic relationship between R. microsporus and Paraburkholderia indicated that the fungus experiences certain lipid changes in metabolism to cope with endobacteria, which, when disrupted, shifts the association from mutualistic to hostile. (Lastovetsky et al.2016). 
Another example: is the endosymbiont Mycoavidus cysteinexigens, to the Mortierella elongata (Mortierellomycotina) (Uehling et al.2017). usually, this bacterium has close lineal connection to Candidatus Glomeribacter gigasporarum CaGg but has another effect on the fungal host prosperity. The candida helps to increase the surge of the host, while the bacteria does exactly the opposite (Uehling et al.2017) Which indicates that this subsequent endobacteria use host fungal metabolic products.
On the other hand, there seem to exist endofungal bacteria reported in the Ascomycota and Basidiomycota in a more temporary manner, nevertheless, they can still affect how the fungi grow and stay healthy (Hoffman and Arnold 2010; Spraker et al.2016). These temporary bacterial-fungal relationships may be environmentally essential to regional habitat-associated modification, where the fungal hosts may act as reservoirs in the environment or shelters for the bacteria. (Spraker et al.2016). Only a few of the methods by which endofungal bacteria invade their hosts have been recognized. To enter the hyphae of the R. microsporus host, Paraburkholderia rhizoxinica aggressively produces chitinolytic enzymes via the type II secretion system. (Moebius et al.2014). Ralstonia solanacearum, on the other hand, demands the development of the lipopeptide ralsomycin in order to infiltrate the chlamydospores of its fungal hosts. (Spraker et al.2016). Some bacteria, like mitochondria, can travel through dolipore septa after they enter the mycelium. (Bertaux et al.2005). Others can also be passed down through generations via fungal spores (Spraker et al.2016), with the bacterial type III secretion system might have a role. (Lackner, Moebius and Hertweck 2011). 
Moreover, more and more studies prove that bacteria and fungi live together. Bacterial DNA is frequently identified in fungal genomics projects, raising the possibility that endobacteria are more abundant in fungus than previously envisioned. Such 'contaminating' DNA could be via external bacteria or endobacteria (both transitory and permanent). With advancements in genome-sequencing technology, whole bacterial genomes may now be assembled from a fungal-bacterial DNA mixture. (Uehling et al.2017). Previous to rejecting these 'contaminating' bacterial readings from their projects, researchers are asked to keep minds open to the potential of endobacterial companions in their fungi. Although the existence of bacterial reads in fungal sequencing projects indicates the presence of possible endobacterial symbionts, the involvement and taxonomic identity of endofungal bacteria should be displayed by other evidence, such as transmission electron microscopy and fluorescence in situ hybridization. (FISH).
C. Interaction between bacteria and fungi in intricate microbial communities, Meta organisms, and multicellulars
In a recent study, the co-occurrence of bacterial and fungal OTUs across 266 soil samples revealed a significant link between bacteria from the genus Burkholderia and a wide range of soil fungi. (Stopnisek et al.2016). This broad association suggests that particular soil bacteria have developed strategies to use fungal-secreted chemicals and avoid fungal defense mechanisms, which are supported by laboratory co-cultivation testing. (Stopnisek et al.2016). 
An association between a yeast called dioszegia and Arabidopsis thaliana's phyllosphere microbiome was discovered by Agler and colleagues where the microbiome and its bacterial interactant utilize the fungus as a hub. Litter (Purahong et al.2016), soils (de Menezes et al.2014; Ma et al.2016; Stopnisek et al.2016), floral nectar (Álvarez-Pérez and Herrera 2013), plants (Bell et al.2014; Agler et al.2016) and human microbiomes (Mukherjee et al.2014; Trosvik and de Muinck 2015) have been previously studied and BF connections were found also the forces that regulate the establishment of communities.
All of these studies showed that fungi and bacteria have unanticipated connections with one another, with positive relationships outnumbering negative ones. Such beneficial relationships are probably indicative of ecological resemblances and potential beneficial interactions between the microorganisms. Such beneficial relationships are probably indicative of ecological resemblances and potential beneficial interactions between the microorganisms. However, they can also be the result of a habitat being extensively colonized by a single selection or dispersal agent, such as certain bacteria in flowers that bees disseminate (lvarez-Pérez and Herrera 2013). According to Mukherjee et al. (2014) and Ma et al. (2016), connections can exist involving a few thousand to more than 50.000 bacteria (as in the oral microbiome). The potential association between BFI and ecosystem functioning can be determined by combining network-inferred predictions with functional evaluations (Ma et al. 2016; Purahong et al. 2016). It was shown that some bacteria may contribute to the N nourishment of fungi while fungi make C available to bacteria by the simultaneous appearance of the lignocellulose decomposer fungi Clitocybe and Mycena spp. with potential N2-fixing bacterial taxa during leaf decomposition (Purahong et al. 2016).
Host-microbiota homeostasis, a state of ecological equilibrium within the microbiome and between the microbiome and its host, is regarded to be essential for the health of both animal and plant hosts (Krom and Oskam 2014; Hacquard et al. 2017). In a variety of research fields, there is rising interest in understanding how microbiomes change from advantageous symbiosis to detrimental dysbiosis and how BFI contribute to this transformation. BFI could be a factor that controls human disease, for instance, if the ecological equilibrium between the two groups changes. This is shown by recurring relationships between fungi and bacteria in infections of burn wounds, denture stomatitis, cystic fibrosis and immunocompromised patients' lungs, (recurrent) bowel disease, and infections related to the use of invasive medical equipment (Dhamgaye, Qu, and Peleg 2016; Förster et al. 2016). Therefore, BFI in such links may have an impact on both interactome partners' pathogenicity. According to numerous studies (e.g., Santhanam et al.2015; Ritpitakphong et al.2016; Expósito et al.2017), the role of plant microbiota in pathogen inhibition is significant.
Many plant infections, comparable to human disorders, are usually associated with dysbiosis and a decrease of microbiome diversity (Santhanam et al. 2015; Koskella, Hall, and Metcalf 2017). Although the mechanisms underlying bacterial-fungal equilibrium within plant tissues are not fully understood, they are probably a combination of host-dependent and host-independent processes, including metabolic and dietary mutual dependence among microbes, antimicrobial secretion, and the production of protective barriers (Wei et al.2015; Mousa et al.2016).
These complex relationships are now being taken into account when creating novel methods for improving harvest development and wellbeing (Panke-Buisse et al.2015; Poudel et al.2016) or when using microbiome-based strategies to manage an imbalance in animals and plants (Fraune et al.2015; Santhanam et al.2015; Adam et al.2016). With a focus on relationships among microorganisms in the context of pathogenesis (Lopes, Azevedo, and Pereira 2014), it is becoming more widely acknowledged that potential synergisms within BF infectious populations must now be taken into account in order to study and treat diseases (Lamichhane and Venturi 2015). Additionally, beneficial BFI effects on human wellness may make it possible to use fungus and/or bacteria as probiotics. Microbiome-based analyses are also used to improve food-related operations, such as the production of cheese and wine (Pinto et al. 2014; Dugat-Bony et al. 2015; Liu et al. 2017), and they may have applications in a variety of diverse industries, such as biological remediation and power generation. 
D. As for parasites few experiments have been undertaken to analyze and detect the relationship between parasites and bacteria.
Historically, the scientific consensus was that parasites lacked the ability to generate biofilms. However, a groundbreaking 2024 study published in Nature has upended this view, offering the first definitive proof of a parasite building a biofilm-like community.
•	The Organism: The research focuses on Lotmaria passim, a trypanosomatid parasite that shares an evolutionary lineage with several human pathogens.
•	The Discovery: Scientists discovered that, given the right environment, these organisms will anchor to surfaces, mutate into specialized (haptomonad-like) cells, and secrete a protective extracellular matrix. These resulting structures check all the boxes for a true biofilm.
Ultimately, this highly recent breakthrough implies that biofilm formation is a far more common survival tactic across the entire microbial spectrum than we originally realized. 
 Romoli, O., Gendrin, M (2018) have elaborated: for the last two decades, the increasing evidence have accumulated regarding the role of mosquito’s microbiome in defining how the plasmodium infection is transmitted.  In contrast the relationship between the mosquito, its microbiota and the plasmodium are intricate that is in need for further investigation. Limitation of plasmodium infection, change of various physiological aspects of the mosquito including its lifetime are among the effects that the microbiota exerts. These interactions can be suggested to be fixed interplay system were all the structure have their separate duty by the virtue of similar effects across all mosquitos and plasmodium species.  
# Ippolito MM, Denny JE, Langelier C, Sears CL, Schmidt NW. have stated:  the dynamics of mammalian’s malaria, the strength and competence of the malaria transference are directly affected by the microbiota. This study reviewed Ten pertinent studies which yielded that there is an elaborate connection between certain gut ecosystems on one hand and the severity of the disease and the resistance to the plasmodium on the other hand. Mice infected with plasmodium species have an abrupt and long-lasting adjustment in their intestinal microbiota. Moreover, human skin microbiota may alter the allure of the mosquito hence increase infection transmission. 
Debanjan Mukherjee, Ângelo Ferreira Chora, Maria M. Mota have found out:  Updated studies have elaborated the fact that intestinal microbiome would be altered after plasmodium infection.  On the other hand, human and mice researches have demonstrated a relationship between certain intestinal microbiota and increased liability of contracting malaria. The suggested mechanisms responsible for this phenomenon are still unknown. This study expects that there is a new relationship (paradigm) among the three related organisms: the host, microbiota and pathogen to fully understand the this and ither infections. 
These research and studies indicate that some types of bacteria are related with malaria infection. That means they might have some sort of control over plasmodium. Also, the microbiota controls the severity of infection with plasmodium.

These interactions seem to be nonrandom since they frequently occur with the same groups or genera. The majority of these reactions also produce predictable outcomes or products. Such reactions can be categorized to be either antagonism, synergism or mutualism with the bacteria having the superior role or benefits most of the times.
