2.6.1. Biofilms and the killing among the components of different biofilms: 
A. phages killing fungi for the favor of other fungi: 
Some animal’s biomass indices contain certain types of fungi which are closely related to specific phages that are capable of fight fungal infections in humans. Meyer S, Thiel V, Joergensen RG, Sundrum A; has discussed that 20% of the fecal mass of many dairy cows is fungal mass.  Ergosterol is specific for Ascomycota, and for Basidiomycota, but also for Mucor plumbeus. This research indicates that faeces of animals (contain a respectable number of fungal cells. Another research has found the phages inside cow manure are effective in eradicating bacterial and fungal biofilms. Kumari N, Patoli BB, Patoli AA, Jabeen S. has stated: This work is likely the first to isolate phages from cow manure in the area and show how effective they are at killing MRSA and E. coli, with the eventual goal of using them as a promising biocontrol tool against infections that are resistant to antibiotics.  Barzegari A, Kheyrolahzadeh K, Hosseiniyan Khatibi SM, Sharifi S, Memar MY, Zununi Vahed S. have mentioned: In general, it would be prudent to gain knowledge about the mechanisms by which probiotics and their derivatives can be employed to treat biofilm infections.  Probiotics may contain a variety of microorganisms. The most common are bacteria that belong to groups called Lactobacillus and Bifidobacterium. Other bacteria may also be used as probiotics, and so may yeasts such as Saccharomyces boulardii. 
b. bacteria biofilms compete against other bacterial biofilms: 
 Sandeep Miryala, Veena G Nair, S Chandramohan, C S Srinandan, have found: The biofilm of the uropathogenic E. coli (UPEC) UTI89 strain was compromised by the cell-free supernatants from growth culture of the screened isolates. According to a physicochemical investigation, the substance might be a polysaccharide or a glycopeptide. Shigella, Escherichia, Enterobacter, and Salmonella were among the Enterobacteriaceae species included in the isolates that hindered matrix synthesis. Competitive tests among the isolates and the UPEC strain produced mutual inhibition, especially during the production of biofilms, which significantly reduced productivity and fitness. Additionally, we demonstrate that Salmonella strains competed eliminated the UPEC strain from the biofilm by preventing the development of its matrix, emphasizing the significance of interference competition.  Bratanis E, Andersson T, Lood R, Bukowska-Faniband E. have found: The obligate predatory bacteria Bdellovibrio and like organisms (BALOs) prey on a variety of Gram-negative bacteria, including drug-resistant human diseases. They have long been identified as a possible medicinal and biocontrol agent due to their peculiar lifestyle. 
 Further researches have been undertaken which show the same outcome for probiotics. Atterbury RJ, Tyson J.  have written: The use of living predatory bacteria, such as Bdellovibrio bacteriovorus (small Gram-negative bacteria that naturally infiltrate and destroy Gram-negative infections of humans, animals, and plants), is one strategy that could be used to combat antimicrobial resistance (AMR). The study and characterization of these bacterial predators in biologically relevant settings is necessary to move toward the application of Bdellovibrio as a "living antibiotic." The above researches and papers have shed the light on the fact that certain bacterial cells can kill other bacterial cells. Furthermore, these bacterial cells can interfere with the growth of biofilms.  
Moreover, biofilms can alter the course of other biofilm maturation and growth by hindering planktonic cell activities. Melo LDR, Pinto G, Oliveira F, Vilas-Boas D, Almeida C, Sillankorva S, Cerca N, Azeredo J. have discussed: However, it was shown that a number of staphylococcal bacteriophages were ineffective against biofilms. This work assessed the effectiveness of the previously described S. epidermidis-specific Sepunavirus phiIBB-SEP1 (SEP1) against S. epidermidis biofilms. SEP1 has a broad spectrum and high activity against planktonic cells. The in vitro biofilm killing experiments showed decreased phage activity. This phage was tested against several populations of planktonic and biofilm-derived bacteria in order to determine the underlying mechanisms affecting SEP1's ineffectiveness against biofilms. SEP1's ability to lyse planktonic cells in various physiological situations raises the intriguing possibility that the 3D structure of biofilms and the matrix's protective properties are to blame for the drug's lack of success in controlling them. SEP1 was tested in disturbed biofilms to determine the effect of the biofilm architecture on phage predation, and the results showed a 2 orders-of-magnitude decrease in the number of viable cells after 6 hours of infection. By including matrix to phage particles, the relationship between SEP1 and the biofilm matrix was further evaluated. The matrix neither altered phage adsorption nor rendered phages inactive, according to the results. Additionally, confocal laser scanning microscopy results showed that phage-infected cells were less common in biofilm locations with more abundant matrix. Our findings offer solid proof that the biofilm matrix might operate as a barrier, preventing the bacteria from becoming infected by phages.  
C. Bacterial biofilms kill phages or viruses
 Yang D, Wang Z, Ma J, Fu Q, Wu L, Wang H, Wang S, Yan Y, Sun J. have written:” The CRISPR/Cas system protects bacteria against bacteriophage and plasmids through a sophisticated mechanism where cas operon plays a crucial role consisting of cse1 and cas3. However, comprehensive studies on the regulation of cas3 operon of the Type I-E CRISPR/Cas system are scarce.”  
Rostøl JT, Marraffini L. have finalized: Although this is rarely how a bacterium uses its arsenal against phage in nature, the phage resistance mechanisms outlined thus far have primarily been examined in isolation in the laboratory. Each of the complementing layers of defense that bacteria use—none of which are mutually exclusive—must be overcome by a phage for successful infection to occur. The environment, however, often comprises a heterogeneous mix of phages, in contrast to experimental settings. Anti-phage mechanisms are beginning to work together synergistically. The above papers indicate the fact that viruses can kill bacteria and vice versa.
In addition, fungi, virus and bacteria can make fearsome animosity. Both sides have the ability to kill their counterparts. Linnakoski R, Reshamwala D, Veteli P, Cortina-Escribano M, Vanhanen H, Marjomäki V. have concluded that fungi and detailed virus point of kill will be studied further in the future. The mechanism by which fungi are opposing viral infections is narrowly explained. To fully comprehend the mechanisms of action, laboratory experiments that directly target the different stages of virus infection are required.  Hongxiang Zhang, Jiatao Xie, Yanping Fu, Jiasen Cheng, Zheng Qu, Zhenzhen Zhao, Shufen Cheng, Tao Chen, Bo Li, Qianqian Wang, Xinqiang Liu, Binnian Tian, David B. Collinge, Daohong Jiang. A have researched: Viruses that infect fungi are known as mycoviruses, and hypovirulence-associated mycoviruses possess the capacity to manage a variety of fungal illnesses. We discovered that Sclerotinia sclerotiorum, the host of a previously reported small DNA mycovirus (SsHADV-1), can change from a typical necrotrophic pathogen to a helpful endophytic fungus by the influence of its virus. Key pathogenicity factor genes are expressed less frequently when S. sclerotiorum is infected by SsHADV-1.   therefore, virus and fungal pathogenicity to each other have been shown. 
Peleg, A., Hogan, D. & Mylonakis, E. have finalized:
•	Acinetobacter baumannii, Burkholderia cepacia, Salmonella enterica subsp. enterica serovar Typhimurium, Pseudomonas aeruginosa, and other Gram-negative bacteria can all kill Candida albicans and prevent filament development. Small molecules like quorum-sensing molecules and other recognized secretory virulence factors, such as phospholipase C and phenazines for P. aeruginosa, are mostly responsible for mediating this.
•	Polysaccharide receptors on C. albicans' surface appear to be the mechanism by which oral streptococci have evolved to cling to it in the human mouth. Many oral disorders have an etiology including this co-aggregation.
•	In order to defend itself from these bacterial enemies, C. albicans secretes the quorum-sensing chemical farnesol. This chemical can impact how bacteria produce virulence factors, maintain viability, and respond to antibiotics. 
The latter papers have strongly indicated the fact that fungi can be used to fight viruses and vice versa. Also, that bacteria and fungi can be found to destroy each other.

2.7. mechanical components and mechanical properties of different biofilms? 
2.7.1. Do biofilms have mechanical components used for food?
Most of the other multicellular organisms have specialized evaginations extended from their body used as mechanical tools for handling things and for eating called hands. These are continuation of their body cells with special functions. However, if biofilms were to function as multicellular, they should possess continuations of their body that can extend for mechanical work that benefit the whole community. 
L. Karygianni, Z. Ren, H. Koo, T. Thurnheer, have found that: The ability of bacterial masses to adapt to difficult conditions is largely driven by EPS, which provides necessary scaffolding, protection, and stability. Through EPS-mediated adhesion and cohesion, bacteria can attach to substrates and form organized clusters called microcolonies. These interactions also give rise to collective biofilm traits, such as spreading and motility, which are vital for environmental adaptation.  
They have also concluded that: Mechanisms of Adhesion and Growth Biofilms rely on specific cellular mechanisms to drive collective behaviours. For instance, in Vibrio cholerae, the matrix protein RbmA exhibits adhesion-like properties that facilitate cell-to-cell coupling. Similarly, Streptococcus mutans utilizes quorum sensing during the early stages of colonization to detect external adhesion forces; this process drives the vertical development of the biofilm above the substratum.
Mechanical Stability and EPS Viscoelasticity The structural integrity of a biofilm is largely due to the viscoelastic properties of the Extracellular Polymeric Substances (EPS). These properties render the biofilm nearly inseparable from the underlying surface, even when subjected to high-pressure fluids or significant mechanical stress. Consequently, the mature EPS matrix provides a robust shield against physical disruption, antibiotics, and host immune responses.
Interkingdom Interactions and Antimicrobial Resistance Increased EPS production offers enhanced protection against environmental stressors, particularly in mixed-species environments. Recent studies indicate that interactions between bacteria and fungi (such as Candida albicans) lead to EPS-mediated cross-resistance. For example, exopolysaccharides secreted by C. albicans can block antibacterials from reaching Staphylococcus aureus. Conversely, glucans from S. mutans can encapsulate and sequester the antifungal drug fluconazole, thereby protecting C. albicans. Similar multidrug resistance (to flucloxacillin, ciprofloxacin, or fluconazole) has been observed in triple-species biofilms containing P. aeruginosa, S. aureus, and C. albicans.
Chemical Gradients and Nutrient Metabolism the EPS matrix functions as a regulatory barrier, manipulating the diffusion of molecules to create gradients in pH, oxygen, signaling molecules, and nutrients within the biofilm's 3D structure. For instance, the matrix creates acidic microenvironments that directly influence pH-responsive ATP-B expression in S. mutans. Furthermore, the matrix acts as a reservoir for biomolecules. In V. cholerae, osmotic stress variations cause colony expansion, improving contact with nutrient-rich substrates. Finally, the matrix can function as an "external digestive system" by immobilizing exo-enzymes. This allows biofilms, such as those formed by C. albicans and Streptococcus oralis, to metabolize basic materials and support growth without compromising mechanical stability. 
Araújo GRS, Viana NB, Gómez F, Pontes B, Frases S.: The exchange of polysaccharides and ECM components is driven by the biofilm's immediate environment. Crucially, water availability regulates mechanical stiffness. Wilking et al. (2011) demonstrated that limited water supply forces the biofilm to adopt a more elastic state, a property that differs markedly from biofilms found in fluid-rich settings. 
Stewart PS has founded:  The diverse material attributes of the biofilm matrix ensure its persistence and allow for effortless dissemination. Furthermore, biofilms employ mechanical defenses to thwart leukocyte endocytosis. While biofilm fragments may trigger an immune response and attract phagocytes, the physical density of the infection site can impede this process. For example, in patients with Cystic Fibrosis (CF), the high viscosity of the airway mucus severely hinders leukocyte chemotaxis. This observation is supported by Matsui et al. (2005), who demonstrated that leukocyte mobility is significantly restricted in concentrated mucus (6.5% dry weight, simulating CF conditions) compared to the freer movement observed in lower density mucus (1.5% to 2.5% dry weight).
Impact of Matrix Density on Phagocyte Motility
The physical density of a biofilm’s environment directly dictates the efficacy of the immune response. Parkhurst and Saltzman (1992) demonstrated that neutrophils migrate freely through normal cervical mucus but face significant resistance in denser media. They quantified an inverse relationship between motility and collagen density: movement notably declines when density exceeds 0.2 mg/ml, with complete immobilization predicted at concentrations of 1 mg/ml (0.1%). 
As the gelatinous matrix's viscoelastic characteristics are determined by the extracellular polymers these means that the White blood cells’ ability to efficiently infiltrate and guard the mucus layers are directly related to EC polymer concentration. The material that usually constitutes the matrix may be obtained from mucus, dead tissue, DNA discharged from dead neutrophils or WBC from extracellular traps, also from the EPS manufactured by the biofilms themselves. 
Here in these researches, we can find that different extracellular matrix components can function as mechanical apparatus for nutrient, water retention and for scaffolding. Also, different biofilm densities yield different elastic properties and would be different in vast range of milieus. This is so compelling because why some cells would pass the nutrients to much more distant cells if there is no mutual relationship between those cells. This needs to investigate the biofilms in different environmental conditions. 
2.7.2. mechanical properties of biofilms that renders them to be thought as multicellular:
Once regarded merely as random clusters of independent bacteria, biofilms are now recognized for displaying key traits associated with multicellular life. This article consolidates recent advances in understanding the mechanical, architectural, and bioelectrical characteristics of biofilms. It illustrates how the extracellular matrix serves a role similar to primitive muscle tissue, how bacterial cells adopt specialized roles akin to differentiated cells in complex organisms, and how communication via ion channels orchestrates collective metabolic activity. We also explore how these communal structures maintain evolutionary stability by managing and neutralizing social cheaters, reinforcing the concept of the biofilm as a unified, organism-like entity.
1. Mechanical Dynamics: The Extracellular Matrix as an Active System: 
Far from being just an inert support system, the extracellular polymeric substance (EPS) is a dynamic material that can generate physical force. Emerging research in biophysics shows that biofilms have the capacity to alternately swell and contract in reaction to their surroundings.
a.	Force from Osmosis:
In Vibrio cholerae biofilms, the EPS absorbs and concentrates solutes, creating osmotic pressure. This internal force acts like a hydraulic mechanism, propelling the biofilm's edges forward to explore and occupy new, nutrient-rich territories (Yan et al., 2017).
b.	Contractile Forces:
 Other biofilms demonstrate the ability to contract their EPS fibers through shifts in pH or ion concentration. This contraction can actively push fluids and chemical signals through the biofilm's network of channels. This process serves as a rudimentary form of a circulatory system, enabling the transport of materials when simple diffusion is too slow. 
2. Structural Organization: Specialized Roles in Space:
A core principle of advanced multicellularity is the specialization of cells for different tasks. Biofilms achieve this through distinct physical zones that create specific conditions, leading to different behavioral states among genetically identical cells.
a.	Outer Layer as Sentinels: 
Bacteria on the periphery, in direct contact with the fluid environment, are highly active. They serve as the community's primary interface with the outside world, sensing changes in oxygen, flow, and nutrients, while also driving expansion and producing the matrix.
a.	Inner Core as Reservoir: 
In the deeper, oxygen-depleted layers of the biofilm, cells enter a non-growing, highly persistent state (Lewis, 2010). These dormant cells function like a protected germline. If the metabolically active outer layers (the "soma") are killed by antibiotics or other threats, this inner population can survive and subsequently regenerate the entire community. 
3.	Bioelectrical Communication: Signalling Through Ion Flow:
For a biofilm to synchronize complex behaviours across its entire structure, it needs a rapid and far-reaching communication system. The identification of electrical signalling has provided a revolutionary insight into how this coordination happens.
Work from the Süel group has revealed that Bacillus subtilis biofilms use potassium ion channels to send electrical signals over long distances (Prindle et al., 2015).
a.	Cycling Metabolism:
As the highly active cells at the periphery rapidly deplete nutrients, they inadvertently create a shortage for cells in the interior. In response, the nutrient-starved interior cells generate an electrical signal.
b.	Synchronized Behaviour:
 This electrical wave travels outward and temporarily arrests the growth of the peripheral cells. This pause allows nutrients to penetrate deeper into the biofilm, feeding the interior. This process provides a compelling answer to the puzzle of why individual cells would curtail their own growth for the benefit of the larger community. 
4. Maintaining Integrity: Defense Against Internal Exploitation
For the multicellular analogy to hold, biofilms must have a way to resolve the inherent conflict between individual and group interests. The shared resources of the biofilm, particularly the EPS matrix, are susceptible to exploitation.
a.	The Cheater Problem:
 In any cooperative system, "cheater" mutants can arise. These cells benefit from the protection and resources provided by the EPS without contributing to its production and maintenance (Nadell et al., 2009).
b.	Mechanisms of Enforcement:
 Without intervention, these cheaters would proliferate and destabilize the community. However, biofilms possess organized control systems. By deploying targeted toxins or using contact-dependent inhibition, the cooperative majority can actively suppress or eliminate cheater strains. This social enforcement ensures the long-term integrity of the biofilm, cementing its identity as a cohesive and regulated entity rather than a mere collection of competing individuals.

2.8.1. Marine microbiome: 
N RAMAIAH. ; Sarma, V. V. S. S. ; Gauns, Mangesh ; Dileep Kumar, M. ; Madhupratap, M (2000) say: “Bacterial abundance and production, numbers, sizes and concentrations of transparent exopolymer particles (TEP) and total organic carbon (TOC) were measured during the 1996 summer monsoon to understand the relationship between TEP, the most labile particulate organic carbon, and bacteria. While high regional variability in the vertical distribution of TOC was discernible, TEP concentrations were high in surface waters at 18--20_N along 64_E with concentrations well over 25 mg alginic acid equivalents l-1 due to upwelling induced productivity”. 
It can be seen that bacteria are abundant in water near the surface and some types of them can live deep inside water.
Woodall LC et. al 2018: “The deep sea is the largest biome on earth, and microbes dominate in biomass and abundance. Anthropogenic litter is now almost ubiquitous in this biome, and its deposition creates new habitats and environments, including for microbial assemblages. With the ever-increasing accumulation of this debris, it is timely to identify and describe the bacterial and archaeal communities that are able to form biofilms on macrodebris in the deep sea”. 
Therefore, one can decide that there are microbiomes inside water. In conclusion, this is one of the biomechanical privileges that biofilms own. On one side there is salinity (high concentration of salt) which makes it mechanically demanding to maintain the stability of the cells inside salty water.
The choice of appropriate biofilm control strategies in membrane systems for seawater desalination pretreatment relies on understanding the properties of the biofilm formed on the membrane. This study reveals how the biofilm composition, including both organic and inorganic, influenced the biofilm behavior under mechanical loading. The investigation was conducted on two Gravity-Driven Membrane reactors employing Microfiltration (MF) and Ultrafiltration (UF) membrane for the pretreatment of raw seawater. After a stabilization period of 20 days (Phase I), a biofilm behavior test was introduced (Phase II) to evaluate (i) biofilm deformation during the absence of permeation (i.e., relaxation) and (ii) biofilm resistance to detachment forces (i.e., air scouring). The in-situ monitoring investigation using Optical Coherence Tomography (OCT) revealed that the biofilms developed on MF and UF membrane presented a rigid structure in absence of filtration forces, limiting the application of relaxation and biofilm expansion necessary for cleaning. Moreover, under shear stress conditions, a higher reduction in biofilm thickness was observed for MF (−60%, from 84 to 34 µm) compared to UF (−30%, from 64 to 45 µm), leading to an increase of permeate flux (+60%, from 9.1 to 14.9 L/m2/h and +20 % from 7.8 to 9.5 L/m2/h, respectively). The rheometric analysis indicated that the biofilm developed on MF membrane had weaker mechanical strength, displaying lower storage modulus (−50 %) and lower loss modulus (−55 %) compared to UF. These differences in mechanical properties were linked to the lower concentration of polyvalent ions and the distribution of organic foulants (i.e., BB, LMW-N) found in the biofilm on the MF membrane. Moreover, in the presence of air scouring led to a slight difference in microbial community between UF and MF. 
A 2022 study in FEMS Microbiology Ecology on biofilm-forming microalgae adds another layer to this. It found that diatoms like Amphora and Navicula could survive long periods of darkness (a terrestrial stressor) and resume normal growth and photosynthesis once favorable conditions (light) returned. This demonstrates the resilience and regenerative capacity of the community's "inner organs" protected by the biofilm structure. 
(gemini Yes,
Also, there is substantial evidence that marine biofilms can survive on land. In fact, the mechanical challenges such as gravity, wind, and intense desiccation (drying out) often trigger the very adaptations that make them more resilient.
Research into intertidal zones and biofouling (marine growth on ship hulls) provides clear evidence of this "terrestrial" survival.
1. The "Elastic Shield": Combating Desiccation
When a marine biofilm is exposed to air, it undergoes a mechanical transition. As water evaporates, the Extracellular Polymeric Substances (EPS) do not simply disappear; they concentrate. The Result: The biofilm transforms from a "soft gel" into a leathery, glass-like skin. This skin traps a microscopic reservoir of water around the bacteria, preventing total dehydration. 
2. Mechanical Strengthening via Air Exposure
Marine biofilms on ship hulls or tidal rocks are frequently subjected to "aerial exposure." Studies have shown that these biofilms actually become stronger after being out of the water:
Capillary Forces: As the biofilm dries, capillary action pulls the bacterial cells closer together, increasing the density of cross-links in the EPS.  
Surface Adhesion: Research on Pseudoalteromonas (a common marine bacterium) shows that drying increases its adhesive strength. When the tide returns, the biofilm is often harder to wash away than it was before it dried out.  
2.8.2. Virus biofilms and fungal biofilms in the deep seas: 
Ding, W., Wang, R., Liang, Z. et al have discussed: Recent metagenomics surveys have provided insights into the marine virosphere. However, these surveys have focused solely on viruses in seawater, neglecting those associated with biofilms. By analyzing 1.75 terabases of biofilm metagenomic data, 3974 viral sequences were identified from eight locations around the world. Over 90% of these viral sequences were not found in previously reported datasets. Comparisons between biofilm and seawater metagenomes identified viruses that are endemic to the biofilm niche. Analysis of viral sequences integrated within biofilm-derived microbial genomes revealed potential functional genes for trimeric autotransporter adhesin and polysaccharide metabolism, which may contribute to biofilm formation by the bacterial hosts. 
Zeghal Emna, et al have reviewed: Although many studies have focused on microbial colonization and the potential role of microorganisms in breaking down marine plastic debris, little is known about fungi-plastic interactions. Marine fungi are a generally understudied group of microorganisms but the ability of terrestrial and lacustrine fungal taxa to metabolize recalcitrant compounds, pollutants, and some plastic types (e.g., lignin, solvents, pesticides, polyaromatic hydrocarbons, polyurethane, and polyethylene) indicates that marine fungi could be important degraders of complex organic matter in the marine realm, too. Indeed, recent studies demonstrated that some fungal strains from the ocean, such as Zalerion maritimum have the ability to degrade polyethylene. 
There are many studies affirming the fact that there are marine fungi and viromes 
A.	The Extraterritorial Virome: Mechanisms of Mechanical Resilience and Inter-Niche Dispersal for Marine Biofilm-Associated Phages
The persistence of marine bacteriophages embedded within biofilm matrices across terrestrial and atmospheric boundaries provides compelling evidence for the mechanical robustness of the extracellular polymeric substance (EPS). Rather than succumbing passively to environmental stressors, these viruses appear to exploit the EPS as an adaptive vehicle, enabling inter-niche genetic dispersal through the mitigation of terrestrial mechanical challenges.
1. Aerosolization via the Sea Surface Microlayer
Marine viruses access terrestrial ecosystems through aerosolization, mediated by the sea surface microlayer (SML) and oceanic foam. Bubble-bursting dynamics at the air-sea interface selectively enrich viruses and organic matter in sea spray aerosols, achieving concentrations orders of magnitude above those in bulk seawater (Michaud et al., 2018). The organic coating acquired from the SML serves as a transient, hygroscopic buffer, shielding virions from rapid desiccation and UV-induced damage during atmospheric transit and subsequent deposition onto terrestrial surfaces (Mayol et al., 2015).
2. Matrix Sequestration and EPS Phase Behavior:
Following deposition and desiccation, marine biofilm EPS undergoes a phase transition that mechanically immobilizes and protects embedded phages. Solvent evaporation elevates polymer concentration (CC) within the matrix, leading to an exponential increase in the elastic shear modulus (GG) consistent with semi-dilute polymer scaling laws (G∼C2.25G∼C2.25; Gloag et al., 2020). This transition from a viscoelastic gel to a glassy, leathery state (Flemming & Wingender, 2010) entraps virions within a rigid scaffold, conferring resistance to shear forces, oxidative stress, and ultraviolet radiation until rehydration restores permissiveness.
3. Subsurface Transport in Terrestrial Matrices: 
Upon incorporation into soil systems, marine-derived phages exhibit distinct transport behaviour attributable to their structural integrity. Due to size-exclusion effects, phages are restricted to larger, hydraulically conductive pores, enabling migration velocities up to four times greater than those of conservative solute tracers in vadose zone environments (Bales et al., 1991). Furthermore, phage decay rates are substantially reduced when virions remain embedded within organic aggregates or soil biofilms, underscoring the protective role of terrestrial matrices in preserving infectivity (Kimura et al., 2008).
4. Rehydration and Host-Density Dynamics:
Rewetting of desiccated, terrestrially deposited biofilms triggers viral reactivation, initiating lytic cycles consistent with the "Kill-the-Winner" ecological model (Thingstad, 2000). Preferentially targeting the most rapidly proliferating bacterial taxa, these resurrected phages impose top-down regulation that curtails competitive exclusion and sustains taxonomic and functional diversity within the resuscitated microbial consortium.
5. Viral Nucleation and Aggregate Formation:
Beyond passive encapsulation, viruses function as active agents in the spatial structuring of microbial communities. The reactive proteinaceous capsids of virions serve as nucleation centers for the assembly of dissolved organic matter and transparent exopolymer particles, facilitating the formation of macroscopic aggregates (Peduzzi & Weinbauer, 1993). This viral-induced aggregation modifies particle buoyancy, driving vertical flux and enabling the translocation of both organic carbon and associated microbial consortia to deeper oceanic strata—an active transport mechanism that expands niche accessibility.

6. Implications for the Multicellularity Paradigm
When the bacterial biofilm is conceptualized as a cooperative multicellular entity, its resident phage population emerges not merely as parasitic agents but as functional extensions of the collective genetic architecture (Costerton et al., 1995; Shapiro, 1998). By surviving aerosolization, desiccation, and terrestrial deposition, these encapsulated virions function as resilient genetic vectors—mobile regulatory elements capable of transmitting population-control signals and facilitating horizontal gene transfer across vast spatial scales. Their capacity to endure mechanical extremes and subsequently reintegrate with susceptible hosts reinforces the conceptualization of biofilms as distributed, highly integrated biological systems whose influence extends well beyond their immediate physical boundaries.

B: Fungal-Only Biofilms: The "Hydraulic Scaffold"
Fungi are the "civil engineers" of the microbial world. Unlike bacteria, they have a rigid cell wall made of chitin, which provides immense structural strength.
1. Mechanical Challenges
The rice blast fungus *Magnaporthe oryzae* forms a unique, bulbous structure known as an appressorium. This specialized cell builds up intense internal pressure—reaching up to 8.0 MPa—by storing high levels of glycerol within a melanin-coated, rigid wall that prevents leakage. This pressure is then transformed into mechanical force, enabling a sturdy penetration peg to puncture hard surfaces. This makes them vulnerable to being snapped off by wind. 
2. Adaptation to Sudden Milieu Change
The shift from round yeast cells to elongated hyphal cells is essential for building a mature biofilm structure. These hyphae form a supportive framework, similar to reinforcement bars, and produce specialized adhesins like Hwp1 and Als3 on their cell walls. These proteins help bind fungal cells together and securely anchor the extracellular matrix. Without them, mutant strains develop fragile biofilms that detach easily and lack proper structural stability. 
3. The "Fungal Highway" and Viral Hitchhiking
 In habitats lacking continuous water, non-motile bacteriophages cannot disperse through liquid diffusion. Instead, they attach to motile bacteria such as Pseudomonas putida, which travel along the thin liquid films surrounding fungal hyphae like those of Pythium ultimum. This form of co-transport enables phages to bridge dry gaps and reach new, physically separated microbial populations. 
4. The "Mixed-Kingdom" Adaptability
The Bacteriophage Adherence to Mucus (BAM) model describes how bacteriophages use Ig-like protein domains on their capsids to weakly bind to glycans in mucosal surfaces—which resemble the sugars found in biofilm matrices. By accumulating within this mucus layer, phages form a defensive barrier that preferentially targets and destroys incoming bacterial pathogens. In this way, they function as a form of acquired immunity for the underlying tissue. 
This suggests that fungal biofilms supply the structural framework, while viruses contribute informational resilience. When faced with a sudden environmental shift—such as the transition from ocean to land—the fungus detects the physical change through mechanotransduction and responds by reinforcing its structure. Meanwhile, the virus enters a glass-like phase state, effectively pausing until conditions improve. Together, they form a complementary partnership: one builds and adapts, the other preserves and endures.
2.8.3. Microbiomes of the atmosphere: 
Christner, B., Morris, C., Foreman, C., Cai, R., & Sands, D. (2008) investigated the sources and distribution of ice nucleators (IN) in snowfall from mid- and high-latitude regions. They found that the most active IN, particularly those functioning above -7°C, were predominantly biological in origin—comprising 69 to 100% of particles larger than 0.2 micrometers, with a significant proportion identified as bacteria. The authors conclude that the biosphere serves as a key source of highly active IN and propose that these biological particles may play a role in influencing precipitation patterns, potentially even facilitating their own dispersal through atmospheric transport. 
DeLeon-Rodriguez, N., et al. (2013) investigated the composition and distribution of microorganisms in the middle-to-upper troposphere (8–15 km altitude) to address unresolved questions regarding their role in atmospheric processes. Using samples collected aboard a NASA DC-8 aircraft during the 2010 Genesis and Rapid Intensification Processes campaign over the Caribbean Sea—including before, during, and after Hurricanes Earl and Karl—the authors found that viable bacterial cells constituted roughly 20% of particles in the 0.25–1 μm size range and were significantly more abundant than fungal cells. This suggests that bacteria are an underappreciated component of micrometer-sized atmospheric aerosols. While hurricane samples introduced distinct bacterial communities, 17 taxa were consistently present across all samples, including organisms capable of utilizing C1–C4 carbon compounds. The authors conclude that the upper troposphere harbors a dynamic microbiome, with potential implications for cloud formation, the hydrological cycle, and climate. 
Sauer et al. (2004) demonstrated that a sudden increase in carbon substrate or pH can rapidly alter *Pseudomonas aeruginosa* biofilm structure. Raising glutamate concentration from 2 to 20 mM caused complete loss of three-dimensional architecture within 60 minutes, correlating with a subpopulation of bacteria that upregulated flagella and downregulated pili, enabling dispersal into flowing liquid. Similarly, *Staphylococcus epidermidis* biofilms exposed to high osmotic pressure (86 to 776 mM NaCl) showed a tenfold reduction in local cell density (Stewart et al., 2013). Increased shear stress on *P. aeruginosa* biofilms reduced cluster formation and downregulated genes involved in polysaccharide synthesis (Crabbé et al., 2008; Dingemans et al., 2016). In gravity sewer biofilms, elevated shear stress altered porosity and reduced chemical oxygen demand (Xu et al., 2017). Aerobic conditions stimulated biofilm development and architectural complexity in Campylobacter jejuni (Reuter et al., 2010; Turonova et al., 2015). Periodic desiccation in environments such as soils and hypersaline ponds can cause EPS to enter a glass-like state, preserving viability until rehydration (Habimana et al., 2014; Decho, 2016; Lennon and Lehmkuhl, 2016). *Bacillus subtilis* biofilms grown at air interfaces form a hydrophobic layer via BslA protein, conferring resistance to ethanol and biocides (Arnaouteli et al., 2016; Epstein et al., 2011). Ultrasonic waves can enhance antimicrobial efficacy by deforming biofilms and improving EPS penetration (Qian et al., 1996; Peterson et al., 2015). Substratum properties also influence biofilm structure and physiology: Acinetobacter baumannii formed dense biofilms on polycarbonate but sparse growth on glass, with live/dead ratios varying by surface material (Greene et al., 2016). Coating silicone rubber with brush polymers altered biofilm architecture and increased antibiotic susceptibility (Muszanska et al., 2012). Similarly, treating surfaces with group II capsular polysaccharides reduced adhesion and biofilm formation by nosocomial pathogens (Valle et al., 2006). Together, these findings illustrate that manipulating local environmental parameters can effectively control biofilm structure and function. 

One can understand from the studies above that bacteria and fungi are available in great no. even in high altitudes in the atmosphere. They are even there during tough storms. This must mean that they (their cells) have a survival mechanism through which they can live, remain, and survive these vigorous conditions. The ability of these sky-biofilms to manipulate their mechanical density to trigger rain (P. syringae) or survive 1,000 km flights (Deinococcus) supports the idea of specialized mechanical tools. The atmosphere is the ultimate test of the biofilm’s multicellularity only by acting as a single, coordinated unit can they survive the mechanical void between the ocean and the land.
2.8.4. Soil microbiomes: 
Chan-Wen Xu et al. (2011) examined the soil properties and nitrogen-fixing bacterial (NFB) communities in the rhizosphere of the invasive plant Ageratina adenophora in Southwest China. Across a broad geographic range, heavily invaded (HI) soils exhibited higher nutrient levels compared to lightly invaded (LI) and non-invaded (NI) soils. The number and species diversity of NFB were significantly greater in HI soils than in NI soils. A total of 416 NFB isolates were obtained and classified into 47 operational taxonomic units (OTUs), representing four bacterial phyla: Proteobacteria (63.9%), Actinobacteria (32.2%), Firmicutes (1.9%), and Bacteroidetes (1.9%). At the genus level, the isolates were distributed across 20 genera, with the majority belonging to dominant groups. 
Santos-Medellin, C., Zinke, L. A., Ter Horst, A. M., Gelardi, D. L., Parikh, S. J., and Emerson, J. B. investigated viral and microbial community dynamics in soil throughout the tomato growing season. Their findings demonstrate the value of soil viromics in revealing that while viral and microbial communities exhibit similar temporal patterns, their spatial distributions appear partially decoupled. The authors suggest that this divergence may result from differences in dispersal capabilities, decay rates, or varying sensitivities to soil heterogeneity between viruses and their microbial hosts.   
This study shows that the soil could be resided by these creatures along with viruses. 
2.8.4.a. The microbiome in soil and its role in soil cementation and water retention
Shariq, A. F., Beyenal, H., and Akin, I. D. investigated the potential of using biofilms for soil improvement, presenting bio-mediated ground enhancement as an environmentally friendly alternative to traditional admixtures for strengthening shallow surfaces. Because biofilms contain extracellular polymeric substances (EPS)—porous materials with charged surfaces that retain water through adsorption and capillary condensation—they offer a novel approach to enhancing soil strength. However, the authors note that most existing studies have only tested soil improvement under dry conditions, leaving the role of water retention by biofilms unclear. To address this, they evaluated the strength of biofilm-amended sand across a wide range of saturations (S from 0.02 to 0.74) using the suction stress characteristic curve (SSCC), which captures adsorptive, capillary, and cementation forces between particles. Their findings show that homogenized anaerobic granule biofilms mixed with sand improve soil strength through cementation across all saturation levels, and additionally through adsorptive forces at low saturations (S < ~0.3). The authors conclude that homogenized biofilms mixed with sand can effectively enhance the strength of sand across varying moisture conditions. 
2.8.4.b. features of soil biofilms: 
 In the soil, the "multicellularity" of biofilms moves from the fluid world into a high-pressure, porous architecture. If marine biofilms use their matrix as a buoyancy tool and atmospheric biofilms use it as a protective capsule, soil biofilms use their matrix as a civil engineering kit.
Soil is a mechanical labyrinth of mineral particles (sand, silt, clay) and air-filled pores. To survive here, biofilms function as "biological glue" that physically restructures the earth.
Mechanical Challenges: The "Crush" and the "Clog"
Soil biofilms face a unique mechanical reality called Matric Potential—the physical force with which soil particles hold onto water.
1. In soil, the availability of water for microbes is governed by matric potential—essentially, the suction force that binds water to particle surfaces. As the soil dries, instruments like tensiometers show that this force becomes more extreme, creating a physical barrier that restricts bacterial movement and the diffusion of nutrients. Bacteria have evolved to survive this by secreting Extracellular Polymeric Substances (EPS). Studies using advanced imaging techniques reveal that these gelatinous materials are highly hydrophilic, allowing them to absorb and retain significant amounts of water, thereby shielding the bacterial community from desiccation. 
2. Pore Clogging (Bioclogging): The mechanics of biofilms growing in pore "throats" and altering hydraulic systems are heavily documented in hydrodynamics and porous media research. Microfluidic studies using transparent artificial soil chips reveal that as biofilms grow, they significantly reduce the intrinsic permeability of the porous medium, a process known as bioclogging (Thullner, 2010). When localized fluid velocity increases due to this clogging, the hydrodynamic shear stress (\tau) spikes. When $\tau$ exceeds the biofilm's yield stress, the biomass abruptly ruptures or detaches. This mechanically opens new channels while closing old ones, creating an intermittent, "pulsing" flow dynamic that regulates nutrient distribution across the spatial microbiome (Coyte et al., 2016). 
3. Compression Resistance: The physical nature of a biofilm is defined by its viscoelasticity—it behaves partly like a thick liquid and partly like a flexible solid. Rheometry allows researchers to quantify this by measuring its elastic storage modulus and its viscous loss modulus. The structural integrity of the biofilm comes from its EPS matrix, which is typically polyanionic (negatively charged). This chemical property becomes a survival advantage when multivalent cations like Ca2+ and Mg2+ are introduced. These cations form ionic bridges between the negative polymer chains. As demonstrated by rheological experiments, this cross-linking effect can cause a log-fold increase in the biofilm's G', effectively stiffening it into a resilient scaffold that protects the community from mechanical compression as soil grains shift and the environment dries out. 
4. Soil Aggregation: The production of EPS by biofilms serves as a prime example of niche construction, where an organism actively modifies its own habitat. By functioning as a biological binding agent, the sticky EPS matrix weaves through the soil, physically entangling fine clay, silt, and sand particles into larger, stable aggregates. This isn't just a visual observation; wet-sieving experiments provide quantitative proof, showing that soils rich in EPS-producing bacteria maintain their structure and resist disintegration when agitated in water, unlike sterile soils. The result of this biological cementing is the formation of macro-pores within the soil. These larger spaces are critical for the ecosystem, as they facilitate the diffusion of oxygen and the infiltration of water, creating a more hospitable environment for the aerobic communities that thrive there. f 
2.8.4.c. Transition Survival: Can a Soil Biofilm survive Marine or Atmosphere?
1. Soil to Atmosphere: Wind Erosion and the "Glassy" State
Calorimetry studies show that as biofilms lose water, the EPS crosses its glass transition temperature (Tg). It hardens from a flexible, water-logged gel into an amorphous, rigid solid. In this glassy state, bacterial cells are physically locked in place, which prevents their delicate proteins and membranes from collapsing and shuts down all metabolic activity, effectively preserving them in suspended animation. This explains why microbes collected from Saharan dust plumes—tracked by satellites across the Atlantic—remain viable. The wind shatters dried biological crusts into tiny fragments, lifting them into the atmosphere where they can travel thousands of miles, with their living cargo perfectly preserved inside their glassy EPS sarcophagus. (Griffin, 2007 ; Potts, 1994 ).
________________________________________
2. Atmosphere to Soil: Hydration Pulses and "Mechanical Reset"
Rheology experiments on dried biofilms show that water acts as a plasticizer. As soon as moisture is reintroduced—whether from rain or simple humidity—it lowers the glass transition temperature (Tg) of the EPS below that of the surrounding environment. This triggers an immediate phase shift: the rigid, glassy matrix relaxes back into a soft, viscoelastic fluid. The polymer network swells, and this is marked by a steep drop in its storage modulus (G'), indicating it has lost its solid-like stiffness. This mechanical softening happens in minutes and restores the physical space needed for molecules to move and diffuse. Metabolic tracing and gene expression studies confirm that this allows cells to rapidly restart their engines, repair any accumulated DNA damage, and begin growing in their new terrestrial home. (Lennon & Jones, 2011 ; Or et al., 2007 ).
3. Marine to Soil: Osmotic Buffering and Hygroscopic EPS Biochemical and elemental analysis of salt-tolerant biofilms reveals that their EPS is specially formulated. It is rich in negatively charged components (polyanionic) like uronic acids and sulfate groups. These chemical groups act like magnets for positively charged salt ions (Na+, Mg2+, Ca2+) in the environment. The EPS actively binds and traps these ions, sequestering them away from the cell membrane. By concentrating the salt in the matrix, the EPS creates a buffered zone. This prevents the catastrophic water loss (plasmolysis) that would normally occur if the cell's interior was suddenly exposed to a high-salt environment, allowing the bacteria to maintain healthy internal pressure and survive where they otherwise could not. (Decho, 1990  ; Halverson et al., 2006  ).
2.8.4.d. Life Examples: The "Engineers" of the Underground
1. Bacillus subtilis: Amyloid Scaffolding and Mechanical Buckling:
The structural integrity of a Bacillus subtilis pellicle depends on two primary extracellular components: exopolysaccharide (EPS) and the amyloid protein TasA. Molecular analyses indicate that TasA forms robust amyloid fibers which function similarly to steel rebar, interlocking with the EPS matrix (analogous to concrete) to provide the biofilm with exceptional tensile strength (Romero et al., 2010 ).
Furthermore, the characteristic wrinkling pattern observed during biofilm maturation is not a random result of desiccation. Instead, microfluidic and biomechanical modeling has revealed that this wrinkling represents a programmed mechanical instability (Asally et al., 2012; Wilking et al., 2013). As the biofilm expands and localized cell death occurs at the base, compressive forces cause the stiff upper layer to buckle. This physical buckling generates a complex network of internal micro-channels that facilitate the transport of liquid and nutrients throughout the dense colony, effectively functioning as a primitive circulatory system (Asally et al., 2012 ; Wilking et al., 2013 ).

2. Myxococcus xanthus: The "wolf pack" hunting strategy of myxobacteria relies on two motility systems, most notably Type IV pili, which enable coordinated swarming across surfaces to engulf prey. When food runs out, the population undergoes a radical transformation guided by the C-signal pathway. As time-lapse microscopy reveals, hundreds of thousands of cells then cooperate to construct a three-dimensional fruiting body. This structure is built by cells physically stacking upon one another and secreting a protective matrix, which lifts the dormant spores up and away from the substrate, positioning them for optimal dispersal into the air."water channels" at the microscopic level. (Velicer & Vos, 2009  ; Zusman et al., 2007 ).
3.Rhizobium Biofilms: 
The infection of leguminous plant roots by symbiotic bacteria begins with the establishment of a localized micro-biofilm on the surface of a root hair. This attachment is facilitated by specific bacterial adhesion proteins, known as rhicadhesins, and unique EPS structures (Downie, 2010; Gage, 2004).
Once attached, the bacteria synthesize and release lipochitooligosaccharide signaling molecules, termed Nod factors. Transcriptomic analysis and fluorescence microscopy have demonstrated that these signals induce a significant morphological response in the plant host, causing the root hair to curl and effectively trap the bacterial microcolony (Downie, 2010 ; Gage, 2004).
Following this entrapment, the biofilm functions as a localized invasive agent. The bacteria enzymatically degrade the plant cell wall, facilitating the formation of an "infection thread"—a tube-like structure created by the invagination of the plant cell membrane. This tunnel, constructed through both mechanical and enzymatic means, allows the proliferating bacteria to actively invade the underlying root cortex tissues, ultimately triggering the development of the nitrogen-fixing root nodule (Downie, 2010; Gage, 2004 ).
Therefore, soil biofilm can be thought of as the skeletal system of the Earth's crust. Functioning as a cohesive, multicellular entity, it performs three critical roles: it exerts mechanical force to bind soil particles together, it regulates the flow of water by blocking or opening pore spaces, and it acts as a highway for genetic exchange, facilitating horizontal gene transfer within its dense matrix.
2.8.5. Biofilms inside trashes: 
Miho Aoshima et al. (2001) operated a commercial composter using a fixed garbage composition, both with and without the addition of soybean oil. The composter was run without seed microorganisms or bulking materials. Microbial communities were analyzed using denaturing gradient gel electrophoresis (DGGE) for oil-amended conditions and 16S/18S rRNA gene sequencing for isolates under non-amended conditions. Regardless of oil addition, all identified bacteria were Gram-positive, with lactobacilli emerging as the key microorganisms. 
Accordingly, bacteria are found in the food leftovers and inside trashes even with no addition of bacterial seeds.
Within the heterogeneous and toxic environment of waste systems, biofilms represent a highly adaptive metabolic collective. Their extracellular matrix serves a dual purpose: it is a chemical refinery for metabolizing diverse substrates (from organics to plastics) and a protective fortress against heavy metals and toxins, demanding a level of mechanical toughness and chemical versatility unparalleled in most other habitats.

1. The Mechanical Challenges: The "Toxic Squeeze"
Heterogeneity: In waste management facilities, the makeup of microbial populations fluctuates heavily based on the specific qualities of the wastewater, the chosen biofilm carriers, and the operational parameters of the reactor (Bakke et al., 2025) . To accurately represent these environments, contemporary biofilm models have shifted away from viewing biofilms as uniform coatings. Instead, they acknowledge them as highly heterogeneous structures comprising complex arrangements of active cells, pores, and extracellular polymeric substances (EPS) (Chen et al., 2022) . Ultimately, this intricate internal architecture is a direct reflection of the highly varied substrates and microenvironments characteristic of waste treatment processes.
The "Chemical Shield": Loosely bound extracellular polymeric substances (LB-EPS) function as the principal reservoir for the accumulation of metals. Research examining electroactive biofilms subjected to EDTA-Cu demonstrated that roughly 53% of the copper complexes were held within the LB-EPS, highlighting its role as an essential protective and metal-binding barrier (Tian et al., 2025) . Furthermore, foundational studies employing fractionation methods showed that more than 60% of organic contaminants, such as BTX, are trapped within the broader EPS structure (Späth et al., 1998) . This capacity for sequestration is largely driven by the negatively charged properties of the EPS, which contains high concentrations of proteins with distinct structural conformations that promote active binding .
Structural Flexibility: Biofilms exhibit viscoelastic characteristics similar to solid hydrogels, granting them the capacity to structurally deform when subjected to mechanical stress. Investigations into anammox granular sludge reveal that extracted extracellular polymeric substances (EPS) assemble into viscoelastic biomaterials characterized by expansive three-dimensional networks.  These networks display solid-like properties analogous to gels possessing infinite relaxation times. Such mechanical dynamics yield significant practical consequences; for example, introducing biofilms into soils designated for waste containment increases the material's ductility and alters its consolidation rates. This highlights the capacity of biofilms to fundamentally modify the mechanical behavior of substrates under physical stress (Daniels et al., 2005) .
2. The External Digestive System
In trash, the biofilm matrix functions as an extracellular gut.
Enzyme Sequestration: The extracellular polymeric substance (EPS) matrix inherently functions as a biological scaffold for the anchoring of degradative enzymes. Investigations utilizing engineered Bacillus subtilis biofilms reveal that specific enzymes, including laccases (CueO, CotA) and esterases (EreB), can be intentionally fused to TasA, the primary matrix protein. This genetic modification facilitates the targeted export and secure integration of these enzymes directly into the biofilm architecture. By physically securing the enzymes, this mechanism minimizes washout and establishes a highly concentrated, stable microenvironment for continuous catalytic activity (Team TU Darmstadt. 2020) . The indispensable nature of the EPS matrix in preserving this enzymatic function is reinforced by studies demonstrating that exposing biofilms to a degradative enzyme cocktail (lipase, cellulase, and proteinase K) significantly reduces EPS density and degrades the structural network. Consequently, this disruption arrests overall biofilm maturation and prevents microcolony aggregation (Li et al., 2022) . Ultimately, these findings validate that a structurally intact EPS is an absolute prerequisite for sustaining the localized environments required for immobilized enzyme operations.
Inter-Species Cooperation: Interactions across multiple biological kingdoms play a foundational role in the development and operational capacity of biofilms within environments such as wastewater treatment facilities. Long-term observational studies tracking granular biofilm maturation over 343 days indicate that prokaryotic and eukaryotic populations undergo parallel successional changes, highlighting that cross-kingdom relationships heavily dictate the overall architecture of the microbial community over time (De Celis et al., 2024) . Empirical evidence strongly supports the structural role of fungi within these complex consortia. For example, the bacterium Bacillus velezensis actively colonizes the hyphae of the fungus Trichoderma guizhouense through the targeted upregulation of specific biofilm-associated genes, namely epsD and tasA. The structural dependency of this relationship is confirmed by gene deletion experiments; removing these genes completely inhibits bacterial attachment, demonstrating that fungal hyphae act as an indispensable physical scaffold for bacterial biofilm assembly (Wang & Shen, 2024) . Comparable structural dynamics are observed in mixed-species biofilms comprising the fungus Candida albicans and the bacterium Staphylococcus aureus. In this system, the fungal hyphal network supplies the necessary physical framework, which simultaneously facilitates cooperative nutritional strategies between the two species, particularly concerning copper metabolism and handling (Kalinina et al., 2025) .
3. The Survival of "Trash-to-Atmosphere" Transitions
Biofilms in trash are a major source of atmospheric biofilms (bio-aerosols).
Methane Bubbling: In oxygen-depleted environments, the release of methane often occurs through ebullition (bubbling), a transport process that exerts direct physical forces on microbial biofilms (XHAB, LLC, 2024) . Studies examining membrane-aerated biofilm reactors (MABRs) indicate that the turbulence caused by standard bubble aeration induces significant shear stress; this mechanical force is capable of stripping dissolved gases and structurally disrupting the biofilm matrix (Lu et al., 2025) . Additionally, as methane bubbles ascend from benthic sludge and rupture at the liquid-gas interface, they can capture and transport intact biofilm fragments and entrained microbes, effectively serving as a primary mechanism for bioaerosol generation (Lu et al., 2025; XHAB, LLC, 2024).
The "Aerosolized Capsule": Empirical evidence demonstrates that bioaerosols originating from landfills function as primary carriers for antibiotic resistance genes (ARGs). An extensive investigation of a municipal waste site revealed that airborne microbial concentrations within active disposal zones peaked at 1234 CFU/m³, dominated primarily by pathogenic bacteria and allergenic fungal species (Wang et al., 2022). Importantly, this research identified substantial concentrations of ARGs within adjacent residential zones. Specific resistance genes, including tetC, acrB, and bacA, were found in greater abundance beyond the landfill perimeter than within it, providing clear evidence for the atmospheric dissemination of these aerosolized genetic elements (Wang et al., 2022) .
The presence of these ARGs showed a strong correlation with the overall bacterial community structure, verifying their transport via bioaerosol particulates. Notably, these particles predominantly ranged in size from 2.1 to 4.7 μm, falling within the respirable fraction capable of deeply infiltrating the human respiratory tract (Wang et al., 2022). Corroborating these findings, subsequent studies characterize landfills as critical reservoirs for multidrug resistance genes (MDRGs). These genes demonstrate considerable mobility, frequently associating with plasmids, which further facilitates their widespread transmission through aerosolization (Zheng et al., 2025) .

2.8.6. Bacteria in the outer space and their remains on meteors: 
 #http://science.nasa.gov/science-news/science-at-nasa/1998/ast01sep98_1/:     on 14/2/2012
in this article the following is written: “Space historians will recall that the journey to the stars has more than one life form on its passenger list: the names of a dozen Apollo astronauts who walked on the moon and one inadvertent stowaway, a common bacteria, Streptococcus mitis, the only known survivor of unprotected space travel. As Marshall astronomers and biologists met recently to discuss biological limits to life on Earth, the question of how an Earth bacteria could survive in a vacuum without nutrients, water and radiation protection was less speculative than might first be imagined”.
Gillet Ph et al (2000) authors say: "This study investigates the physical signatures of terrestrial weathering and associated biological interactions on the Tatahouine meteorite. Utilizing scanning and transmission electron microscopy on the weathered meteorite samples and the adjacent soil, researchers identified two distinct, bacteria-like structures localized on the mineral surfaces. These structures are classified as either rod-shaped forms (RSF), measuring 70–80 nm in width and 100–600 nm in length, or ovoid forms (OVF), with diameters ranging from 70 to 300 nm.
Morphologically, these entities resemble unicellular organisms enclosed by a cell wall. Bulk chemical analysis revealed a primarily organic elemental profile consisting of sodium, potassium, carbon, oxygen, and nitrogen, alongside trace amounts of phosphorus and sulfur. Furthermore, electron diffraction and rigorous chemical assessments definitively ruled out an inorganic origin, confirming that the RSFs and OVFs are not composed of magnetite, other iron oxides, iron hydroxides, silicates, or carbonates.
While the dimensions of these structures fall below the typical size thresholds for known bacteria, they bear a striking resemblance to the bacteriomorphs previously documented in the ALH84001 Martian meteorite. Collectively, these structural and chemical observations provide compelling evidence that these microscopic forms are authentic bacteria or their fossilized remnants. This conclusion is highly corroborated by secondary microbiological analyses; culturing the soil immediately surrounding the meteorite yielded pleomorphic bacteria morphologically identical to the observed RSF and OVF structures. Ultimately, these findings establish that bacteria-like structures with diameters under 100 nm can genuinely represent biological organisms or their biological remains.

Published in Wickramasinghe, N. C., Steele, E. J., Gorczynski, R. M., Temple, R., Tokoro, G., Klyce, B., et al. (2020). Comments on the origin and spread of the 2019 coronavirus. Virology: Current Research 4, 1. doi: 10.37421/Virol Curr Res.2020.4.435.

# Wickramasinghe NC, Steele EJ, Temple R, Tokoro G, Smith WA, Klyce B, Wickramasinghe DT, Arachchi DM. have discussed: Wickramasinghe and colleagues have discussed the long-standing observation of organic structures resembling bacteria, eukaryotic cells, and viruses within carbonaceous meteorites. These findings span decades, including the work of Pflug in the 1980s and more recent investigations by Richard Hoover. For instance, Figure 3 displays carbonaceous formations from the Murchison meteorite that are interpreted as fossilized microbiota. Notably, a virus-like structure documented by Pflug bears a striking morphological resemblance to a coronavirus, as illustrated in Figure 4. Additionally, a 2013 fall of carbonaceous stones in Sri Lanka yielded structures with clear biological features, shown in Figure 5. However, due to the remote location of the fall in Sri Lanka and the absence of video documentation, this event has yet to be officially recognized as a meteorite fall. 
Biofilms in space (on the ISS or future Mars missions) are not just survivors; they are structural innovators that behave in ways never seen on Earth.

1. The "Column-and-Canopy" Architecture
Microgravity fundamentally alters biofilm architecture. Unlike the gravity-limited, dense mounds seen on Earth, biofilms in space—such as Pseudomonas aeruginosa studied by Kim et al. (2013)—form a "column-and-canopy" structure. This allows the EPS to extend further into the surrounding fluid, maximizing nutrient absorption by acting as a broad surface area "hand" reaching into the environment.
2. Mechanical Challenges: The "Quiet" Fluid Environment
Lack of Convection and Nutrient Starvation: In microgravity environments, the elimination of buoyancy-driven convection results in fluid stagnation, restricting the transport of nutrients and metabolic byproducts strictly to the slow process of molecular diffusion (Castillo, 2023 ; Kim et al., 2013 ). Theoretically, this severe limitation in mass transfer should lead to microbial starvation, as the absence of natural fluid mixing prevents the adequate replenishment of essential resources (Sowmeya & Sathiavelu, 2025) .
Lack of Convection and Nutrient Starvation: To counteract these environmental constraints, biofilms undergo significant architectural adaptations. For instance, spaceflight experiments involving Pseudomonas aeruginosa demonstrate the development of a distinct "column-and-canopy" morphology, characterized by substantial increases in both overall biomass and structural thickness when compared to terrestrial controls (Kim et al., 2013) . Furthermore, research utilizing simulated microgravity indicates that these biofilms cultivate a highly porous architecture featuring complex, well-defined channel networks designed to maximize diffusion efficiency (Cheng et al., 2014) . These highly organized internal pathways essentially operate as a rudimentary circulatory system, far exceeding the structural complexity typically observed on Earth (Karapantsios, 2023 ; Sowmeya & Sathiavelu, 2025 ).
Biofilm as a Multicellular OrganismThe intentional formation of such sophisticated channel networks bolsters the paradigm that microgravity biofilms function less as mere aggregates of independent cells and more as unified, multicellular entities. By actively engineering an advanced internal architecture, the microbial community collectively overcomes the profound physical transport limitations imposed by their environment (Sowmeya & Sathiavelu, 2025 ).

3. The "Extreme Stress" Multicellularity
Radiation Shielding (eDNA and Pigments)
Research on dried biofilms of the cyanobacterium Chroococcidiopsis exposed on the ISS for 2.5 years demonstrated that biofilms recovered better than planktonic counterparts, accumulating less damage from UV and cosmic radiation (Baqué et al., 2019). This enhanced survival was attributed to "the presence of abundant extracellular polymeric substances" and features acquired upon drying, with the outer layers acting as a protective "bark" that shields inner cells (Baqué et al., 2019  ).
Enhanced Virulence and Stress Response
Bacterial behavior consistently changes during spaceflight, with "higher final cell counts, enhanced biofilm formation, increased virulence, and reduced susceptibility to antibiotics" reported for cells cultured in space. Gene expression data from the Antibiotic Effectiveness in Space (AES-1) experiment revealed a systematic activation of glucose starvation and acid resistance genes, corroborating the model where microgravity acts as a stress signal (NASA, 2025) . Studies confirm that pathogens like Salmonella enterica and Serratia marcescens display increased virulence when cultured in spaceflight environments (Ott et al., 2025)  .
someone could argue that space is the ultimate proof of multicellularity theory. When a biofilm is removed from the environment it evolved in (Earth), it doesn't just die; it re-engineers its own body using its "mechanical tools" (EPS) to adapt to the lack of gravity. This level of structural plasticity is a hallmark of complex multicellularity.

Yet these survive and live in a unique way that further prove that bacteria live in a multicellular like organism. Theses conditions include the cold weather of minus degrees and quiet strong winds in the atmosphere. Also, rapid change in the environment renders them to take precautions and adaptations that are even difficult for multicellular like organisms.
