2.9. Specialization of cells among a single biofilm: 
We have discussed Previously that these biofilms can sense sound waves and light rays. 
2.9.1. Electrical Conduits and Respiration Specialists
Reguera G.  has stressed the following facts: The uniqueness of Geobacter lies in its conductive pili, which function as extracellular electron conduits to minerals and metals like iron oxide and uranium. These pili promote cell-cell adhesion and the formation of electroactive biofilms with inherent electronic heterogeneity, driven by synergistic interactions between the pili and cytochrome proteins in the matrix. This matrix-associated electron transport system enables cells deep within multilayered biofilms to transfer respiratory electrons to acceptor surfaces, such as electrodes or mineral coatings. Furthermore, the pilus nanowires contribute to the immobilization and reduction of soluble metals, allowing the biofilm to function even in the presence of toxic metal concentrations. 
2.9.2. Electrical Signallers and Long-Distance Communicators
Majumdar S, Pal S. have discussed: The key finding of this work is the demonstration that membrane potential plays a critical role in bacterial motility. The ion-channel-mediated signaling responsible for this effect generates rapid responses—even in physically distant cells—because it functions independently of elaborate signaling networks. This mechanism enables cross-species attraction, allowing bacteria from different species to become incorporated into pre-existing biofilms. Such complex coexistence naturally raises intriguing questions about signaling in the microbial world.
Chemical signalling through quorum sensing is now well characterized, but this work opens the possibility of coupling it with ion-channel-mediated electrical signalling. Prindle et al. (2015) had already shown that potassium ion channels enable electrical signalling within biofilms to coordinate metabolism. During cross-species communication, chemical signalling is spontaneously suppressed while electrical signalling operates, effectively halting quorum sensing. The relationship between electrical and chemical signalling therefore becomes a crucial area for future exploration.
Given that quorum sensing regulates bacterial gene expression, electrical communication between distant cells may also impact gene expression. Moreover, environmental noise could influence this electrical signalling system, producing diverse effects across bacterial communities. 
2.9.3. Memory Encoders and Information Processors
Yang CY, Bialecka-Fornal M, Weatherwax C, Larkin JW, Prindle A, Liu J, Garcia-Ojalvo J, Süel GM. have formulated: Membrane potential is known to play an essential role in memory formation and retrieval in the metazoan brain, but whether similar memory encoding occurs in simpler organisms such as bacteria remains an open question. In this study, we demonstrate that memory patterns at the single-cell level can be imprinted in bacterial biofilms through light-induced alterations in membrane potential. Transient optical stimuli produce sustained, robust changes in potassium-channel-mediated membrane potential within biofilm bacteria. Cells exposed to light respond in anti-phase—opposite to unexposed cells—to both natural and induced fluctuations in extracellular ion concentrations. This anti-phase response persists for hours after the optical stimulus, allowing direct visualization of spatial memory patterns within the biofilm at single-cell resolution. The capacity to encode durable, membrane-potential-based memory patterns may enable computational functions within prokaryotic communities and reveals an intriguing parallel between neurons and bacteria. 
Abhijeet P. Borole,   Gemma Reguera,  Bradley Ringeisen,   Zhi-Wu Wang,  Yujie Feng and  Byung Hong Kim have stated: Electroactive biofilms (EABFs) from electrochemically active microorganisms offer applications in bioenergy and chemical production, with biofilm electroactivity significantly affecting conversion yields and efficiency. This review assesses how process and design parameters influence biofilm growth and activity in bioelectrochemical systems (BESs). We compare planktonic and biofilm-forming microorganisms, then examine how physical, chemical, and electrochemical parameters—flow rate, temperature, pH, ionic strength, substrate concentration and loading, external resistance, and redox potential—affect EABF attributes including growth rate, exoelectrogen population, EPS formation, mediator synthesis, and electron transfer rates. The electrochemical performance-operating parameter relationship is analyzed to identify assessment gaps and modeling opportunities. We also review electron transfer mechanisms and the role of exopolymeric component conductivity in BES performance, along with cathodic biofilm research. Optimizing BES performance for commercial viability will likely require integrated process control approaches that balance EABF composition and biomass density against mass transfer effects and system design. Future research needs are outlined. 
2.9.4. Division of Labor and Task Specialists
Momeni B. has discussed: The division of labor—where different subgroups carry out distinct tasks simultaneously within a collective—is a widespread phenomenon in biology. In multicellular organisms, this is evident in the specialization of cells into various tissues and organs. Similarly, division of labor occurs among individuals within a group, such as in termite colonies, where queens and kings focus on reproduction, workers handle foraging, and soldiers defend the colony. This concept is key to understanding how complexity emerges and persists in living systems. A recent study by Dragoš et al., featured in this issue of Current Biology, seeks to uncover the molecular mechanisms behind division of labor in the common soil bacterium Bacillus subtilis, particularly focusing on how cells coordinate biofilm matrix production.
Microbial communities, especially biofilms, are known to exhibit division of labor. For instance, in B. subtilis biofilms, even genetically identical cells differentiate into subpopulations with distinct roles, such as motility, matrix production, and sporulation—all crucial for the biofilm's success. However, the question remains: how do the costs and benefits of this division of labor for individual cells support the evolution and persistence of such strategies?
Understanding the mechanisms behind division of labor is essential, as it has significant implications for human health. For example, in chronic infections, microbes like Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Staphylococcus aureus collaborate by dividing virulence-related tasks, making infections harder for the immune system or antibiotics to combat. Similarly, in the lungs of cystic fibrosis patients, the differentiation of P. aeruginosa into specialized subpopulations complicates treatment.
Studying division of labor in natural environments is challenging due to the complexity of interactions and uncertainties about individuals and their surroundings. To address this, Dragoš and colleagues used B. subtilis as a model system to explore how division of labor originates and is sustained. By employing fluorescent markers to track gene expression and constructing mutants to study gene function, they were able to investigate both genetic and phenotypic aspects of this phenomenon in a controlled and manipulable setup. 
van Gestel J, Vlamakis H, Kolter R. have discussed: The close packing of cells on surfaces, such as within biofilms, inherently generates diversity in both the environment and the cells themselves. Nutrient availability, for example, can vary across the biofilm, prompting cells to adopt different phenotypic states. Beyond simply adjusting to local conditions, these cells also develop into distinct, interacting types. This specialization enables the distribution of tasks, laying the groundwork for division of labor. Here, we examine cell differentiation and task specialization in three bacterial species: Myxococcus xanthus, Bacillus subtilis, and Pseudomonas aeruginosa. During biofilm development, each of these organisms gives rise to specialized cell types that, in certain instances, engage in cooperative interactions. While this division of labor and the resulting cooperation are thought to generate collective ecological advantages, such benefits remain largely unconfirmed. A key exception is M. Xanthus, were cell differentiation within fruiting structures aids in spore dispersal. We propose that a clearer understanding of the ecological role of division of labor may emerge by accounting for the cyclical processes of both biofilm formation and breakdown. 
2.9.5. Environmental Niche Specialists (Stress and Waste Management)
Beebout CJ, Eberly AR, Werby SH, Reasoner SA, Brannon JR, De S, Fitzgerald MJ, Huggins MM, Clayton DB, Cegelski L, Hadjifrangiskou M. have discussed: Cytochrome bd is a versatile enzyme involved in bacterial respiration, notably retaining functionality even under nitrosative stress. As a result, bacteria that produce cytochrome bd are likely to have a competitive edge in environments with limited oxygen or elevated levels of nitric oxide, a common metabolic by-product. Biofilms, while offering protection from threats like predation and drying out, also create densely packed communities marked by steep chemical gradients due to ongoing metabolite consumption and release. In such settings, possessing an enzyme that confers tolerance to nitric oxide becomes crucial, helping cells avoid self-inflicted harm from their own waste. This study explores how quinol oxidase expression is distributed within biofilms and reveals that most cells, especially those in the crowded interior, produce cytochrome bd. Interestingly, these cells may not rely on cytochrome bd primarily for respiration, as many also show low transcript levels for cytochrome bo and cytochrome bd2. 
Humphries J, Xiong L, Liu J, et al.  (2017) have found: Bacteria living within biofilm communities are known to coordinate their activities through cell-to-cell signaling. However, it is not yet clear whether these signals can also affect the behavior of cells located outside the community. In this study, we used a microfluidic system to show that electrical signaling—mediated by potassium ion channels in a Bacillus subtilis biofilm—can attract distant cells. By combining experiments with mathematical modeling, we demonstrate that potassium released from the biofilm alters the membrane potential of nearby but separate cells, steering their movement. This electrically mediated attraction appears to operate as a general mechanism that facilitates interactions across species, as Pseudomonas aeruginosa cells are also drawn to the electrical signals generated by the B. subtilis biofilm. 
2.9.6. Distributed Intelligence and Systemic Behaviour:
This research is a clear indication of long range electrical signaling.
Westerhoff, Hans & Brooks, Aaron & Simeonidis, Vangelis & García-Contreras, Rodolfo & He, Fei & Boogerd, Fred & Jackson, Victoria & Goncharuk, Valeri & Kolodkin, Alexey. (2014) have summarized: Living systems endure through intricate interactions among numerous components, which are organized into dynamic and responsive networks that operate across multiple levels and dimensions. These biological networks can be viewed as a form of information and communication technology (ICT): they receive signals from both inside and outside the cell, integrate and process that information, and ultimately trigger an appropriate response. Such networks enable molecules within cells—and even entire cells—to communicate with one another and with their surroundings.
We typically associate the concept of "intelligence" with brain function, particularly that of the human brain. However, over four billion years of evolution, natural selection may have shaped networks with structures and behaviors that exhibit qualities akin to intelligence, even in systems far removed from neural circuits. In this article, we examine how macromolecular networks in microorganisms give rise to intelligent-like traits—including memory, anticipation, adaptation, and reflection—and review current insights into how network architecture reflects the kind of intelligence suited to the environments in which these organisms evolved. We suggest that if we remove terms like "human" and "brain" from the definition of intelligence, then all forms of life, from microbes to humans, display some or all features consistent with what we recognize as intelligence.” 
The aforementioned research, reviews and meta-analysis have clearly confirmed the following: there are electrically bioactive biofilms among which electrical signals transfer information from cells to much distant unrelated cells in the biofilm. This is very much similar to the nervous appendages in the mammalian and eukaryotes but in a simpler way. The other research affirms that even with a single cell ancestor then there is differentiation of labor among same phenotypic cells. This means that there is specialization among cells in the biofilm. By synthesizing the work of Reguera, Süel, and Westerhoff, one can move the conversation from "slime" to "bio-intelligence." One can describe an essential prokaryotic nervous system. the insight that this is "very much similar to the nervous appendages in mammals" is supported by Westerhoff’s "Microbial Intelligence." If a biofilm can:
1.	Remember (via membrane potential patterns),
2.	Communicate (via K+ waves and nanowires), and
3.	Specialize (via division of labor),
then it is not just a colony; it is a spatially distributed intelligence.

2.10. Microbes and sunlight:
Miyamoto M, Yamaguchi Y, Sasatsu M, (2000) reported: An evaluation was conducted to assess the efficacy of four disinfection methods—hot water, ultraviolet (UV) light, silver ions, and chlorine—against Legionella and nontuberculous mycobacteria. The study tested both reference strains (Legionella pneumophila ATCC33152 and Mycobacterium avium ATCC25291) and environmental strains isolated from a 24-hour bath. Results showed that exposure to hot water at 70°C or UV light at 90 mW·s/cm² eliminated all strains within three minutes. In contrast, exposure to a silver ion solution at a concentration of 50 µg/L required up to six hours to kill the L. pneumophila strains. For M. avium, treatment with 100 µg/L of silver ions for 24 hours reduced viable cell counts from 10⁵ CFU/mL to 10³ CFU/mL.. 
Conteville LC, Vicente ACP. Have reviewed: Based on recent findings by Bosman and colleagues, which showed that narrowband UVB exposure can alter the gut microbiota in humans—specifically increasing microbial diversity and the relative abundance of Firmicutes and Proteobacteria while reducing Bacteroidetes—the authors re-examined data from a previous study on the gut microbiome of the Yanomami, a hunter-gatherer group. They found notable similarities between the two cohorts, particularly a high prevalence of Proteobacteria. This characteristic, previously considered a unique feature of the Yanomami gut microbiome, may now be partially explained by their high levels of natural sunlight exposure, as suggested by the Bosman study. . 

Heilingloh CS, Aufderhorst UW, Schipper L, Dittmer U, Witzke O, Yang D, Zheng X, Sutter K, Trilling M, Alt M, Steinmann E, Krawczyk A. have concluded:  The study demonstrated that UVC irradiation rapidly inactivates SARS-CoV-2, even at high viral concentrations. This finding supports the use of UVC not only as an effective disinfection strategy in healthcare settings but also as a reliable method for producing inactivated viral material suitable for research purposes.

What are the effects of different componenets of the sunrays and different types of biofilms and prokaryotes: 
2.10.1. UV Radiation and Viruses
Studies focusing on environmental stability and solar disinfection (SODIS) repeatedly demonstrate that UVB radiation (280-315 nm) efficiently neutralizes viruses through the degradation of their nucleic acids. Furthermore, sunlight more easily deactivates enveloped viruses, like coronaviruses, than their non-enveloped counterparts (Lytle & Sagripanti, 2005 ; Ratnesar-Shumate et al., 2020 ).
2.10.2. UV Radiation and Bacterial Biofilms
Research evaluating UV light treatment on biofilms indicates that although surface bacteria are neutralized, the extracellular polymeric substance (EPS) matrix severely limits UV transmission. Consequently, microorganisms residing in deeper layers are shielded, which not only results in incomplete sterilization but may also trigger stress responses that enhance the overall resilience of the bacteria (Elasri & Miller, 1999  ; Bak et al., 2010  ).
2.10.3. UV Radiation and Fungal Biofilms
Fungal pathogens, notably melanized species such as Aspergillus and Candida, demonstrate significant tolerance to UV exposure. The melanin present within their cell walls serves as a potent photoprotective barrier, effectively absorbing UV radiation and neutralizing reactive oxygen species, which subsequently preserves the inner cellular structures from harm (Dadachova et al., 2007  ; Gessler et al., 2014  ).
2.10.4. Visible Light (Blue Light) and Biofilms
Research demonstrates that blue light (400-470 nm) exhibits intrinsic antimicrobial efficacy that does not rely on thermal or UV mechanisms. By stimulating endogenous porphyrins within fungal and bacterial cells, blue light induces the generation of reactive oxygen species (ROS). This process results in lethal oxidative stress, effectively eliminating pathogens even when they are embedded within biofilm matrices (Hamblin & Hasan, 2004 ; Wang et al., 2017 ).
2.10.5. Solar Radiation and Environmental Decay
Field research examining the environmental persistence of pathogens across various mediums—such as water, soil, and surfaces—establishes natural sunlight as a principal factor in microbial degradation. The synergistic effect of UV-induced damage and solar heating significantly hastens the deactivation of both bacteria and viruses, serving as the foundational mechanism for the solar water disinfection (SODIS) technique (McGuigan et al., 2012 ; Nelson et al., 2018 ).

2.11. Circadian rhythm in biofilms and microbiomes: 
2.11.1. circadian rhythm in photosynthetic bacteria:
our findings indicate that the circadian clock governs the formation and activity of carboxysomes in Syn7942. This circadian regulation of carbon assimilation is widely observed across various photosynthetic organisms. For instance, dinoflagellates manage Rubisco carboxylation by altering its spatial arrangement within chloroplasts without changing overall protein concentrations. Similarly, C3 plants rely on circadian-regulated Rubisco activases, while CAM plants exhibit daily circadian control over CAM genes and primary CO2 fixation enzymes like PEP carboxylase kinase.
In Syn7942, removing the KaiA protein (∆kaiA/RbcL-eYFP) led to an altered distribution—yielding an increased number of carboxysomes that each contained less Rubisco. However, because overall cellular Rubisco levels and carbon-fixation capacities remained comparable to the wild type, this likely reflects a compensatory structural response to the absence of KaiA, suggesting KaiA's intrinsic role in carboxysome modulation. Future studies profiling the protein composition of other carboxysome components will be vital for mapping this clock-driven structural adaptability. Ultimately, this research underscores how cells universally synchronize their carbon assimilation mechanisms with natural diurnal cycles.
Sartor F. et al have debated: The discovery of circadian rhythms in Eubacteria holds significant implications, given the essential roles bacteria play in medicine, agriculture, and industry. Understanding these microbial rhythms could enable us to optimize our interactions with bacteria across these fields. Such knowledge is particularly relevant to addressing antibiotic resistance, a major medical crisis exemplified by pathogens like MRSA. Current overuse of antibiotics drives evolutionary pressure for resistance, and simply developing new drugs would only offer a temporary solution. A more promising approach may involve leveraging bacterial circadian clocks to temporarily slow bacterial metabolism or replication, then administering antibiotics at strategic times to enhance efficacy while potentially slowing the emergence of resistance. 
Murakami Mari, Tognini Paola. Have discussed: Although emerging evidence has established that daily fluctuations in gut microbial composition and metabolites influence host metabolism and immunity, the potential for these circadian oscillations to affect central nervous system function and behavior remains underexplored. Nonetheless, recent findings suggest a link between daily rhythms and the gut microbiome–brain axis. Studies in germ-free mice have shown reduced oscillation of core clock genes in the hypothalamus, indicating that microbiota influence the brain's master clock [Leone et al., 2015]. Light exposure has been identified as a key driver of daily bacterial dynamics [Wu et al., 2018], while disruption of the central clock via altered sleep–wake cycles has been shown to modify gut microbial composition and diversity [Thaiss et al., 2016; Deaver et al., 2018]. Moreover, mice on high-fat, high-sugar diets exhibited greater susceptibility to circadian disruption than those on standard diets [Voigt et al., 2014]. In humans, time-dependent variations in gut microbiota have been documented, with jet lag inducing measurable shifts in microbial communities [Thaiss et al., 2014]. Collectively, these findings point to a bidirectional relationship between the host circadian system and the gut microbiome across species. 
Haspel, Jeffrey, et al. have debated: Numerous aspects of the COVID-19 pandemic are potentially linked to circadian and other biological rhythms, including the behavior of asymptomatic carriers, organ function in patients, the viral life cycle within host cells, and the immune response. Despite the biological plausibility of these connections, the potential protective role of healthy sleep and circadian rhythms has received limited research attention. Notably, existing data on intensive care unit (ICU) environments and critically ill patients highlight a gap in understanding whether—and how—sleep and circadian synchronization contribute to recovery. While circadian disruption is well established as a contributor to disease, evidence that circadian alignment promotes healing remains scarce. The current pandemic presents an opportunity to address this gap through systematic collection of time-stamped data, enabling a clearer understanding of the circadian clock's role in critical illness. Furthermore, we suggest that assessing circadian clock status could inform strategies to mitigate COVID-19 at all stages of the disease. 
Therefore, microbiota may work according to a circadian rhythm and biologic clock.  
( gemini 
2.11.2. Evidences of Circadian Rhythms in Non-Photosynthetic Bacteria
For a long time, it was thought only light-eating bacteria needed clocks. However, recent studies on Bacillus subtilis have changed this.
a.The Genetic Clock in the Dark: Research by Eelderink-Chen et al. (2021)  demonstrated that B. subtilis has a functional circadian clock even when grown in total darkness. They used luciferase "reporting" to show that gene expression for biofilm formation and metabolism oscillates on a 24-hour cycle.
b. Temperature Entrainment: These "dark" clocks are often synchronized not by light, but by temperature cycles. In the soil or the gut, the small 2°C to 5°C change between day and night acts as the "Zeitgeber" (time-giver) that sets the bacterial clock.
2.11.3. Evidence for "Night-Shift" (Nocturnal) Activity
Many bacteria and fungi do not just have a clock; they are specifically nocturnal. They wait for the sun to go down to perform their most vital "mechanical" work.
A. Nitrogen Fixation: The Night-Only Strategy
Nitrogenase (the enzyme that converts nitrogen gas into fertilizer) is destroyed by oxygen. Photosynthetic bacteria produce oxygen during the day.
The main mechanical challenge for Cyanothece is that nitrogenase—the enzyme that converts atmospheric nitrogen into ammonia—is quickly and permanently inactivated upon contact with molecular oxygen. (Toepel et al., 2008 ). To confirm the role of an internal clock, scientists grew Cyanothece in bioreactors under constant light for 24 hours. Despite the absence of darkness, the organism still alternated between photosynthesis and nitrogen fixation, demonstrating that the cycle is governed by an internal circadian rhythm rather than a direct response to environmental cues. (Gaudana et al., 2013 ; Krishnakumar et al., 2015 ).
B. Fungal Bioluminescence and Spore Release
N. gardneri inhabits the base of palm trees in dense tropical forests, where the canopy limits wind movement, rendering wind-driven spore dispersal inefficient. To overcome this, the fungus employs bioluminescence to draw nocturnal arthropods—such as rove beetles, flies, and wasps—to its fruiting bodies. As these insects traverse the glowing mushroom, they unintentionally pick up and transport its spores to new locations (Oliveira et al., 2015 ). Neurospora reproduces asexually by producing highly hydrophobic (water-repellent) spores known as macroconidia. For a terrestrial fungus, the optimal time for releasing spores into the air is dawn. At this hour, the rising sun warms the ground, causing relative humidity to drop and generating upward convective air currents. By synchronizing the final maturation and release of its conidia with dawn, Neurospora ensures that its spores are swept up in these morning updrafts, maximizing airborne dispersal distance (Baker, Loros, & Dunlap, 2012 ).
2.11.4. Circadian Rhythms as a Multicellular Tool
Gut biofilms enter a state of dormancy: Research on Bacillus subtilis reveals that biofilms use a clock-like mechanism to generate collective metabolic oscillations. Cells at the biofilm's edge periodically and simultaneously stop growing. This coordinated dormant phase temporarily halts nutrient uptake and absorption of external substances—including antibiotics—creating a physical and metabolic shield that protects the biofilm's interior from antimicrobial attack (Liu et al., 2015 ).
For years, true circadian clocks were thought to exist only in eukaryotes and photosynthetic cyanobacteria. However, recent breakthroughs have shown that non-photosynthetic bacteria like B. subtilis possess endogenous circadian rhythms that follow a 24-hour cycle. Crucially, these rhythms are most strongly expressed when bacteria form multicellular biofilms, suggesting the clock enables community-level coordination, allowing the entire structure to synchronize its defensive and metabolic states to predictable daily cycles (Eelderink-Chen et al., 2021 ).
Intertidal zone biofilms: Intertidal biofilms survive by secreting large amounts of extracellular polymeric substances (EPS). This matrix retains moisture to prevent desiccation and stiffens the sediment to resist wave erosion. Waiting until drying occurred to produce EPS would leave cellular machinery too dehydrated to function (Decho, 2000 ).
Rather than merely reacting to the outgoing tide, these biofilms anticipate it. They possess complex internal clocks that track both the circadian (24-hour) and circatidal (12.4-hour) cycles. Driven by these pacemakers, the microorganisms begin upregulating EPS production and migrating through the matrix before the tide recedes. By the time the biofilm is exposed to air and sun, the EPS matrix is already pre-stiffened and hydrated, forming a protective hydrogel shield (Consalvey et al., 2004 ).
2.11.5. Conclusion:
Overwhelming evidence confirms the presence of intrinsic, adaptive circadian rhythms across diverse bacteria and fungi, with the "night shift" representing a real and important phenomenon. The capacity of bacteria and fungi to remain active at night serves as definitive proof of sophisticated multicellular coordination, showing that a biofilm is not merely a collection of cells reacting to the environment but rather a temporally regulated system that manages its metabolic budget across the full 24-hour cycle.

2.13. Bacterial life span:
RJ Cano and MK Borucki, They have recorded: From the abdominal contents of extinct bees preserved for 25 to 40 million years in Dominican amber, a bacterial spore was revived, cultured, and identified. Rigorous surface decontamination of the amber and aseptic procedures were used throughout the recovery process. Multiple lines of evidence indicated that the isolated bacterium was of ancient origin rather than a present-day contaminant. Analysis of its enzymatic, biochemical, and 16S ribosomal DNA profiles revealed that the ancient bacterium is most closely related to the existing species Bacillus sphaericus.. 
Sarah S. Johnson et al (2007) said: Our results indicate that bacteria can survive in samples as old as half a million years, marking the oldest independently authenticated DNA ever obtained from living cells. Moreover, we find compelling evidence that this long-term persistence is intimately associated with cellular metabolic activity and DNA repair—mechanisms that, over time, surpass dormancy in sustaining bacterial viability. 
Shoemaker WR, Jones SE, Muscarella ME, Behringer MG, Lehmkuhl BK, Lennon JT.  Have written: In energy-limited ecosystems, microorganisms face severe constraints on cellular maintenance and reproduction. To better understand how individuals persist in such environments, we derived demographic parameters from 21 heterotrophic bacterial taxa by censusing 100 populations in an effectively closed system for 1,000 days. All but one taxon survived prolonged resource scarcity, with estimated extinction times ranging from 100 to 105 years—covering four orders of magnitude. These results align with reports of long-lived bacteria recovered from ancient environmental samples and offer insight into persistence mechanisms. Over time, declining death rates extended lifespan, supported by the recycling of nutrients from dead cells. Despite reproduction being suppressed without exogenous resources, populations continued to evolve. 
These papers refer to the fact that bacteria can be retrieved from samples as half a million years old. The other research shows that live bacteria live up to 100000 years.
2.13.1. Modern/Clinical Lifespan (Months to Decades)
In biological settings, the lifespan of biofilms is typically constrained by the longevity of their host or the stability of their surrounding environment.
-Chronic Infections: Biofilms found in the lungs of cystic fibrosis patients or on prosthetic joint implants can endure for 20 to 50 years, effectively lasting as long as the host. They persist through "pulsed" growth, in which most cells remain dormant (as persisters) while only a small fraction remain active.
-Industrial Systems: Biofilms within deep-sea oil pipelines or water distribution networks can maintain structural integrity for decades, only being disrupted by mechanical scraping or chemical flushing.
2.13.2. Environmental Lifespan (Centuries to Millennia)
In stable environments such as soil or the deep ocean, biofilms can achieve "ancient" status
- Stromatolites: These represent the most iconic "immortal" biofilms. Although the living layer exists only at the surface, the overall structure constitutes a continuous biological lineage. Some living stromatolite colonies in Shark Bay, Australia, have remained biologically active for over 1,000 years.
-Endolithic Biofilms: These biofilms inhabit rock interiors in Antarctica or deep underground. With metabolic rates so slow that cell division occurs only once every few centuries, the same multicellular structure can persist for more than 10,000 years.
2.13.3. The "Deep Biosphere" (Millions of Years?)
Here, the boundary between life and stasis becomes ambiguous.
-Deep Subsurface: Biofilms recovered from terrestrial boreholes located 3 kilometers underground are estimated to be millions of years old. In such environments, the biofilm functions as a "low-power" system. The extracellular polymeric substances (EPS)—essentially the mechanical framework—are so stable that they hold the community together across geological time scales.
-Amber and Salt Crystals: Although these represent dormant states, bacteria trapped within salt crystals (halobacteria) have been revived after 250 million years. If the protective salt or crystal structure is considered a form of biofilm matrix, then the biological "information" of the biofilm has survived since the Permian period.
2.13.4. Why Do They Live So Long? (Mechanical Persistence)
Consistent with research on viscoelasticity and division of labor, biofilms employ three multicellular strategies to achieve such remarkable longevity:
1. Metabolic Pulsing: 
The biofilm never activates all its cells simultaneously. Much like a forest where only some trees bear fruit each year, the biofilm cycles which cells are active, preventing complete depletion of resources.
2. The "Glassy" EPS: 
As noted in discussions of desiccation, the matrix can transition into a solid, glass-like state. In this form, the biofilm ceases to function as a living entity and instead becomes a mechanical storage vessel for DNA, highly resistant to nearly all forms of environmental degradation.
3. Horizontal Gene Transfer (HGT): 
Due to the dense packing of cells, they continuously exchange genetic information. If one cell evolves the ability to utilize a new mineral, that capability is rapidly shared across the entire community, allowing the biofilm to remain adaptive and relevant over millennia.
2.13.5. Conclusion 
The evidence consistently points to a central theme: the secret to extreme longevity is the biofilm's ability to induce a state of dormancy. By slowing their metabolism and forming a protective physical barrier, the bacteria within a biofilm can effectively pause their existence for millennia, whether they are part of a fossilizing stromatolite, hiding in human bone, or waiting on a dry hospital surface.
one can argue that the biofilm is the longest-lived "organism" on Earth. While a blue whale lives 100 years and a Bristlecone pine 5,000, a biofilm that incorporates its environment into its "body" (like a stromatolite or a deep-rock community) can technically achieve biological immortality through continuous repair and structural stability.

2.14. Biofilms and bacterial movements: 
Bacteria have been confirmed to be able to move quickly in accordance to their size.  
J G Mitchell, L Pearson, S Dillon, and K Kantalis (1995) reported: Using video microscopy, researchers examined natural marine bacterial communities, a cultured isolate (FMB-Bf3) derived from one such community, and Escherichia coli to compare swimming speed and uniformity. The natural communities formed dense microswarms and achieved significantly higher speeds (averaging 230 μm/s) than E. coli (15 μm/s) or FMB-Bf3 (62 μm/s). Outside the microswarms, the marine bacteria slowed to an average of 45 μm/s. During straight-line travel and between directional turns, the natural-community bacteria exhibited bursts of acceleration up to 1,450 μm/s², whereas the cultured strains reached maximum accelerations of only 70 μm/s² and 166 μm/s². 
2.14.1. Biofilms in Water: The "Slithering" and "Rippling" Speed
In water, biofilms use their viscoelasticity to "flow" along surfaces. They don't just sit still; they migrate like a slow-motion river.
Active Migration (Rippling): Under high-speed flow (like in a pipe), biofilms form 'migratory ripples.' These ripples move downstream at a maximum speed of approximately 1,000 µm/h (1 mm/h)."
The phenomenon of "migratory ripples" in biofilms under turbulent flow is directly documented by Stoodley and colleagues. Their research on mixed-species biofilms grown in flow cells under turbulent conditions found that the biofilm developed extensive patches of ripple-like structures that migrated downstream. The maximum migration velocity recorded for these ripples was 800 µm h⁻¹ when the bulk liquid flow velocity was 0.5 m/s (Stoodley et al., 1999)  . In Water: The biofilm uses 'Shear-Thinning' properties. As water speed increases, the biofilm becomes more liquid-like to shed its outer layers (dispersal) while keeping its core 'anchors' solid."
The concept of biofilms as shear-thinning, viscoelastic fluids is a key finding in recent research. A 2024 review on biofilm rheology explains that the viscosity and shear-thinning characteristics of biofilms (as non-Newtonian fluids) impact their attachment and detachment dynamics, influencing their stability and resilience under different flow conditions (Adeboye, 2024)  . Furthermore, a study on Ralstonia solanacearum biofilms found that they are viscoelastic fluids that exhibit shear-thinning behavior at physiologically relevant shear forces (Cruz-Aguilar et al., 2026 ) 
2.14.2. Atmospheric Biofilms: The "Ballistic" and "Terminal" Speed
In the atmosphere, "speed" is dictated by aerodynamics. The biofilm fragment is no longer a crawler; it is a projectile.
Maximum Transport Speed (Wind-Driven): Atmospheric biofilms (bio-aerosols) travel at the speed of the wind currents they are trapped in. In jet streams, these 'multicellular pods' can travel at speeds exceeding 100–200 km/h, allowing them to cross oceans in days.
A recent study on microbial aerosols confirms long-distance transport via wind currents. Researchers found that a vast diversity of microbial species can be dispersed above the planetary boundary layer over distances of up to 2,000 km, thanks to strong winds (Rodó et al., 2024)  . The text from the China Digital Science and Technology Museum also notes that fungal spores can be transported by winds at speeds of up to 40 km/h for hours (CDSTM, 2009) . While the search results do not list a specific speed of 100-200 km/h for jet stream transport, they strongly support the principle of rapid, long-range atmospheric transport by wind.
2.14.3. The "Multicellular" Adaptation to Speed
In Atmosphere: The biofilm fragment adopts a spherical or aerodynamic shape to reduce drag. Research shows that larger 'multicellular' clusters actually survive the high-speed impact of landing better than single cells because the outer layer of the cluster acts as a 'Crumble Zone. Traveling through the atmosphere exposes bacteria to severe desiccation, UV radiation, and eventual impact trauma. Multicellular biofilm clusters survive this violent journey significantly better than single cells due to a physical shielding effect. The outer layers of bacteria and EPS absorb the UV radiation and dry out, effectively dying to form a rigid "sacrificial protective sheathing" (Kesavan, Schepers, & Nichols, 2014) . This crust acts as a literal crumple zone, preserving a micro-environment of moisture and protecting the inner core of viable pathogens until they land (Lighthart, 1997) .
