2.15. Bacteria and fungi can produce certain products of alcoholics: 
It is well known fact that fungi and bacteria are producing wines and other types of alcoholics.
Aline Lonvaud-Funel, Annick Joyeux (1994) noted that: A strain of Leuconostoc œnos 9204 was isolated and investigated because it continued to produce histamine even after multiple transfers. In a synthetic medium, this strain generated significant amounts of histamine, particularly under the most limited nutritional conditions (in the absence of glucose and L-malic acid). These findings clearly indicate that Leuc. œnos involved in winemaking could contribute to the production of biogenic amines. Additionally, the vinification process may affect the final concentration of amines in wine.. 
Bastard A, Coelho C, Briandet R, Canette A, Gougeon R, Alexandre H, Guzzo J, Weidmann S. have investigated: The winemaking process begins with the alcoholic fermentation of must, frequently followed by malolactic fermentation (MLF). MLF is primarily driven by the lactic acid bacterium Oenococcus oeni and is employed to enhance wine quality when a reduction in acidity is desired. It also helps prevent microbial spoilage and enhances the wine’s sensory characteristics.
Earlier observations revealed that O. oeni can survive for several months under the challenging conditions found in wine when attached to oak barrels. Given that biofilms represent a common microbial lifestyle in natural environments, researchers investigated the ability of O. oeni to form biofilms on winemaking surfaces such as stainless steel and oak chips. Using Scanning Electron Microscopy and Confocal Laser Scanning Microscopy, they found that O. oeni could adhere to these materials and form structurally organized microcolonies encased in extracellular substances.
To evaluate the survival benefits of this biofilm lifestyle in wine, the characteristics of biofilm-grown cells were compared with those of free-living (planktonic) cells after inoculation into a fermented must (with pH levels of 3.5 or 3.2 and 12% ethanol). The findings showed that growing O. oeni as a biofilm led to (i) improved resilience to wine-related stress, and (ii) effective malolactic activity, demonstrating strong functional performance. 
Liu D, Chen Q, Zhang P, Chen D, Howell KS have gained the following result:  The findings indicate that both soil and must microbiomes can be used to differentiate wine-growing regions. A correlation was observed between microbial communities and wine metabolic profiles across varying environmental conditions, particularly in relation to soil characteristics and weather patterns. Fungal communities were found to contribute to the regional distinctiveness of wine. The evidence suggests that the soil microbiome serves as a source of fungi associated with grapes and must, and that weather and soil may shape wine characteristics through their influence on the soil fungal community. 
conclusion
Bacterial and fungal biofilms play a remarkably complex and significant role in alcohol production. Far from being passive structures, they act as active agents that can substantially enhance, alter, and safeguard the fermentation process. Drawing from current evidence, their functions fall into three primary categories: boosting production, facilitating chain elongation to generate higher alcohols, and shaping flavor and overall profile.
These alcohol-producing biofilms can be aptly described as "industrial organs." They exhibit a clear division of labor, with outer layers managing exposure to toxic alcohol while inner layers concentrate on the high-energy conversion of sugars. This spatial organization is precisely what enables them to surpass the performance of single-cell fermentations in challenging, high-gravity conditions.
On the other hand, they could survive the toxic effect of the alcohol they are producing. Once ingested by humans these alcohols have antimicrobial effect and prebiotic effect which mean they enhance the growth of some strains and destroy others(dysbiosis). Also, once ingested by humans they have temporarily inflicted a behavioral change. So, can these behavioral changes be programmed from them? could they lead to further, changes in the biofilm in the human body?
2.16. The Microbiome of healthy canines:
Moreno PS, Wagner J, Mansfield CS, Stevens M, Gilkerson JR, Kirkwood CD. have discussed: While the virome has been extensively studied in many animal species and various body sites, leading to the discovery of numerous viruses, the fecal virome of healthy dogs has remained unexplored. This study used shotgun metagenomics to characterize the fecal virome of healthy dogs and those with acute diarrhea in Australia. The viral sequences detected encompassed a variety of RNA and DNA virus families, including known enteric pathogens. A total of twelve viral families were identified, four of which were bacteriophages. The eight eukaryotic viral families found were Astroviridae, Coronaviridae, Reoviridae, Picornaviridae, Caliciviridae, Parvoviridae, Adenoviridae, and Papillomaviridae. Notably, Astroviridae, Picornaviridae, and Caliciviridae were detected exclusively in dogs with acute diarrhea, with Astroviridae being the most prevalent in this group. Given its high prevalence, the complete genome of a canine astrovirus was sequenced and characterized. The findings demonstrate that metagenomic analysis is an effective tool for investigating viral populations in canine feces, highlighting the need for further research into the epidemiological and biological significance of these viruses. 
Pilla R, Suchodolski JS. Have found: Here is a paraphrase of the provided text: The gut microbiome plays a crucial role in host metabolism, defense against pathogens, and immune system education, and through these core functions, it directly or indirectly influences most physiological processes in the host. Molecular techniques have advanced our understanding by revealing a diverse array of previously unknown, unculturable bacteria. In the canine gastrointestinal (GI) tract, most bacterial sequences identified belong to five phyla: Firmicutes, Fusobacteria, Bacteroidetes, Proteobacteria, and Actinobacteria. Although the composition of the microbiome varies along the GI tract, most clinical research focuses on fecal samples. Factors such as age, diet, and various environmental influences play significant roles in maintaining a healthy microbiome; however, the shifts they cause are minor compared to those observed in diseased animals. GI disorders are the most evident conditions linked to gut dysbiosis. In dogs, both chronic and acute intestinal inflammation are associated with substantial changes in the composition of the intestinal microbiota. Gut dysbiosis occurs when these alterations lead to functional changes in the microbial transcriptome, proteome, or metabolome. 
We have learned the fact that healthy dogs have microbiomes that contain viromes as well.
The relationship between the canine microbiome and its host is a complex, living ecosystem. As you noted from the research by Moreno and Pilla, this system isn't just a collection of bacteria; it’s a multi-kingdom environment involving viruses (the virome), fungi (the mycobiome), and bacteria.

2.16.1. How the Canine Microbiota Reshapes Itself
The "reshaping" of the gut environment is a dynamic process driven by competitive exclusion, horizontal gene transfer, and host signals.
a. The "Vicious Cycle" of Dysbiosis
Gastrointestinal disorders in dogs, such as inflammatory bowel disease (IBD) and acute diarrhea, trigger a profound structural reshaping of the gut microbiome. This dysbiosis is fundamentally driven by a die-off of beneficial strict anaerobes and a simultaneous bloom of facultative anaerobes (Suchodolski, 2011 ; Ziese & Suchodolski, 2021 ). In cases of IBD, this shift manifests as an overgrowth of Proteobacteria (e.g., E. coli) and a depletion of beneficial groups like Fusobacteria, Bacteroidetes, and specific Firmicutes (such as Faecalibacterium spp. and Ruminococcaceae). Conversely, dogs with acute diarrhea typically exhibit elevated levels of Clostridium perfringens coupled with decreases in Ruminococcaceae and Blautia spp. (Ziese & Suchodolski, 2021) . This microbial imbalance ultimately drives a "vicious cycle" within the gut: the resulting dysbiosis provokes intestinal inflammation, which in turn further alters the local environment to continuously favor the survival of pathogenic bacteria over beneficial commensals (Ziese & Suchodolski, 2021) .
b. Biofilm Multicellularity: The canine gastrointestinal tract is naturally lined with protective biofilms, making biofilm formation a crucial and highly desirable attribute for effective probiotic supplements. When probiotic strains successfully form these structures, they are able to adhere to and colonize the mucosal surface, often integrating into mixed microbial communities (Gallina et al., 2024) . Once established, these probiotic biofilms provide significant benefits to the host: they promote mucosal healing, support a stable community structure, enhance immunomodulatory responses, and competitively exclude colonizing pathogens. For example, research evaluating a targeted canine probiotic blend (Lactobacillus acidophilus, Lacticaseibacillus casei, and Enterococcus faecium) demonstrated its capacity to adhere strongly to canine epithelial cells and generate biofilms as robust as those formed by Staphylococcus aureus (Gallina et al., 2024) . However, this protective barrier remains susceptible to environmental shifts; the severe inflammation driven by gut dysbiosis can force a reconstruction of the enteric flora, fundamentally disrupting the structure and function of these vital biofilms (Ziese & Suchodolski, 2021) .
c. Metabolic Crosstalk (Short-Chain Fatty Acids - SCFAs)
The canine intestinal microbiome functions as a crucial metabolic organ that ferments dietary components into essential nutrients, most notably short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs) (Ziese & Suchodolski, 2021) . These SCFAs, particularly butyrate, are vital for maintaining host gastrointestinal health due to their potent anti-inflammatory properties and their role as the primary energy source for colonocytes (Ziese & Suchodolski, 2021) . Because this microbial ecosystem is highly responsive to nutritional inputs, dietary interventions directly reshape both the fecal microbial community and its resulting fermentation profile (Sandri et al., 2017 ; Hiney et al., 2024  ). For example, comparative studies reveal that feeding dogs a raw meat-based diet—as opposed to traditional commercial kibble—significantly alters the microbiota composition and metabolome, ultimately driving a higher relative production of beneficial butyrate (Sandri et al., 2017 ; Hiney et al., 2024 ). Consequently, a fundamental shift in diet directly dictates the bacterial population, which in turn reshapes the metabolic health of the host.
2.16.2. Survival and Transfer: From Dogs to Humans
When canine microbes move from the gut (or mouth) into a human or a new environment, their survival depends on their metabolic plasticity.
Zoonotic Survival (The "Jump")
Canine microbes are frequently transferred to humans through "social licking" or shared environments.
Capnocytophaga canimorsus is a Gram-negative commensal bacterium that colonizes the oral cavities of dogs and cats. Transmission to humans, typically through bites or licking of open wounds, can result in severe sepsis, especially in immunocompromised or asplenic individuals. The bacterium's primary survival strategy involves immune evasion. It achieves this by modifying the lipid A component of its lipopolysaccharide (LPS). Due to the high specificity of human Toll-like receptor 4 (TLR4), this altered LPS fails to activate the receptor, thereby circumventing the host's initial inflammatory response and enabling the bacterium to proliferate undetected.(Stephens) 
Antimicrobial Resistance (AMR): Companion animals are increasingly recognized as reservoirs for antimicrobial-resistant (AMR) bacteria. Administration of beta-lactam antibiotics, such as amoxicillin, to dogs generates selective pressure within the canine gastrointestinal tract, promoting the proliferation of extended-spectrum beta-lactamase (ESBL)-producing Escherichia coli and vancomycin-resistant enterococci (VRE). As these bacteria share environmental niches with humans, they have the capacity to colonize the human gut. Upon colonization, they employ horizontal gene transfer (HGT)—predominantly via conjugation of mobile genetic elements such as plasmids—to directly transfer resistance determinants to the human microbiome. (Pomba et al., 2017) .
2.16.3. conclusion
In light of the research on bacterial multicellularity, the canine gastrointestinal tract may be regarded as a valuable in vivo system for the study of this phenomenon. The transition of microbial communities from a planktonic to a biofilm-associated state within the canine mucosa represents a key adaptive mechanism. This structural reorganization facilitates microbial survival against both the acidic conditions of the stomach and the host immune responses encountered within the intestinal environment. Are these viromes inside the canine gut more virulent than others? What is their role for stabilizing the environment of the dog’s? are they found in certain types and not in others? Does this make the dog’s mouth unhealthy to humans?
2.17. Some animals and their microbiome: 
Gilbert C, Meik JM, Dashevsky D, Card DC, Castoe TA, Schaack S. have found: Numerous viruses have been characterized in reptiles, with at least 11 families documented in snakes. However, hepadnaviruses, bornaviruses, and circoviruses have not been reported in this group, with the exception of a truncated bornavirus nucleoprotein transcript (94 amino acids) identified in a Gaboon viper transcriptome and a TBLASTN hit corresponding to a python endogenous bornavirus-like nucleoprotein (EBLN3) noted in a supplementary table by Horie et al. To date, bornaviruses and hepadnaviruses have never been observed in non-avian reptiles, and only a single member of the Circoviridae has been identified in a non-avian reptile, specifically a green sea turtle. Nevertheless, the multiple endogenization events documented across various taxa in this study indicate that snakes have been repeatedly exposed to divergent members of these three viral families over the past 50 million years. Although these findings do not suggest that snakes are currently interacting with these viruses, they provide evidence of historical interactions. Collectively, these results underscore the value of paleo virology in elucidating past host–virus dynamics, formulating and testing hypotheses concerning host-switching events, and informing the selection of plausible host taxa for future surveillance of circulating viruses. In addition to identifying previously undocumented endogenous viruses in snakes, the phylogenetic analysis reveals a notable lack of congruence between host and viral phylogenies, suggesting that these three viral families have likely undergone one or more cross-species transmission events between mammals and reptiles in the past. 
Smith SN, Colston TJ, Siler CD et al have constructed:  Although progress has gradually been made in understanding microbial variation across distinct anatomical sites within the reptilian gastrointestinal tract, knowledge regarding the composition, diversity, and functional attributes of snake microbiomes remains substantially limited. Establishing a foundational understanding of venomous snake microbiomes, particularly with respect to regional anatomical associations (e.g., gastrointestinal tract versus venom gland), will be essential for elucidating the potential roles that host-specific microbiomes may have played in the adaptive evolution of venomous snakes. Furthermore, while 16S rRNA gene amplicon sequencing often serves as an initial approach for identifying compositional patterns among host-associated bacterial communities, the application of alternative genomic technologies—such as shotgun metagenomic sequencing—offers promising avenues for characterizing the functional potential of these microbiomes and their interactions with the host. By presenting the findings of this comparative study across three host species, each representing a distinct snake family (Colubridae, Elapidae, Viperidae), we aim to address this knowledge gap for one of the most ecologically diverse and species-rich vertebrate lineages on Earth. 
Mims TS, Abdallah QA, Stewart JD, Watts SP, White CT, Rousselle TV, Gosain A, Bajwa A, Han JC, Willis KA, Pierre JF. have found: In this study, we examine whether variations in the abundance and composition of the gut mycobiome correlate with key features of host metabolism. To assess whether distinct initial mycobiomes influence host adaptation to processed diets, we obtained animals from four commercial sources. Our findings indicate that the gut mycobiome of healthy mice is shaped by environmental factors, including diet, and is significantly associated with metabolic outcomes. Exposure to processed diets results in persistent alterations in fungal communities, which in male mice correlate with differential body mass accumulation compared to those fed a standardized diet. Hepatic fat deposition, transcriptional changes in metabolically active tissues, and serum metabolic biomarker levels are all linked to shifts in fungal community diversity and composition. Notably, variation in fungi from the genera Thermomyces and Saccharomyces shows the strongest association with metabolic disturbance and weight gain. 
Bolaños LM, Rosenblueth M, Castillo-Ramírez S, Figuier-Huttin G, Martínez-Romero E. have discovered: Scorpions are regarded as "living fossils" that have retained ancestral morphological characteristics while successfully adapting to a wide range of habitats. Despite this, the diversity of their gut microbiota remains unexplored. In this study, we characterized the gut microbiota of two scorpion species, Vaejovis smithi and Centruroides limpidus. Our findings demonstrate that the gut microbiota of scorpions is species-specific and that food deprivation leads to a reduction in bacterial diversity. Phylogenetic analysis of 16S rRNA gene sequences revealed novel bacterial lineages exhibiting low sequence identity to any known bacteria. Moreover, these novel lineages were found to be exclusive to each scorpion species. Predicted metagenomic profiling further identified a core set of metabolic pathways that were highly abundant in both species, predominantly associated with amino acid, carbohydrate, vitamin, and cofactor metabolism. 
Here it is discovered that there are microbiomes plus/minus virome and fungi inside reptilian animals, mice, scorpions specially and snakes.
2.17.1. Reshaping the Snake Gut: Feast, Famine, and Venom
Snake microbiomes are among the most "plastic" in the animal kingdom because of their extreme feeding ecology (long fasts followed by massive meals).
a. The Metabolic "Switch"
When a python or viper eats a meal that can be 50% of its own body weight, the gut environment undergoes a violent transformation.
1.The Bloom: Firmicutes and Digestion: Snakes, such as pythons, undergo extreme physiological shifts when feeding after long periods of fasting.  . During fasting, the gut is mostly dormant and dominated by Bacteroidetes. However, within 12 to 48 hours of ingesting a large meal, the gut undergoes massive morphological hypertrophy, and the microbial load explodes. Firmicutes (specifically from the class Clostridia and family Peptostreptococcaceae) rapidly outcompete other bacteria. They are specifically adapted to ferment the dense influx of complex proteins, amino acids, and lipids from the prey's muscle, bone, and scales. (Costello et al., 2010). 
2. The extreme pH drop is a hallmark of snake digestion. A fasting snake maintains a near-neutral stomach pH (around 7.0 to 7.5). Upon swallowing prey, gastric acid production is massively upregulated, plunging the stomach pH down to 1.0–2.0 within a day. This highly acidic bath is primarily designed for the chemical breakdown and decalcification of the prey's skeleton. Microbiologically, this acts as a severe selective bottleneck. Only highly acid-tolerant bacterial extremophiles can survive this gastric transit to initiate colonization and form early digestive biofilms on the surface of the prey before it reaches the small intestine. 
3.The concept of the oral/venom microbiome acting as a biological weapon is an exciting frontier in toxinology. Researchers (like Smith et al.) have demonstrated that a snake's oral cavity and venom glands harbor a distinct microbiota separate from the gut, often containing pathogenic bacteria like Enterococcus, Pseudomonas, and Clostridium. When a snake strikes, these microbes are inoculated deep into the puncture wound alongside the venom. Instead of just being passive hitchhikers, it is theorized that these bacteria act synergistically with venom metalloproteinases to rapidly break down local tissue. This exacerbates the necrotic (tissue-rotting) effect of the bite, essentially initiating the digestion process from the inside out before the snake even swallows the prey. 
b. Survival and Transfer: From Reptiles to Humans
Palaeobiology and Viral "Jumps"
For millions of years, certain viruses have achieved deep evolutionary persistence through endogenization. When an ancient bornavirus infected a host (like a snake's ancestor), segments of its viral RNA were reverse-transcribed and integrated directly into the host’s germline DNA (sperm or egg cells). These integrated sequences, known as Endogenous Viral Elements (EVEs), were then passed down vertically from parent to offspring. They act as "molecular fossils," allowing the viral genetic code to hide and survive across geological timescales without needing to actively infect new hosts.
The Gilbert et al. (2014) paper proved that the evolutionary history of snakes involves multiple viral host-switches between reptiles and mammals. For a zoonotic spillover to occur today, a virus must exhibit extreme plasticity, specifically by mutating its viral envelope glycoproteins to successfully bind to homologous cellular receptors in the new human host. The discovery of these fossilized, mammalian-like bornaviruses embedded in modern snake genomes is highly significant; it proves that the cellular "lock and key" mechanisms between reptiles and mammals have historically overlapped, making the zoonotic bridge much shorter than previously thought. (Gilbert et al., 2014) 
2.17.2. Conclusion: 
In this paper arguing for the multicellularity of biofilms, the scorpion gut (from the Bolaños paper) provides a "clean" example. Because scorpions have novel bacterial lineages restricted to specific species, it suggests that these bacteria have evolved a highly "social" and specialized multicellular structure that is perfectly tuned to the scorpion's unique internal environment. This "niche specialization" is a hallmark of multicellular evolution.
2.18. Microbiome of donkeys: 
Câmara RJF. Et al have discovered: Donkeys (Equus asinus) and mules constitute approximately half of the global domestic equine population and serve a vital role in the livelihoods of countless people, particularly in developing nations. Despite their significance, these animals are currently considered neglected and threatened due to factors such as abandonment, indiscriminate slaughter, and inadequate sanitary management. Specific knowledge regarding the infectious viral diseases affecting this group of Equidae remains limited. In many instances, donkeys and mules are managed similarly to horses, with the physiological distinctions among these species often overlooked. The majority of infectious diseases affecting the Equidae family are confined to this family and carry substantial economic consequences for the equine industry. However, certain viruses have the potential to cross the species barrier and infect humans, thereby posing a significant risk to public health.  
Liu G, et al has noted: The gastrointestinal microbiota exerts a significant influence on the nutrition and health of monogastric herbivores, including donkeys. Nevertheless, the microbiota across different gastrointestinal compartments of healthy donkeys has yet to be characterized. In this study, we investigated the abundance and functional capacity of microbiota at various sites along the gastrointestinal tract (GIT)—including the foregut (stomach, duodenum, jejunum, and ileum) and hindgut (cecum, ventral colon, dorsal colon, and rectum)—of healthy adult donkeys, primarily using 16S rRNA gene sequencing and PICRUSt (Phylogenetic Investigation of Communities by Reconstruction of Unobserved States) analysis.
Collectively, our findings reveal that donkeys harbor a rich, diverse, and multifunctional microbiota throughout the GIT. Overall, microbial richness and diversity were substantially higher in the hindgut compared to the foregut. At the phylum level, Firmicutes predominated in the foregut, whereas both Firmicutes and Bacteroidetes were abundant in the hindgut. At the genus level, Lactobacillus was dominant in the foregut, while Streptococcus was more prevalent in the hindgut. PICRUSt analysis further demonstrated that the microbial communities along the GIT were functionally aligned with the distinct physiological roles of each compartment. For instance, microbes in the foregut exhibited greater activity in carbohydrate metabolism, whereas those in the hindgut showed enhanced activity in amino acid metabolism.
This study represents the first characterization of the donkey digestive system with respect to microbial composition and function, providing foundational data on the healthy gastrointestinal microbiota of donkeys. These findings may serve as a reference for evaluating donkey health and offer valuable insights for future investigations into digestive physiology, nutrition, and the development of microbial supplements for this species. 
These research and papers denote that the guts of the donkeys are inhabited by microbiota. 
Conclusion:
In this paper on bacterial multicellularity, it is noted that the donkey's hindgut serves as an excellent example of division of labour. The bacteria residing in the cecum are not merely a collection of individual cells; rather, they are thought to function as a cohesive organ. If they did not operate as a multicellular unit—communicating with one another to regulate the initiation and cessation of fermentation—it is suggested that the donkey would be at risk of fatal bloating or acidosis. This process of quorum sensing is considered a fundamental component of the multicellular argument being developed.
2.19. Do biofilms have embryo like stages?
Futo M, Opašić L, Koska S, Čorak N, Široki T, Ravikumar V, Thorsell A, Lenuzzi M, Kifer D, Domazet-Lošo M, Vlahoviček K, Mijakovic I, Domazet-Lošo T. have found: Using time-resolved transcriptomic and proteomic profiling, the study found that biofilm ontogeny—or development—correlates with evolutionary measures, such that evolutionarily younger and more divergent genes were increasingly expressed at later stages of biofilm growth. Molecular and morphological signatures further revealed that biofilm growth is a highly regulated process organized into discrete ontogenetic stages, resembling those observed in eukaryotic embryos. Collectively, these findings suggest that biofilm formation in Bacillus constitutes a genuine developmental process, comparable to organismal development in animals, plants, and fungi. Given that the majority of cells on Earth exist within biofilms and that biofilms represent the oldest known fossils, it is proposed that the conventional view of early life as consisting of single-celled, free-living organisms warrants reconsideration. 
Chatterjee S, Das S.: Fungal biofilms are ubiquitous in natural environments. Beyond biomass, the primary structural component of a fungal biofilm is the extracellular matrix (ECM), within which fungal hyphae are embedded. Key physical properties of biofilms—including attachment, mechanical stability, and antibiotic resistance—are attributed to the ECM. This study investigated the stages of biofilm formation in the filamentous manglicolous fungus Aspergillus niger BSC-1. Spectroscopic analysis revealed that biofilm formation increased significantly (p < .0001) with incubation time up to 36 hours. Scanning electron microscopy and confocal microscopy demonstrated that biofilm development progressed through six distinct stages: (a) adsorption, (b) active attachment, (c) germling and monolayer formation, (d) hyphal development and ECM production, (e) ECM maturation, and (f) spore dispersal. At the maturation stage, biofilm thickness reached approximately 15 μm. Approximately 8.1 mg of ECM material was extracted from 20 mL of broth culture using ethanol precipitation. 
The authors of the above paper suggest and have observed developmental stages of biofilm growth the same or close to what other mammals have.
The papers provided above  are seminal works in what is increasingly known as "The Multicellular Turn" in microbiology. The work by Futo et al. is especially groundbreaking because it uses phylostratigraphy (tracing the evolutionary age of genes) to show that a biofilm follows the same "hourglass" pattern of development as a vertebrate embryo. This suggests that the "free-living single cell" may be an artifact of laboratory culture, and that the "true" natural state of these organisms is a multicellular life cycle.
Here are more examples and a detailed elaboration on how these developmental stages and molecular signatures support the multicellularity of biofilms.
2.19. 1. The Phylostratigraphic Evidence (The "Hourglass" Model)
In animal development, embryos go through a "phylotypic stage" where they most resemble other species in their phylum. During this stage, evolutionarily ancient genes are expressed.
Futo et al.'s Discovery: In Bacillus subtilis biofilms, the team found that as the biofilm "ages," it expresses increasingly younger genes. This ontogenetic succession—moving from ancient "housekeeping" genes to newer, specialized "regulatory" genes—is the exact same pattern seen in the development of a fruit fly or a human. This favors multicellularity: If a biofilm were just a random pile of cells, gene expression would be chaotic or purely reactive to the environment. Instead, it follows an internalized clock. This pre-programmed "instruction manual" for building a structure is a hallmark of multicellular organisms.
If biofilms are "bona fide developmental processes," as Futo et al. suggest, then the "first life" was likely not a lone cell in the ocean, but a multicellular mat.
The Stromatolites (fossilized biofilms) from 3.5 billion years ago are not just piles of bacteria; they are the remains of complex, organized "cities" that had to coordinate their growth to survive the harsh UV radiation and chemical environment of early Earth.
2.19.2. conclusion: 
When it is been argued for the multicellularity of biofilms, then essentially the argument is that the biofilm is the organism, and the planktonic cell is merely a "seed" or "gamete"—a temporary, mobile state used to move from one "multicellular body" to the next.
If biofilms were multicellular: if we stop one stage of development, can we stop the whole epidemics? The introduction of different genes within certain stage, would it render them more beneficial or less harmful? If taking in the consideration of viromes are they closely related to these stages of biofilm development?
2.20. different sizes of biofilms: 
When the discussion is about the "size" of a biofilm, it’s helpful to distinguish between the thickness of the microbial "slime" and the geographic scale of the entire cooperative structure. Biofilms can range from microscopic layers on a tooth to massive, "organism-like" mats that cover kilometres of the ocean floor.
Here are the biggest examples of biofilms ever recorded:
2.20.1. Deep-Sea Microbial Mats (The "Kilometer" Scale)
The concept of biofilm multicellularity extends well beyond microscopic host-associated environments, culminating in the largest continuous biofilms on Earth, which are found across the ocean floor. Thriving in extreme habitats such as hydrothermal vents, cold seeps, and the highly productive continental shelves off Namibia and Peru, giant filamentous sulfur bacteria—predominantly from the genus Beggiatoa—form extensive benthic mats (Schulz et al., 1999) . These structures can reach thicknesses of several centimeters and cover areas ranging from hundreds of square meters to kilometers across the seafloor (Teske & Salman, 2014) . Rather than functioning as isolated organisms, the bacteria within these macroscopic mats operate as a highly coordinated, unified metabolic unit. By positioning themselves precisely at the oxic-anoxic interface, the biofilm physically bridges the gap between the oxygen-rich (or nitrate-rich) water column above and the toxic, hydrogen sulfide-producing sediment below, thereby efficiently driving biogeochemical cycling in these regions (Jørgensen, 2010) .
2.20.2. Snottites and Cave Biofilms (The "Curtain" Scale)
In extreme subterranean environments, such as the sulfur-rich Cueva de Villa Luz in Mexico, biofilms develop highly specialized macroscopic formations colloquially referred to as "snottites." These pendant-like structures, which visually resemble mucus-based stalactites, are composed of dense matrices of sulfur-oxidizing bacteria and can grow to exceed one meter in length (Macalady et al., 2007) . Maintaining a structure of this scale requires a highly organized internal architecture. Analogous to the circulatory system development observed in early embryonic stages, these biofilms employ complex internal microchannels to efficiently transport atmospheric nutrients inward while facilitating the outward removal of metabolic waste (Hose et al., 2000) . The metabolic activity of this coordinated community is exceptionally potent; the bacteria convert hydrogen sulfide into concentrated sulfuric acid, producing drips with a pH ranging from 0 to 1. This highly corrosive waste actively dissolves the surrounding limestone bedrock, meaning that these multicellular biofilms effectively carve and expand the cave system over time through biogenic speleogenesis (Hose et al., 2000 ; Macalady et al., 2007 ).
2.20.3. Giant Single-Cell Colonies: Thiomargarita magnifica
The recent discovery of Thiomargarita magnifica in the sulfur-rich mangroves of Guadeloupe represents a fundamental paradigm shift in our understanding of bacterial biology, aggressively pushing the boundaries of what constitutes a single cell. While technically a solitary prokaryote, T. magnifica routinely grows up to a centimeter in length—making it visible to the naked eye—and forms massive, string-like filamentous structures on sunken leaves (Volland et al., 2022) . This organism demonstrates that the architectural principles observed in dense biofilms can allow individual cells to reach macroscopic proportions. Most remarkably, T. magnifica challenges the foundational divide between prokaryotes and eukaryotes through its internal architecture. The cell contains complex, membrane-bound structures termed "pepins" that actively compartmentalize its DNA and ribosomes (Volland et al., 2022) . This advanced level of genomic and metabolic organization, a feature historically reserved exclusively for complex multicellular eukaryotes, perfectly illustrates the profound structural plasticity and evolutionary potential of bacterial life.
2.20.4. Conclusion: 
The observation that these biofilms can attain scales of meters or even kilometers lend support to the argument that they should not be regarded merely as clusters of individual cells, but rather as macroscopic multicellular organisms. In the same way that a whale consists of coordinated cells functioning as a unified organism, a kilometer-long Beggiatoa mat represents a collection of coordinated bacteria operating collectively as a single environmental "organ."
2.21. chapter summary: 
The characteristics shared in this chapter were the following: Their bodily cells and the Extra cellular matrix are transparent and can be seen in the range of the UV light and by some animals. Moreover, their bodies can produce soundwaves and sense them at the same time. Furthermore, their cells and growth can be affected by sound waves (both negatively and positively) and communicate between each other. In addition, these can sense pain and light which lead to the fact that they have organs for sensation and for other bodily function which are controlled by networks higher functional system like brain and heart (more suitable to their nature).  Also, they have mechanical appendages that can be used for food (the same as human hands). What is most amazing is that viruses are part of some biofilms (not all) and exterior appendages like human hairs. However, these viruses can be used as weapons against other biofilms and their animals’ biofilms (fungi, protozoa, etc.……. other micro-organisms) in the same way these latter can destroy viruses. Besides, these creatures have long existed in this form and with these features before the humans existed. Also, their life spans are long and may reach few thousands of years. The same as humans’ systems follow a circadian rhythm their systems strongly follow the same but with peak activity in the night. Additionally, a 3d structure is observed among these creatures which can take many shapes as the incarnate many mammalians and reptiles. Also, their cells and those of their animals take part in the production of alcohol. All, the cells in one biofilm structure can move together. Lastly, it appears that these structures have stages of development the same as embryonic stages. 

Communication and Sensory Systems
Biofilms demonstrate sophisticated communication through sound, electrical signals, and mechanical sensation. Bacillus subtilis produces audible and ultrasonic sound waves (8–43 kHz) that influence distant bacterial populations even through physical barriers, indicating collective mechanisms beyond individual cell capacity (Matsuhashi et al., 1998). Electrical signaling via potassium ion channels coordinates metabolic activity across biofilms, enabling nutrient-starved interior cells to temporarily arrest peripheral growth for resource redistribution (Prindle et al., 2015).
Mechanosensation occurs through specialized structures including flagella, pili, and PilY1 proteins, allowing detection of surface contact and mechanical pressure, with biofilms exhibiting structural plasticity in response to physical stimuli (Dufrêne & Persat, 2020). Photoreception capabilities include cyanobacteria functioning as microlenses that focus light to direct motility, while whole biofilms demonstrate coordinated metabolic and virulence responses to light gradients.
Structural Organization and Division of Labor
The extracellular matrix (EPS) functions as dynamic material with viscoelastic properties that transition between gel-like and glass-like states, providing mechanical protection and enabling complex three-dimensional architecture. Cellular specialization occurs within biofilms: peripheral cells serve as active sentinels while interior cells enter dormant states as community reservoirs. In the donkey hindgut, bacteria operate as a cohesive organ through quorum sensing, preventing fatal bloating or acidosis—a clear example of division of labor (Liu et al., 2018).
Developmental Programs
Biofilm formation proceeds through discrete ontogenetic stages analogous to embryonic development. Time-resolved transcriptomic profiling reveals that through time younger genes are increasingly expressed during later biofilm growth stages, following the same pattern observed in animal development (Futo et al., 2021). Fungal biofilms exhibit defined stages including adsorption, attachment, hyphal development, matrix maturation, and spore dispersal (Chatterjee & Das, 2019).
Ecological Plasticity and Environmental Adaptation
Biofilms inhabit diverse environments from deep oceans to atmosphere and soil. Deep-sea Beggiatoa mats extend over kilometers as unified metabolic units bridging oxic-anoxic interfaces (Schulz et al., 1999). Cave "snottites" exceed one meter in length, employing internal microchannels for transport while actively carving their environment through acid production (Macalady et al., 2007). Canine gut biofilms transition from planktonic to biofilm states to withstand gastric acidity and immune pressures, demonstrating adaptive structural reorganization (Gallina et al., 2024).
Temporal Organization and Longevity
Biofilms exhibit endogenous circadian rhythms, with non-photosynthetic bacteria maintaining 24-hour gene expression cycles synchronized by temperature fluctuations (Eelderink-Chen et al., 2021). Many display nocturnal activity patterns, timing nitrogen fixation and spore release to nighttime hours. Biofilms demonstrate extraordinary longevity, with viable bacteria recovered from samples hundreds of thousands to millions of years old through dormancy and EPS-mediated preservation.
Viral and Fungal Integration
Viruses function as integrated biofilm components rather than mere pathogens. Filamentous phages reinforce matrix structure, while viral particles embedded within biofilms serve as defensive weapons against competitors. Fungi form structured biofilms and engage in complex, non-random interactions with bacterial biofilms characterized by recurring mutualistic or antagonistic patterns.
Implications for Multicellularity
The cumulative evidence—coordinated communication, sensory capabilities, structural specialization, programmed development, ecological plasticity, and temporal organization—supports reconceptualizing biofilms as multicellular entities. The biofilm represents the primary unit of bacterial life, with planktonic cells serving as transient dispersal stages analogous to seeds or gametes in higher organisms.
 
3. Chapter two: the attached biofilms to a human being and their impacts on the attached human physiology: 
3.1. Human’s attached microbiota dates back to?
Dunn Robert R., Amato Katherine R., Archie Elizabeth A., have found:  Ultimately, what emerges regarding the potential influence of microbes on hominin existence and adaptation is only a preliminary sketch—one that remains subject to revision and is based on observable contemporary phenomena. We conclude by exploring, albeit more speculatively, possibilities for microbial influence. Specifically, we consider whether certain microbes associated with hominin microbiomes—whether in the stomach, intestines, skin, the extended environment, or sites such as breast milk and the vaginal tract, the latter of which exhibits substantial variation across primate species (Miller et al., 2016)—may have directly favored specific forms of social behavior. In doing so, they might account for two major social transitions in hominins: the shift toward larger, more sedentary populations and the transition to urban living. As previously noted, human management of microbial populations likely facilitated such transitions, for instance through food preservation and the fermentation of potable beverages from contaminated water. Conversely, could microbes have exerted control over human behavior? Recent research demonstrates that certain microbes influence host social behavior in ways that enhance microbial fitness. The eukaryote Salpingoeca rosetta, for example, can adopt either a solitary or a multicellular lifestyle, with this transition mediated by lipids produced by its bacterial commensal, Algoriphagus machipongonensis (Woznica et al., 2016). In other words, the metabolic products of this eukaryote's microbiome determine its sociality. One might argue that the proliferation of high-alcohol-producing yeast strains represents a relatively recent instance of such a dynamic—where alcohol-producing yeasts contribute to addiction, socially disinhibited interactions, and increased demand for their fermentation products. However, the possibility of far more ancient microbial influences on human social behavior cannot yet be ruled out. 
Davenport ER et al. have found:  While comparing non-Western and Western microbiomes provides valuable insights into how modern diets and medical practices shape microbial composition, no contemporary human population possesses a truly "ancestral" microbiome. Instead, ancient samples—including coprolites, dental calculus, permafrost-preserved tissue, and mummified remains—offer a window into the microbiomes that existed throughout hominin existence and adaptaion.
Coprolites and mummified intestinal contents provide the most direct snapshots of ancient gut microbiomes; however, these samples often preserve poorly, and not all profiled specimens contain what are recognized as gut microbes. Dental calculus, in contrast, preserves remarkably well. Analysis of samples spanning from 7,000 years ago to the present day reveals that oral microbiome diversity declined with the advent of agriculture. Furthermore, ancient oral microbiomes harbor known opportunistic pathogens and, intriguingly, putative antibiotic resistance genes.
Recent sequencing of Neanderthal dental calculus from two caves demonstrated that oral microbiomes varied according to inferred meat consumption patterns at each site. The microbiota of specimens interpreted as meat-eaters closely resembled those of modern hunter-gatherer populations, whereas non-meat-eating specimens more closely aligned with those of foraging chimpanzees. Given the rapid expansion of ancient DNA research, our understanding of prehistoric human microbiomes is likely to advance considerably in the coming years. 
These research and reviews refer to the fact that microbiomes were found even in ancient human beings. ( gemini… 
Here is a breakdown of further research and scientific elaboration supporting the deep ancestral ties between humans and our microbiota.
3.1.1.  Tracking Ancient Human Migration via Microbes (Helicobacter pylori)
In certain contexts, the genetic mutations embedded within our microbiomes can chronicle early human history with greater precision than the fossil record. A profound example of this is Helicobacter pylori, a bacterium that extensively colonizes the human gastric mucosa. A landmark global genetic analysis by Linz et al. (2007)  demonstrated that the genetic diversity of H. pylori strains decreases incrementally the further populations are located from East Africa. This pattern precisely mirrors the sequential genetic bottlenecks, or founder effects, observed in human genomic data during ancient global dispersal. Ultimately, this phylogenetic mapping indicates that H. pylori has maintained a continuous, intimate association with its human hosts for at least 100,000 years, proving that these microbes actively accompanied early modern humans during their foundational migrations out of Africa approximately 60,000 years ago (Linz et al., 2007)  .
3.1.2. Direct Evidence from Ancient DNA: Coprolites (Paleofeces)
While comparative genomic studies of modern microbiomes allow researchers to infer our changing past, the sequencing of DNA from ancient feces (paleofeces) provides direct, physical proof of ancestral human biology. In a landmark study, Wibowo et al. (2021) sequenced desiccated paleofeces from dry caves in the Americas—dating back up to 2,000 years—to successfully reconstruct dozens of ancient microbial genomes. Their analysis revealed that early humans harbored a highly diverse gut microbiome characterized by species such as Treponema succinifaciens. The trajectory of this specific bacterium highlights a profound modern shift: while T. succinifaciens remains prevalent in wild primates and modern traditional hunter-gatherer societies (like the Hadza), it is virtually extinct within industrialized, Western populations. This physical fossil record confirms that these complex microbial communities were integral companions to the human gut for millennia, only recently being eradicated by the advent of modern processed diets, rigorous sanitation practices, and widespread antibiotic use (Wibowo et al., 2021) .
3.1.3. Deep-Time Oral Microbiomes: Neanderthals and Early Hominins
The preservation of ancient microbial DNA within fossilized biofilms offers unique insights into the evolutionary history of early hominids. In a landmark study, researchers analyzed ancient dental calculus—calcified oral plaque—from Neanderthals and successfully reconstructed the genome of Methanobrevibacter oralis, a prominent oral archaeon (Weyrich et al., 2017). By comparing the Neanderthal strain to modern human variants, geneticists determined that the two lineages diverged between 112,000 and 143,000 years ago. Notably, this timeline aligns precisely with known periods of interbreeding between Neanderthals and early Homo sapiens. Since M. oralis is exclusively an oral microbe, this genetic overlap provides compelling evidence that these distinct human lineages were directly exchanging oral microbes—likely through intimate behaviors such as kissing or shared food—hundreds of thousands of years ago (Weyrich et al., 2017) .
3.1.4. Summary and conclusion: 
The scientific consensus is that the human microbiome is practically an "extended organ" that has been subject to natural selection alongside our own cells. The microbiota closely related to humans today did not just adapt to us recently; its ancestors were inhabiting the guts and mouths of early Homo sapiens, Neanderthals, and Australopithecus millions of years ago.) the fact that these biofilms can alter and affect the behaviour of humans this mean that they have been an active part of the human rules and manners. (passively)
3.2. The Human gut microbiota before, during and after birth
Ventura M, Milani C, Lugli GA, van Sinderen D.  have reviewed: The establishment of the infant gut microbiota is a complex process shaped by numerous environmental and host factors (Rodriguez et al., 2015). Although the prevailing dogma maintains that the in utero environment is sterile and that microbial colonization begins only at birth, accumulating evidence suggests that early colonization may occur during the fetal stage (Jimenez et al., 2005; DiGiulio et al., 2008; Aagaard et al., 2014). The mechanism by which bacteria achieve this prenatal colonization remains unclear. However, the recent discovery of a circulating microbiota in human blood (Kowarsky et al., 2017) reinforces the notion that no human organ is entirely sterile and lends support to the hypothesis of prenatal gut microbiota establishment. This perspective may open new research avenues and enable potential manipulation of the gut microbiota through probiotic intervention during pregnancy.
Additionally, factors such as diet, maternal age and metabolic status, family genetics, and lifestyle have been shown to influence infant gut microbiota composition (Milani et al., 2017). The origin of the human gut microbiota is closely tied to the vertical transmission of microbial assemblages from mother to offspring. Notably, the mammalian newborn does not simply acquire a microbiota passively; rather, the mother appears to actively promote the transfer of specific microbial members to her offspring, thereby directly influencing the development of her child's nascent microbiota. 
Shao, Y., Forster, S.C., Tsaliki, E. et al. have suggested the following: Immediately following birth, newborns undergo rapid microbial colonization from their mothers and the surrounding environment. Perturbations to this colonization process during infancy may mediate the development of diseases in childhood and later life. However, the effects of cesarean section delivery on the earliest stages of gut microbiota acquisition and development—specifically during the neonatal period (≤1 month)—remain a subject of debate.
In a large-scale study, researchers applied longitudinal sampling and whole-genome shotgun metagenomic analysis to 1,679 gut microbiota samples collected at multiple time points during the neonatal period and infancy from 596 full-term babies born in UK hospitals. For a subset of these infants, additional matched samples were obtained from mothers (175 mothers paired with 178 babies). The analysis revealed that delivery mode significantly influences gut microbiota composition throughout the neonatal period and into infancy.
Matched large-scale culturing and whole-genome sequencing of over 800 bacterial strains isolated from these infants identified virulence factors and clinically relevant antimicrobial resistance genes within opportunistic pathogens, potentially predisposing individuals to opportunistic infections. These findings underscore the critical role of the local environment in establishing the gut microbiota during very early life and identify colonization with antimicrobial-resistant opportunistic pathogens as a previously underappreciated risk factor associated with hospital births. 
Sanger.ac.uk website has posted: Research has demonstrated that infants born vaginally possess distinct gut bacterial communities—their microbiomes—compared to those delivered by cesarean section. Scientists from the Wellcome Sanger Institute, University College London, the University of Birmingham, and their collaborators found that while vaginally delivered infants acquired the majority of their gut bacteria from their mothers, cesarean-born infants did not. Instead, the latter harbored greater proportions of bacteria typically associated with hospital environments within their guts. 
Mutic AD, Jordan S, Edwards SM, Ferranti EP, Thul TA, Yang I. have mentioned: Biological and environmental changes affecting maternal and newborn microbiomes during the postnatal period can significantly influence health outcomes for the mother–baby dyad. Postpartum sleep deprivation and inadequate dietary intake may alter commensal bacterial populations and disrupt gut–brain communication. Additionally, perineal injury and breast infections can shift microbial community composition, creating conditions that favor pathogen proliferation. The gut microbiome—comprising microorganisms that function in harmony—when disrupted, along with impaired gut–brain communication, may contribute to postpartum depression, a potentially serious complication.
In newborns, changes to the gut and skin microbiomes occur rapidly after birth and are strongly influenced by delivery mode, feeding method, and bathing and skin care practices. During this period, infant microbiomes are highly vulnerable and susceptible to a range of influences. Maternal–newborn nurses play a valuable role in supporting mothers and infants in promoting healthy microbiome development. This text presents the factors affecting the rapidly evolving postnatal microbiomes of mothers and their newborns, along with the role nurses can play in positively influencing both immediate and long-term health outcomes. 
Reyman, M., van Houten, M.A., van Baarle, D. et al.  have revealed: The early-life microbiome appears to be influenced by delivery mode, though this effect may be confounded by intrapartum antibiotic exposure. In this study, researchers assessed the impact of delivery mode on gut microbiota independent of intrapartum antibiotics by delaying routine antibiotic administration to mothers until after cord clamping in 74 vaginally delivered and 46 cesarean-section-born infants.
Results showed that microbiota composition differed between cesarean-born and vaginally delivered infants throughout the first year of life. Vaginally delivered infants exhibited enrichment of Bifidobacterium species alongside reductions in Enterococcus and Klebsiella species. Microbiota composition at one week of age was associated with the number of respiratory infections occurring over the first year. The bacterial taxa driving this association were more abundant in cesarean-born children, suggesting a potential link between delivery mode and susceptibility to infectious outcomes. 
All the above-mentioned papers indicate the fact that the dwelling human microbiota are definitely found around the time of birth.

The development of the human microbiome is a dynamic, lifelong process that is deeply intertwined with our physiological development, immune system calibration, and overall health. 3.3. Does Human blood microbiome present?  
It is evident to everyone that bacteria can be found in blood of humans with sepsis.
Marcia A. Ryder (2005) has written:” Nearly all microorganisms exist within complex colonies encased in self-produced exopolymer matrices known as biofilms. These biofilms enable microorganisms to adhere to virtually any surface, whether living or nonliving. Adaptive and genetic alterations occurring within biofilm-embedded microorganisms confer resistance to all known antimicrobial agents. Consequently, the diagnostic and therapeutic approaches employed against acute infections prove ineffective in eradicating biofilm-associated infections related to medical devices or chronic biofilm diseases. Currently, vascular catheter-related bloodstream infections represent the most serious and costly healthcare-associated infections. 
 
Image. 14. Biofilm inside catheters . image taken from Marcia A. Ryder, Catheter-Related Infections: It's All About Biofilm
Richard W. McLaughlin et al (2002) authors say: During the search for spirochetes associated with Alzheimer's disease, pleomorphic bacteria were observed in the blood of healthy human subjects using dark-field microscopy. This finding was unexpected, as the bloodstream in healthy humans is generally considered sterile except when tissue membrane integrity is compromised. However, the concept of bacteria residing in the blood of healthy individuals has become more plausible due to the advent of cultivation-independent laboratory approaches.
The report of naturally occurring viable pleomorphic bacteria in the blood of healthy humans should not be viewed as controversial. In 1969, G. Tedeshi and colleagues at the University of Camerino in Italy published findings in Nature describing similar bacteria as intraerythrocytic parasites in clinically healthy individuals. Their research demonstrated that the presence of these bacteria enhanced the uptake of radioactive thymine, uridine, and glycine by red blood cells—processes not part of normal erythrocyte metabolism. More recently, the detection of bacterial 16S rDNA in the blood of healthy subjects by Nikkari and colleagues further substantiates these observations. 
CASTILLO DIEGO J. et al 2019 Say : Evidence supporting the presence of a microbial component in the blood of healthy individuals is steadily accumulating, with numerous studies identifying comparable bacterial phyla in healthy human blood (McLaughlin et al., 2002; Amar et al., 2011, 2013; Dinakaran et al., 2014; Gosiewski et al., 2016; Païssé et al., 2016; Li et al., 2018; Loohuis et al., 2018; Whittle et al., 2018; Qiu et al., 2019). While each of these studies has individual limitations, a coherent picture of the healthy blood microbiome emerges when considering the methodological strengths contributed by each. These include the incorporation of microscopy (McLaughlin et al., 2002) and the application of both DNA-based (Dinakaran et al., 2014; Païssé et al., 2016; Li et al., 2018; Whittle et al., 2018) and RNA-based analyses (Loohuis et al., 2018; Whittle et al., 2018) to blood samples from healthy individuals.
Based on the literature reviewed and considering the growing trend toward incorporating analytical controls—both positive and negative—in human blood microbiome studies, it is concluded that the existence of a healthy (non-diseased) human blood microbiome cannot simply be dismissed.  
Conclusion and summary:

3.4. ear canal microbiome: 
3.4.1. The composition of earwax and ear bacteria? 
Bortz JT, Wertz PW, Downing DT (1990): Researchers extracted lipids from fresh cerumen and used quantitative thin-layer chromatography to analyze their composition. Lipids made up 52% of the cerumen’s dry weight. The lipid profile included squalene (6.4%), cholesterol esters (9.6%), wax esters (9.3%), triacylglycerols (3.0%), fatty acids (22.7%), cholesterol (20.9%), ceramides (18.6%), cholesterol sulfate (2.0%), and several unidentified polar components (7.5%). 
It is obvious that a portion of the ear wax is made from wax esters and cholesterol ester.
Barney BM, Wahlen BD, Garner E, Wei J, Seefeldt LC (2012): In some bacteria, wax esters serve as a reserve of carbon and energy. Their biosynthesis is completed by a bifunctional enzyme known as wax ester synthase/acyl-coenzyme A: diacylglycerol acyltransferase (WS/DGAT). This enzyme catalyzes the final step in the pathway by combining various fatty alcohols with fatty acyl-CoA molecules to form the corresponding wax esters. 
Hence, bacteria can produce wax esters which are one the composition of earwax. Also, most of the contents of the earwax are either materials that can be easily metabolized or produced as a by-product by the gut microbiota (plethora of research and evidence are present) like cholesterol esters.

Sjövall, Atte; Aho, Velma T.E; Hyyrynen, Taneli; Kinnari, Teemu J.; Auvinen, Petri; Silvola, Juha; Aarnisalo, Antti; Laulajainen-Hongisto, Anu, have illustrated: In the healthy external auditory canal (EAC), the most prevalent bacteria in the microbiome are Staphylococcus auricularis, Propionibacterium acnes, Alloiococcus otitis, and Turicella otitidis. The diversity of the EAC microbiota appears to be greater and more unique to each individual than earlier research suggested. Additionally, practices related to ear cleaning appear to influence the composition of the EAC microbiome. 
This paper refers to the fact that specific bacterial assemblage can always be found related to the external auditory canal.
Kociszewska D, Chan J, Thorne PR, Vlajkovic SM. Et. al have argued: Sensorineural hearing loss (SNHL) has a complex and multifaceted etiology, involving both congenital and acquired factors. Emerging research indicates that poor gut health may also contribute to its risk. Conditions such as inflammatory bowel disease (IBD), type 2 diabetes, diet-induced obesity (DIO), and high-fat diet (HFD) have all been associated with hearing impairment. Earlier studies have demonstrated that a high-fat diet can lead to microangiopathy, impaired insulin signaling, and oxidative stress within the inner ear. Additionally, HFD consumption can cause harmful alterations in gut microbiota and compromise intestinal barrier integrity, resulting in increased intestinal permeability—commonly known as leaky gut. This, in turn, can trigger chronic systemic inflammation, which may affect organs beyond the gastrointestinal tract. In this context, we propose that gut microbiota changes induced by chronic high-fat diet and diet-induced obesity may provoke a systemic inflammatory response capable of disrupting the blood–labyrinth barrier in the inner ear, thereby leading to cochlear inflammation and hearing loss. 
Alternatively, these results refer that a type of hearing loss is due to the byproducts of the dwelling bacteria (microbiota) in the colon.
3.4.2. Can the Gut Microbiome Contribute to Hearing Loss?
How Gut Bacteria Contribute to SNHL
1. Systemic Inflammation and the Leaky Gut The proposed mechanism involving short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs) and intestinal barrier integrity is strongly supported by existing literature. Dysbiosis, often induced by a high-fat diet or antibiotic use, has been associated with degradation of the intestinal barrier. Upon hyperpermeability of this barrier—commonly referred to as "leaky gut"—proliferation of Gram-negative bacteria that produce lipopolysaccharides (LPS) occurs. These endotoxins are subsequently released into the circulation, where a chronic, low-grade systemic immune response is triggered via toll-like receptors (Kociszewska et al., 2021). Additionally, it has been confirmed through research that the production of SCFAs by a healthy microbiome plays a critical role in attenuating such systemic inflammation and in maintaining adequate vascular supply (Godur et al., 2023) .
2. Compromising the Blood-Labyrinth Barrier (BLB): 
The comparison of the blood–labyrinth barrier to the blood–brain barrier has been framed by researchers as precisely illustrative of this vulnerability. The blood–labyrinth barrier is understood to be a semipermeable partition that serves to protect the highly sensitive endolymph and delicate structures of the inner ear. It is not, however, impervious to systemic distress. Systemic inflammation and circulating endotoxemia originating from gut dysbiosis have been shown to directly compromise the permeability of the blood–labyrinth barrier (Kociszewska et al., 2021) . 
3. Cochlear Damage: 
Once the blood–labyrinth barrier has been breached, the inner ear is rendered vulnerable to systemic threats. Bacterial metabolites and pro-inflammatory factors originating from the bloodstream are permitted to cross into the cochlea, where reactive oxygen species and pro-inflammatory cytokines are introduced in abundance (Godur et al., 2023) . This localized oxidative stress is associated with disruption of cellular homeostasis, which directly results in damage to and death of the sensory hair cells. These cells are responsible for the transduction of mechanical sound waves into electrical impulses; thus, their impairment is understood to drive sensorineural hearing loss (Guo et al., 2024) .
4.Age-Related and Autoimmune Hearing Loss: 
The connection to presbycusis and autoimmune inner ear disease (AIED) has been correctly noted. In the context of age-related hearing loss, this process is frequently referred to as "inflammaging"—a chronic, low-grade inflammatory state driven by a combination of immunosenescence, defined as the aging of the immune system, and age-dependent gut dysbiosis. This inflammaging has been shown to accelerate cochlear degeneration (Kociszewska & Vlajkovic, 2022) . With regard to autoimmune inner ear disease (AIED), although direct causal pathways linking specific bacterial taxa to the condition continue to be actively investigated, the prevailing theoretical framework aligns with the statement that systemic autoimmune responses—often involving circulating CD4+ T cells that mistakenly target inner ear self-antigens—are increasingly thought to be primed by molecular mimicry and dysbiosis originating in the gut microbiome.
3.4.3. Conclusion: 
The human body manufactures the raw materials of earwax, which the local ear microbiome then feeds upon and chemically modifies. Meanwhile, deep inside the body, the metabolic byproducts of the gut microbiome dictate the level of systemic inflammation, acting as a major hidden variable in whether the delicate structures of the inner ear remain healthy or degrade over time. 3.5. Nasal microbiota 
It is well established that there dwelling microbiota in the nasal cavities of humans. Furthermore, they are the cause or directly related to some diseases there.
Rawlings BA, Higgins TS, Han JK (2013) have written: According to a 2010 study by the American Society for Microbiology, lead author Katherine Lemon of Children’s Hospital Boston noted that the nose and throat serve as key sites for pathogen colonization, yet their microbiota remained relatively unexplored through culture-independent methods at the time. Prior knowledge about bacteria in these areas was largely derived from culture-based techniques, which primarily targeted pathogenic species.
In the study, Lemon and her team compared bacterial communities in the noses and throats of seven healthy adults using two culture-independent approaches, including a 16S rRNA microarray called the PhyloChip. This tool contained 500,000 probes capable of identifying around 8,500 distinct bacterial groups. Despite the anatomical proximity of the nose and throat, the researchers observed clear differences in bacterial composition. The nasal cavity was predominantly colonized by bacteria from the Firmicutes and Actinobacteria phyla, with a profile resembling that of the skin. In contrast, the throat was dominated by Firmicutes, Proteobacteria, and Bacteroidetes, showing a composition more similar to that of saliva. 
Hence, microbiota is found to be residing the nose and throat of a normal human. Also, that the nose and throat are colonized by these bacteria.
3.5.1. Nasal microbiota and sleep apnea:
Wu BG, Sulaiman I, Wang J, Shen N, Clemente JC, Li Y, Laumbach RJ, Lu SE, Udasin I, Le-Hoang O, Perez A, Alimokhtari S, Black K, Plietz M, Twumasi A, Sanders H, Malecha P, Kapoor B, Scaglione BD, Wang A, Blazoski C, Weiden MD, Rapoport DM, Harrison D, Chitkara N, Vicente E, Marin JM, Sunderram J, Ayappa I, Segal LN have measured:  Measurements and main results: Across two groups, the study found three key associations: 1) the severity of obstructive sleep apnea (OSA) was linked to variations in the diversity and makeup of the microbiome; 2) individuals with severe OSA had higher levels of Streptococcus, Prevotella, and Veillonella in their nasal microbiome; and 3) these differences in the nasal microbiome correlated with markers of inflammation. Network analysis revealed clusters of bacteria that frequently occurred together, forming distinct microbial communities. Several bacteria commonly found in the mouth—such as Streptococcus, Rothia, Veillonella, and Fusobacterium—were correlated with the apnea-hypopnea index. Treatment with continuous positive airway pressure for three months did not alter the composition of the nasal microbiota.
Conclusions: The findings indicate that a disrupted microbiome in severe OSA is associated with inflammatory markers. Further studies using experimental methods are needed to determine whether a causal relationship exists. 
These researches refer to the fact that microbiota can be found inside the human nose and also can lead to sleep apnea by some mechanisms.
3.5.2. The History of the Nasal Microbiota
Similar to the microbiomes of the gut and mouth, the nasal microbiome is not a recent phenomenon in modern humans but rather the outcome of millions of years of coevolution (Moeller et al., 2016) .
Deep ancestral roots: Microbial colonization of the human respiratory tract has been ongoing since the early stages of mammalian evolution. Given that the nasal cavity is perpetually exposed to the external environment—taking in thousands of liters of air each day—it functions as a key site for filtration and immune system education (Man et al., 2017) .
Evidence from the nasopharynx: While ancient DNA from the gut is preserved in fossilized feces and oral ancient DNA is retained in dental calculus, the cartilaginous tissues of the nasal cavity do not fossilize well. Nevertheless, because the nose and throat are closely linked at the nasopharynx, researchers infer the evolutionary history of the nasal microbiome using ancient dental calculus (Warinner, 2016) .
Ancient strains: Genetic analysis of Neanderthal dental plaque, dating to over 50,000 years ago, has identified respiratory tract bacteria, including ancient lineages of Streptococcus and Actinomyces (Weyrich et al., 2017) . These findings suggest that the core bacterial groups present in the upper respiratory tracts of modern humans have inhabited the Homo genus for hundreds of thousands of years (Fellows Yates et al., 2021). 
3.5.3. How the Nasal Microbiota Influences Sleep Apnea
To understand the link between bacteria and obstructive sleep apnea (OSA), it is essential to examine how the host's immune system responds to microbial imbalances, or dysbiosis.
a. The Inflammatory Cascade: 
As noted in the Wu et al. (2019)  study cited, severe OSA is closely associated with both an overrepresentation of specific bacteria such as Streptococcus, Prevotella, and Veillonella and a concurrent rise in inflammatory biomarkers.
b. Epithelial reaction: When the nasal microbiome shifts toward an overgrowth of opportunistic or pathogenic bacteria, the mucosal lining of the nasal cavity detects these microbes (Cai et al., 2021) .
c. Tissue edema (swelling): In response, the immune system releases pro-inflammatory cytokines. This inflammation leads to engorgement of blood vessels in the nasal and pharyngeal tissues and subsequent swelling, resulting in edema (Kheirandish-Gozal & Gozal, 2019) .
d. Airway narrowing: It is this inflamed, swollen tissue—not the bacteria themselves—that constricts the upper airway. The resulting increase in nasal resistance forces the individual to breathe more forcefully, generating negative pressure that makes the throat more prone to collapse during sleep, thereby contributing to OSA (Cai et al., 2021) .
The Vicious Cycle: Intermittent Hypoxia
The relationship between the microbiome and sleep apnea is bidirectional. Not only does dysbiosis exacerbate OSA, but OSA also significantly alters the microbiome (Wang et al., 2022) .
Hypoxia: During an apnea event, oxygen levels in both the blood and local tissues drop markedly—a condition known as intermittent hypoxia (Kheirandish-Gozal & Gozal, 2019) .
Favoring anaerobes: This low-oxygen environment creates a selective advantage for anaerobic bacteria, which thrive without oxygen, including genera such as Prevotella and Fusobacterium (Wu et al., 2019) .
Amplified inflammation: These anaerobes tend to be highly immunogenic, meaning they provoke a strong immune response. As their numbers increase, they drive further local inflammation, leading to more tissue swelling and, in turn, more severe airway collapse (Wang et al., 2022) .
3.5.4. Conclusion:
The nasal microbiota is an ancient companion that dates back to our earliest hominin ancestors. In the context of sleep apnoea, bacteria do not physically obstruct the airway; rather, a shift in the microbial population triggers a local immune response. The resulting inflammation and tissue swelling reduce the diameter of the airway, significantly increasing the likelihood of structural collapse during sleep.
