The findings were notable: significant differences in brain response patterns emerged during the emotional task. Women who had consumed the fermented milk product for four weeks exhibited a calmer neural response, whereas the no-intervention group displayed increased brain hyperactivity during the same task. 
Jeremy Appleton, ND has written: The gut-brain axis constitutes a bidirectional communication system connecting the enteric nervous system with the central nervous system. This network encompasses not only anatomical pathways but also endocrine, humoral, metabolic, and immune routes of communication. Key components such as the autonomic nervous system, the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis, and gastrointestinal nerves facilitate the connection between the gut and the brain. Through these pathways, the brain can modulate intestinal functions—including the activity of immune effector cells—while the gut, in turn, can influence mood, cognition, and mental health. 
Clapp M, Aurora N, Herrera L, Bhatia M, Wilen E, Wakefield S. have suggested: In recent years, the bidirectional relationship among the brain, gut, and microbiome has emerged as a key focus within medical research. A growing body of evidence supporting this connection suggests it will become an important area for future clinical practice, nutrition, and scientific inquiry. This review highlights the significance of a healthy microbiome—particularly the gut microbiota—for individuals with anxiety and depression, as dysbiosis and central nervous system inflammation have been implicated as potential contributors to mental illness. Notably, studies have demonstrated that probiotics can alleviate symptoms of anxiety and depression with an efficacy comparable to that of conventional psychiatric medications.
Despite the absence of regulatory guidelines, patients receiving mood-altering drugs may benefit from the concurrent use of probiotics. The dysbiosis induced either by these medications or by the underlying neurological condition itself could potentially be alleviated through the introduction of beneficial gut bacteria in probiotic form. Ultimately, the central question to be addressed is whether probiotics alone are sufficient to remedy the condition, or whether they must be administered alongside mood-stabilizing treatments. 
Thus, theses research clearly indicate that mood of humans can be mutilated by the gut microbiome. 
Emerging evidence strongly suggests that the systemic dysregulation characteristic of severe psychiatric conditions, including paranoia, is associated with the metabolic activity of the gut microbiota.
The following analysis outlines the mechanisms by which the microbiome influences such severe psychiatric states, examines the available short- and long-term evidence, and elucidates how these findings further substantiate the theory of bacterial multicellularity.
4.5.2. The Implication of Metabolites in Paranoia and Psychosis
Paranoia and psychotic abnormalities are neurologically distinct from generalized anxiety. They are heavily driven by the profound dysregulation of specific neurotransmitter systems, primarily dopamine and glutamate, both of which are significantly influenced by the gastrointestinal microbiome (Zheng et al., 2019) .
The Dopamine Hypothesis: 
Paranoia and schizophrenia are classically linked to a hyperactive dopaminergic system in the central nervous system (CNS). Evidence demonstrates that specific gut bacteria possess the enzymatic machinery to manufacture dopamine and its precursors. Furthermore, a dysbiotic intestinal flora can alter the host's absorption and synthesis of essential amino acids like tyrosine—the primary precursor to dopamine—thereby directly impacting central dopaminergic availability and systemic tone (Strandwitz, 2018) .
The Kynurenine Pathway and Neuroinflammation: 
The most robust link between the microbiome and severe psychiatric disorders lies in tryptophan metabolism. In a homeostatic gut, tryptophan is largely directed toward serotonin synthesis. However, in a dysbiotic, highly inflamed gastrointestinal environment, pathogenic bacteria shunt tryptophan metabolism down the kynurenine pathway (Erhardt et al., 2017) .
Receptor Disruption:
 The kynurenine pathway produces kynurenic acid, an astrocytic metabolite that readily penetrates the brain and acts as an endogenous antagonist of N-methyl-D-aspartate (NMDA) receptors. The hypofunction of NMDA receptors is a primary, validated pharmacological trigger for auditory hallucinations, cognitive deficits, and paranoid delusions characteristic of schizophrenia (Erhardt et al., 2017 ; Plitman et al., 2017  ).
4.5.3. Short-Term vs. Long-Term Effects on Humans
The gut-brain axis operates on different timescales, explaining both acute emotional reactions and chronic psychiatric conditions.
1. Short-Term Effects
Vagal Nerve Signalling: 
Research demonstrates rapid microbial influence on brain function. For instance, a landmark functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) study demonstrated that a four-week probiotic intervention significantly altered the brain's processing of emotional tasks (Tillisch et al., 2013) . This rapid neurocognitive shift is largely mediated by the vagus nerve, which acts as a bidirectional conduit, reading the immediate metabolic environment of the gut and transmitting either calming (GABAergic) or alerting signals to the CNS (Breit et al., 2018) .
Acute Stress Response:
 Acute psychological stress rapidly activates the host's hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis, initiating the systemic release of cortisol. This neuroendocrine response rapidly alters gut motility, mucosal permeability, and the local biochemical environment, inducing immediate shifts in bacterial gene expression and metabolic output (Foster et al., 2017) .
2. Long-Term Effects
Microglial Priming and Chronic Neuroinflammation: 
Long-term intestinal dysbiosis steadily degrades the mucosal epithelial barrier, resulting in increased intestinal permeability ("leaky gut"). This degradation facilitates the continuous translocation of bacterial endotoxins, such as lipopolysaccharides (LPS), into systemic circulation. Over extended periods, this chronic, low-grade inflammation primes the brain's resident immune cells (microglia) to adopt a hyper-reactive state. Chronic microglial activation contributes to synaptic pruning and neurodegeneration—universal hallmarks observed in the neuroanatomy of patients with severe, long-term psychiatric disorders, including paranoid schizophrenia (Severance et al., 2016) .
Epigenetic Changes: 
Prolonged exposure to altered microbial metabolite profiles (e.g., the depletion of neuroprotective short-chain fatty acids like butyrate) can induce epigenetic modifications in host brain tissue. Butyrate acts as a histone deacetylase (HDAC) inhibitor; its absence can permanently alter the transcriptional expression of genes regulating the host's stress response and neurotransmitter receptor density (Stilling et al., 2014) .
4.5.4. Required Further Studies
To definitively link gut metabolites to paranoid abnormalities, the scientific community needs highly specific methodologies:
Fecal Microbiota Transplants (FMT) in Animal Models: 
Researchers must increasingly rely on FMT paradigms, transferring the gut microbiota of human patients experiencing active paranoid psychosis into germ-free murine models. Observing whether these mice develop corresponding behavioral phenotypes—such as severe social withdrawal, hyper-locomotion, or impaired prepulse inhibition (proxies for human psychosis) is critical for establishing causality (Zheng et al., 2019) .
Targeted Longitudinal Metabolomics: 
Current research relies too heavily on cross-sectional taxonomy. Future longitudinal studies must track the exact chemical composition of a patient's metabolome (via blood and stool arrays) immediately prior to, during, and after a psychotic episode to isolate specific microbial chemical triggers (Nikolova et al., 2021) .
4.5.5. Solidifying the Microbiota Multicellularity Theory
The ability of gut bacteria to alter host perception of reality (which is what paranoia is) offers a staggering piece of evidence for the multicellularity theory.
Metabolic Thresholds Require Synchronization: 
A single bacterium, or a disorganized planktonic swarm, cannot synthesize sufficient dopamine precursors or kynurenine metabolites to independently alter the neurochemistry of the human brain. Achieving this requires a massive, coordinated, tissue-level metabolic output. This indicates that bacteria operate within highly structured biofilms, utilizing quorum sensing to precisely synchronize their metabolic factories (Flemming et al., 2016) .
The Biofilm as an Endocrine Organ: 
When the microbiome actively regulates host neurochemistry, it functions indistinguishably from a native human endocrine gland. This demonstrates that the bacterial biofilm is a cohesive, multicellular structure integrated into the host's broader systemic regulatory network (Clarke et al., 2014) .
Ecological Defense Mechanisms: 
From an evolutionary multicellular perspective, severe dysbiosis represents the collapse of an internal ecosystem. When the biofilm structure deteriorates, the resulting erratic metabolic output and systemic inflammation can be interpreted as a biological "danger signal." Consequently, resulting host behaviorssuch as hyper-vigilance, paranoia, and social isolation—may represent a systemic, albeit maladaptive, evolutionary response to the catastrophic failure of the host-microbe multicellular alliance (Zilber-Rosenberg & Rosenberg, 2008) .
4.5.6. conclusion: 
In conclusion, although direct investigation into the relationship between paranoia and the microbiome remains at an early stage, findings from research on anxiety, psychosis, and social behavior strongly indicate that microbial metabolites are involved in pathways relevant to paranoid cognition. Collectively, this body of evidence offers some of the most persuasive support for the multicellular theory the conceptualization of the host not as an autonomous entity, but as a complex multicellular ecosystem in which mental health arises from the coordinated functioning of both human and microbial cells. If microbiome were multicellular then what if: 1. The hallucinations whether visual and or audio were directly caused by them? 2. What if these were planned and not random? 3. What if for these hallucinations to be seen and or heard some special immunities or barriers or centers should be surpassed?
4.6. Depression and insomnia can microbiota have a relation?
4.6.1. derpression and pathophysiology of sleep and relationship to microbiota:
Madison A, Kiecolt-Glaser JK. have said: Within a complex network of interactions, psychological stress, depression, and diet each play a role—both separately and together—in shaping the composition of the gut microbiota. Stress and depression influence gut bacteria through immune, autonomic, and endocrine pathways, while diet determines which bacterial strains thrive. Stress and depression can also lead to unhealthy eating patterns, and in turn, diet affects how a person responds to stress and their emotional state. This means diet and stress can influence each other, jointly shaping the gut environment.
In response, gut bacteria produce metabolites, toxins, and neurohormones. Dominant bacterial species may act in their own interest rather than the host’s, releasing substances that mimic appetite-related hormones and intensify food cravings. Through this diet-related route or others—such as the vagus nerve—gut bacteria can affect stress reactivity and mood. Ultimately, a person’s stressors, emotional state, and diet, combined with their gut bacteria, can influence immune function and overall health. 
Limbana T, Khan F, Eskander N have established: The gut microbiome plays a key role in human health, with evidence suggesting it contributes to mental well-being. Once established, the quality of the gut microbiota—supported by food and other dietary sources—becomes an important factor in daily life. This literature review involved a PubMed search for studies examining the relationship between the gut microbiome and depression. Relevant articles were identified using Medical Subject Heading (MeSH) keywords. After applying inclusion and exclusion criteria and verifying accessibility, 26 articles were selected. The review aimed to clarify the role of the gut microbiome in depression, and the findings indicate a strong connection between microbiome function and mental health. 
Smith RP et. Al have said: The gut microbiome can affect human health via the brain-gut-microbiome axis, and there is mounting evidence that it also influences sleep quality. However, studies on sleep deprivation and the gut microbiome have produced inconsistent results. One recent study reported that sleep deprivation alters gut microbiome composition, while another found no such effect. As a result, the relationship between sleep physiology and the gut microbiome remains unclear.
To investigate this uncertainty, we used actigraphy to objectively measure sleep and collected gut microbiome samples to explore how the microbiome correlates with various sleep parameters. We also assessed immune biomarkers and neurobehavioral performance, as these factors may influence the link between sleep and microbiome composition.
Our findings showed that overall microbiome diversity was positively associated with greater sleep efficiency and longer total sleep time, and negatively associated with time spent awake after falling asleep. Total microbiome diversity was also positively correlated with levels of interleukin-6, a cytokine known to affect sleep. Looking at specific bacterial groups, we found that the richness of Bacteroidetes and Firmicutes was positively correlated with sleep efficiency, interleukin-6 levels, and abstract thinking. In contrast, several taxa—including Lachnospiraceae, Corynebacterium, and Blautia—showed negative correlations with sleep measures.
These findings establish connections among gut microbiome composition, sleep physiology, immune function, and cognition, and may point toward future strategies for improving sleep by targeting the gut microbiome. 
Li Y et. al. have found: results: Compared to healthy controls, individuals with insomnia showed lower gut microbiome richness and diversity, along with fewer anaerobic bacteria and short-chain fatty acid (SCFA)-producing bacteria, and a greater presence of potential pathobionts. Specific bacteria distinguished different insomnia groups: Lachnospira and Bacteroides were characteristic of acute insomnia, whereas Faecalibacterium and Blautia were characteristic of chronic insomnia. These insomnia-related bacterial signatures also correlated with self-reported sleep quality and levels of the inflammatory marker IL-1β in the blood.
Conclusion: These results indicate that insomnia symptoms, gut microbiota, and inflammation may be interconnected in complex ways. The gut microbiota could serve as a valuable biomarker for supporting insomnia diagnosis and may offer new targets for therapeutic intervention in sleep disorders.  
Li Y, Hao Y, Fan F, Zhang B. have discussed: In summary, a bidirectional relationship exists among the gut microbiome, sleep, and depression, with inflammation and endocrine hormones serving as key mediators in this process. First, chronic disruption of circadian rhythms, sleep loss, and depression affect the metabolism of native gut bacteria and lead to shifts in their composition. These shifts typically involve a reduction in bacteria from the Lactobacillaceae family alongside an increase in Bacteroides multiforme, Enterococci, Lachnospiraceae, and Ruminococcaceae, resulting in microbial dysbiosis. Second, gut bacteria help regulate the tight junctions between epithelial cells, which maintain intestinal barrier integrity and reduce permeability. When this epithelial barrier breaks down, bacteria and their harmful metabolites can enter the mesenteric lymph tissue, triggering inflammatory immune responses and activating the vagus nerve and spinal afferent pathways. One potential mechanism underlying this process is inflammation caused by gut microbiome dysbiosis, which leads to abnormal immune reactions, affects the central nervous system, and contributes to the onset or worsening of insomnia and depression, while also promoting metabolic disease.
Additionally, the gut microbiota interact with clock genes through various pathways. Nevertheless, further research is needed to understand how the rhythmic activity of the gut microbiota contributes to the circadian control network in the intestine and throughout the body, as well as to clarify how rhythmic oscillations and functional activities are coordinated between host and microbes. 
The fact that can be concluded from these research and reviews is that there is a direct implication of Microbiome on depression and insomnia.

4.6.2. The Direct Link Between Metabolites and Depressive Abnormalities
Depression is increasingly understood not merely as a neurochemical disorder but as a condition shaped by systemic metabolic and inflammatory processes originating in the gut microbiome.
Metabolite-Driven Inflammation:
 According to contemporary models of the microbiota-gut-brain axis, dysbiosis contributes to elevated levels of pro-inflammatory cytokines, including interleukin-6 (IL-6) and interleukin-1β (IL-1β) (Smith et al., 2020; Li et al., 2021). This inflammatory response is mediated by microbial metabolites. Disruption of the intestinal mucosal barrier—frequently resulting from a reduction in short-chain fatty acid (SCFA)-producing bacteria—allows lipopolysaccharides (LPS) from bacterial cell walls to enter systemic circulation. This phenomenon, known as metabolic endotoxemia, triggers systemic neuroinflammation, which in turn affects the central nervous system (CNS) and contributes to depressive symptoms such as fatigue, sleep disturbance, and low mood (Kelly et al., 2015) .
Neurotransmitter Modulation: 
Gut bacteria are capable of synthesizing, metabolizing, and regulating key neurotransmitters, including gamma-aminobutyric acid (GABA), serotonin, and dopamine (Strandwitz, 2018) . As noted by Madison and Kiecolt-Glaser (2019) , certain bacteria release compounds that mimic host neurohormones. When the microbiome becomes dysbiotic, the overall production of these mood-regulating metabolites declines, effectively depriving the gut-brain axis of the neurochemical substrates necessary for emotional stability.
Tryptophan Metabolism: 
In the context of inflammation and dysbiosis, host and microbial metabolic pathways increasingly divert tryptophan the essential precursor to serotonin away from indoleamine synthesis and toward the kynurenine pathway. This shift results in the production of neurotoxic metabolites, such as quinolinic acid, which can cross the blood-brain barrier and promote behaviors associated with depression, including anhedonia and lethargy (Schwarcz et al., 2012) .
4.6.3. Temporal Dynamics of the Gut-Brain Axis: Acute Versus Chronic Effects
Communication along the gut-brain axis operates across distinct temporal scales, influencing both transient physiological states and enduring psychiatric conditions (Cryan et al., 2019) .
1. Acute Effects
Immediate Stress Response: 
Psychological stress or a single episode of sleep deprivation rapidly activates the autonomic nervous system and the hypothalamic-pituitary-adrenal (HPA) axis. This neuroendocrine response quickly alters gastrointestinal motility, mucosal secretions, and intestinal permeability. Within hours, these physiological changes modify the local gut environment, prompting rapid adjustments in the metabolic activity and gene expression of the resident bacterial community (Foster et al., 2017) .
Short-Term Dietary Changes: 
Modifications in diet can shift active microbial metabolic pathways within 24 to 48 hours. For example, a sudden increase in highly processed carbohydrates rapidly supports the growth of pathobionts, resulting in a temporary inflammatory response that may lead to acute cognitive fatigue, lethargy, and transient declines in mood (David et al., 2014) .
2. Chronic Effects
Sustained Vagal Dysfunction: 
Over extended periods, persistent disruptions—such as chronic insomnia or long-standing clinical depression—maintain a state of microbial dysbiosis. The prolonged reduction in short-chain fatty acid (SCFA)-producing bacteria compromises the tight junction proteins that maintain intestinal barrier integrity. The resulting chronic, low-grade inflammation diminishes afferent signaling through the vagus nerve, contributing to sustained depressive symptoms and heightened stress sensitivity (Breit et al., 2018) .
Disruption of Circadian Rhythms: 
Chronic insomnia disrupts the coordinated daily oscillations between the host's central circadian clock and the metabolic rhythms of the gut microbiota. This uncoupling impairs metabolic balance and may intensify psychiatric symptoms (Thaiss et al., 2014) .
4.6.4. Methodological Priorities for Advancing Research
To establish causality between specific microbial metabolites and depressive pathophysiology, the field must move beyond observational study designs.
Longitudinal Multi-Omics Approaches: 
Large-scale longitudinal studies are needed to continuously profile patients' metabolomes—the full spectrum of chemical products in blood and feces—alongside metatranscriptomic data, which captures active bacterial gene expression. Such tracking should occur before, during, and after major depressive episodes or periods of insomnia (Zheng et al., 2020) .
Isotope Tracing Techniques: 
Research employing stable isotope tracers is essential for tracing the pharmacokinetics of microbially derived compounds, such as specific short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs). This approach would clarify whether these metabolites cross the blood-brain barrier and directly influence neural function during depressive states (Brial et al., 2018) .
Targeted Mechanistic Interventions: 
Human clinical trials should focus on isolating specific variables through highly controlled interventions. Rather than using broad-spectrum probiotics, researchers should administer precisely synthesized microbial metabolites—known as postbiotics—to determine whether individual compounds alone are sufficient to alleviate depressive symptoms (Vrzáčková et al., 2021) .
4.6.5. Supporting the Theory of Microbial Multicellularity
The ability of bacteria to advance their own fitness by influencing host appetite, mood, and circadian rhythms offers compelling support for the concept of bacterial multicellularity.
Biofilm-Mediated Ecological Engineering: 
A single bacterial cell lacks the metabolic capacity to meaningfully alter human neurochemistry. To induce systemic inflammation, modulate vagal tone, and affect sleep architecture, the microbial community must produce metabolites in large, coordinated quantities. This level of coordination requires bacteria to organize into structured multicellular biofilms, using quorum sensing to synchronize their collective metabolic activity (Flemming et al., 2016) .
The Self-Interested Superorganism:
 Evidence suggests that dominant bacterial species release signaling molecules that influence host food preferences, steering consumption toward nutrients that support the microbial community rather than the host's own nutritional needs (Alcock et al., 2014 ; Madison & Kiecolt-Glaser, 2019 ). This implies that the biofilm functions as a cohesive, localized superorganism capable of shaping host behavior to secure resources that enhance its evolutionary success.
Circadian Coordination:
 The finding that gut microbiota interact with host circadian genes and undergo coordinated daily structural reorganization indicates that the biofilm is not merely a static collection of cells. Instead, it functions as a dynamic, tissue-like assembly that shifts in multicellular synchrony with the host's daily and environmental cycles (Thaiss et al., 2014) .
The more we understand how gut microbes orchestrate systemic psychological states, the more undeniable it becomes that they are functioning as a complex, multicellular organ deeply integrated into our biology.
4.6.6. Conclusion
The cumulative evidence strongly indicates that clinical depression and related psychiatric conditions are directly influenced by the metabolic activity of the gut microbiota. This bidirectional relationship functions across multiple timeframes, from the rapid regulation of neurotransmitters to the long-term epigenetic shaping of stress responses. Importantly, these findings offer robust support for the theory of microbial multicellularity the understanding that humans are not autonomous, independent entities but rather complex multicellulars. Mental well-being arises from the deeply integrated, mutually regulated interactions between human and microbial cells. As Madison and Kiecolt-Glaser (2019)  characterize it, humans exist within a "dynamic human-microbe cycle" in which stress, diet, mood, and microbial activity continually shape one another. This viewpoint does not undermine the authenticity of human psychological experience; instead, it deepens it by revealing the extensive multicellular partnerships that underlie human consciousness and behaviour. Lastly, what if they were multicellular then: 1. What are the mechanisms used by microbiota during certain times of day, night and month? 2.are there external microbiota that interfere with the host’s microbiota to cause depression? 3. Can the host’s microbiota cooperate with other persons surrounding this person to interfere with the emotions’of the host? 
4.7. The human microbiome and the sensation of fear:
4.7.1. gut microbiota and relationship to phobia:
A phobia is a type of anxiety disorder. It is a strong, irrational fear of something that poses little or no actual danger. There are many specific phobias. Acrophobia is a fear of heights. 
Panic attacks= anxiety
Yang B, Wei J, Ju P, Chen J. have found: Our analysis reveals that over half of the included studies reported a positive effect on anxiety symptoms through modulation of the gut microbiota. Two types of interventions—probiotic and non-probiotic—were used to regulate the intestinal microbiota, and notably, non-probiotic approaches appeared to be more effective than probiotic ones. However, further research is needed to confirm this finding, as a meta-analysis could not be conducted at this stage. 
Simpson CA, Diaz-Arteche C, Eliby D, Schwartz OS, Simmons JG, Cowan CSM. have stressed out the following findings: A growing body of research suggests that the collection of microorganisms in the gastrointestinal tract—known as the gut microbiota—is linked to anxiety and depressive disorders. This study presents the first systematic review focusing specifically on the gut microbiota in anxiety disorders, while also providing an updated review for depression. Given the high rates of comorbidity between these conditions, it is important to consider shared underlying factors. Following PRISMA guidelines, the systematic search yielded 26 studies: two case-control comparisons of gut microbiota in generalized anxiety disorder, 18 in depression, one including both anxiety and depression, and five involving only symptom-based measures. Findings on alpha and beta diversity were inconsistent; however, differences in bacterial composition suggested that these disorders may be characterized by a greater abundance of proinflammatory bacteria (such as Enterobacteriaceae and Desulfovibrio) and fewer short-chain fatty acid-producing bacteria (such as Faecalibacterium). Several bacterial taxa and their mechanisms of action may contribute to the pathophysiology of anxiety and depression by facilitating communication between peripheral inflammation and the brain. Although the gut microbiota remains a promising target for prevention and therapy, future research should account for confounding factors—especially diet and psychotropic medications—and investigate microbial function. 
Within the context of the above data, anyone can conclude that Gut Microbes maybe the indirect cause of Anxiety and excessive fear in humans. 
Incorporating these specific phobic mechanisms into the broader theoretical framework of ongoing research on bacterial multicellularity yields a compelling argument: the gut biofilm may actively participate in the host's threat-assessment and memory-extinction processes. The following analysis examines how microbial metabolites are implicated in phobias, evaluates the short- and long-term evidence, outlines the studies necessary to substantiate this hypothesis, and discusses how such findings would further support the theory of bacterial multicellularity.
4.7.2. The Direct Link Between Metabolites and Phobia (Fear Extinction)
Phobias are neurologically characterized not merely by the presence of fear, but by a definitive failure in fear extinction. When a healthy neurobiological system encounters a conditioned trigger and assesses it as non-threatening, the medial prefrontal cortex sends inhibitory signals to the amygdala to "extinguish" the fear response. In phobia and trauma-related disorders, this extinction learning fails. Emerging evidence indicates that the gastrointestinal microbiome acts as a master regulator of this specific neurocognitive process (Chu et al., 2019) .
Brain-Derived Neurotrophic Factor (BDNF): 
Fear extinction requires profound neuroplasticity; the brain must actively consolidate new "safety" pathways. This process is highly dependent on BDNF. Research demonstrates that a dysbiotic or absent microbiome severely downregulates BDNF expression in the hippocampus and amygdala (Clarke et al., 2013) . Without adequate microbially signaled BDNF, the host's central nervous system lacks the neurochemical plasticity required to rewrite fear memories.
Microbial Metabolites and Fear Conditioning: 
Landmark studies utilizing germ-free murine models demonstrate that specific microbial metabolites travel to the brain and directly influence the neurons responsible for fear extinction. While germ-free mice can successfully acquire fear conditioning, they exhibit profound deficits in unlearning the fear when the threat is removed—mirroring human phobic pathology. The reintroduction of a healthy microbiome, or specific microbial metabolites, successfully restores extinction learning pathways (Chu et al., 2019) .
The GABA/Glutamate Balance in the Amygdala: 
The immediate phobic panic response is driven by an excitatory glutamate spike. A deficit in microbially derived gamma-aminobutyric acid (GABA) precursors leaves the amygdala hyper-excitable. This metabolic imbalance transforms a baseline startle response into a full-blown phobic panic attack by preventing necessary inhibitory signalling (Strandwitz, 2018) .
4.7.3. Short-Term vs. Long-Term Effects on Humans
The interaction between a phobic trigger and the microbiome creates an immediate physiological feedback loop, which, if sustained, can calcify into a long-term neurological deficit.
1. Short-Term Effects
Acute Vagal Withdrawal and Motility Shifts:
 Upon encountering a phobic stimulus, the acute "fight-or-flight" response immediately suppresses vagal tone and redirects blood flow from the splanchnic circulation to skeletal muscles. This creates a sudden shift in intestinal oxygen tension and pH levels. The localized microbial environment experiences an acute physiological shock, transiently halting the production of beneficial, mood-stabilizing metabolites (Foster et al., 2017) .
Catecholamine-Induced Biofilm Dispersion: 
High surges of host stress hormones, specifically norepinephrine, serve as environmental cues for certain enteric bacteria. Catecholamines can induce pathogenic bacteria to acutely disperse from their structural biofilms and increase virulence, releasing endotoxins that further amplify systemic inflammation and host panic (Hughes & Sperandio, 2008) .
2. Long-Term Effects
Failure of the Extinction Loop: 
Over the long term, if the host microbiome lacks adequate populations of short-chain fatty acid (SCFA)-producing bacteria (such as Faecalibacterium), the brain fails to receive critical homeostatic signals. Consequently, the phobia becomes neurologically entrenched because the biochemical environment required to successfully rewrite the fear memory is chronically absent (Burokas et al., 2017 ; Simpson et al., 2021 ).
4.7.4. Concreting the Microbiota Multicellularity Theory
Applying these neurobiological dynamics to the core arguments of biofilm multicellularity introduces a profound evolutionary perspective.
The Biofilm as a Threat-Assessment Co-Processor: 
A phobic panic attack represents a massive metabolic expenditure for the host macro-organism, burning substantial ATP, elevating cortisol, and disrupting homeostasis. If the bacterial biofilm synthesizes metabolites that actively promote fear extinction, the multicellular colony is effectively down-regulating host panic to conserve the multicellular’s shared energy resources (Zilber-Rosenberg & Rosenberg, 2008) .
Coordinated Metabolic Release: 
Synthesizing sufficient neuroactive metabolites or BDNF-stimulating molecules to alter the amygdala's fear-extinction pathways cannot be achieved by disorganized, planktonic bacteria. It requires a synchronized, tissue-level metabolic output originating from a highly organized biofilm structure, operating via quorum sensing (Flemming et al., 2016) .
Host-Microbe Symbiotic Memory: 
This dynamic suggests that cognitive memory—specifically the contextual memory of safety versus danger—is not stored exclusively within human neural architecture. The biochemical prerequisites for that memory are continuously manufactured by the bacterial collective. In this context, the multicellular biofilm acts as an ecological co-processor, maintaining the foundational biochemical baseline required for the host to accurately perceive reality and adapt to its environment (Stilling et al., 2014) .
4.7.5. Conclusion: 
The available evidence strongly indicates that phobias and fear-related abnormalities are directly associated with the metabolites and signaling molecules produced by the gut microbiota. Key pathways—including the indole-SK2 channel-amygdala axis, the role of butyrate in threat-safety discrimination, and the capacity of probiotics to enhance fear extinction—all point to a causal role for microbial involvement. These mechanisms operate across multiple timescales, ranging from rapid modulation of neuronal activity to long-term programming of fear-related neural circuits.
Importantly, this body of research provides some of the most compelling validation to date for the theory of microbiota multicellularity. Recognizing that fear responses—among the most fundamental of survival mechanisms—are co-regulated by microbial partners underscores the view of humans as multicellulars, whose mental health emerges from the integrated activity of both human and microbial cells. As noted by Professor Patrick Tan, this line of research serves as "a reminder that mental health is not just in the brain—it's in the gut too."
Then if bacteria were multicellular from this perspective, then: 1. Are these fear attacks the key or the opening of the host’s immune system to breach it? 2. Are these attacks used to control the host in certain ways (meaning changing the direction of flow of thoughts)? 
4.8. The Human behavior and the microbiome:
4.8.1. human behavior shaped by the microbiome:
In the last few sections different types of mood changes were seen that were caused by the gut microbiota directly or indirectly. Below is some further evidences.
Timothy G. et al have postulated: The human gastrointestinal tract hosts a dynamic and intricate microbial ecosystem, comprising approximately one kilogram of bacteria in the average adult—a mass roughly equivalent to the weight of the human brain. The evolutionary development of a complex gut microbiota in mammals has been instrumental in facilitating brain development and, potentially, sophisticated social behavior. The collective genetic material of these gut microbes, known as the microbiome, vastly outnumbers the total number of human genes in the body and is capable of synthesizing a wide range of neuroactive compounds. Gut microbes operate as part of the unconscious regulatory system that governs behavior. Recent research indicates that these microorganisms significantly influence cognitive function and fundamental behavioral patterns, including social interaction and stress responses. In the absence of gut microbes, underlying neurochemical processes are profoundly altered. Consequently, the study of gut microbes may contribute meaningfully to advancing the understanding of disorders characterized by impaired cognitive function and social interaction, such as autism spectrum disorder. 
Sampson TR, Mazmanian SK have reached the following statement: Although microbiologists have recognized for decades that microbes can alter host behavior across various invertebrate and vertebrate systems, it is only within the last five years that other scientific disciplines have begun to appreciate the potential magnitude and depth of the influence exerted by commensal microorganisms on complex neurological function in mammals. A limited number of molecular mechanisms governing these interactions have been identified; nevertheless, the field is poised to achieve significant progress in understanding this emerging paradigm, driven by growing awareness of the gut–microbiome–brain axis. Multidisciplinary collaborations among microbiologists, immunologists, neuroscientists, and bioinformaticians will continue to advance discoveries regarding how symbiotic microbes shape brain function and behavior, and how these organisms might be harnessed to combat neurological diseases.
Neurodevelopmental, psychiatric, and neurodegenerative disorders rank among the most serious medical and societal burdens of the present era. Yet the etiology of most brain disorders remains unknown, and available therapies are largely either ineffective or associated with severe side effects. Novel conceptual frameworks and therapeutic approaches are urgently needed to explain and address numerous neurological conditions. It appears that future breakthroughs in neuroscience will not rely solely on the study of the brain, but rather, unexpectedly, on exploration of a forgotten organ of comparable size and complexity—the human microbiome. 
Alcock J, Maley CC, Aktipis CA have suggested: Contemporary biology suggests that the human body is composed of a diverse array of organisms that compete for nutritional resources. Evolutionary conflict between the host and its microbiota may give rise to food cravings and cognitive dissonance regarding dietary choices. Exercising self-control over eating behaviors may therefore be partly a matter of suppressing microbial signals originating in the gut. Acquired tastes, in turn, may result from the acquisition of microbes that derive benefit from those foods. The review concludes that one strategy for modifying eating behavior involves intervening in the composition or activity of the gut microbiota. 
Quagliariello Andrea, Del Chierico Federica, Russo Alessandra, Reddel Sofia, Conte Giulia, Lopetuso Loris R., Ianiro Gianluca, Dallapiccola Bruno, Cardona Francesco, Gasbarrini Antonio, Putignani Lorenza, have reached the following statement: Pediatric acute-onset neuropsychiatric syndrome (PANS) and pediatric autoimmune neuropsychiatric disorders associated with streptococcal infections (PANDAS) are conditions that disrupt normal neurological function in children, resulting in the sudden onset of tics, obsessive-compulsive disorder, and other behavioral symptoms. Recent research has underscored the bidirectional communication between the gut and the brain, emphasizing how gut microbial composition can influence behavior and brain function. Accordingly, the present study investigated the relationship between PANS/PANDAS and gut microbiota ecology. The gut microbial composition of a cohort of 30 patients diagnosed with PANS/PANDAS was analyzed and compared with that of control subjects using 16S rRNA-based metagenomics. Data were examined for α- and β-diversity; differences in bacterial distribution were identified using Wilcoxon and LEfSe tests, while metabolic profiles were predicted using PICRUSt software. These analyses revealed an altered bacterial community structure in PANS/PANDAS patients relative to controls. Notably, ecological analysis identified two primary subject clusters based on age range. In conclusion, the study suggests that streptococcal infections modify gut bacterial communities, thereby promoting a pro-inflammatory state through the selection of specific bacterial strains associated with gut inflammation and immune response activation. These findings highlight the potential for identifying bacterial biomarkers linked to this disorder and may lead to the development of novel therapeutic strategies.  
WT. Lee, LC. Wong. Have written the following: Conclusions: The study identified a reduced prevalence of Prevotella and an increased prevalence of Ruminococcus in children with Tourette syndrome (TS). Additionally, the proportion of Prevotella was found to be negatively correlated with tic severity. Future population-based research may help elucidate the role of gut microbiota in the pathogenesis of TS. 
Tourette (too-RET) syndrome is a disorder that involves repetitive movements or unwanted sounds (tics) that can't be easily controlled. For instance, you might repeatedly blink your eyes, shrug your shoulders or blurt out unusual sounds or offensive words. 
It is evident that the gut microbes affect the human behaviour and helps to shape it. Moreover, there are certain conditions like Tourette syndrome that are closely related to the gut microbiota. These conditions have symptoms of uncontrolled behaviors. 
The hypothesis linking sleepwalking to unconscious foraging behaviour represents a highly innovative conceptual advance. It extends the notion of microbial manipulation beyond subtle alterations in mood into the domain of gross motor control and complex behavioral hijacking.
In developing a theoretical framework for a scientific paper on bacterial multicellularity and biofilms, this specific hypothesis offers a compelling anchor point. It compels the scientific community to confront a fundamental question: which entity is truly directing the host?
The following analysis explores additional human-specific evidence of behavioural shaping, the mechanisms underlying the "sleep-foraging" hypothesis, and the profound implications of this framework for the theory of multicellularity.
4.8.2. Further Compelling Evidence of Behavioral Shaping in Humans and Microbiome Influence on Complex Neurological Conditions
1. Autism Spectrum Disorder (ASD) and Microbiota Transfer Therapy
 ASD is characterized by atypical social behavior, communication challenges, and frequently by severe gastrointestinal (GI) distress. A landmark open-label clinical trial by Kang et al. (2017) , followed by a longitudinal observational study (Kang et al., 2019) , employed Microbiota Transfer Therapy (MTT) in children diagnosed with ASD. This protocol involves bowel cleansing followed by the introduction of a healthy, highly diverse microbial ecosystem.
Behavioral Shift: Two years post-treatment, researchers observed not only a sustained significant reduction in severe GI symptoms among patients, but also a nearly 50% decrease in core behavioral symptoms of ASD—including social impairment and irritability—relative to baseline. The transplanted microbiome successfully engrafted, suggesting that durable alterations to the gut ecosystem are associated with long-term shifts in behavioral phenotypes, likely mediated by systemic neurochemical changes (Kang et al., 2019) .
2. Parkinson's Disease (PD) and Enteric Origins
Parkinson's disease is characterized by motor symptoms (e.g., tremors, bradykinesia) and non-motor behavioral changes (e.g., apathy, cognitive decline), fundamentally driven by the aggregation of misfolded alpha-synuclein proteins. Clinical data indicate that GI dysfunction, notably chronic constipation and specific gut dysbiosis, frequently precedes the onset of PD motor symptoms by as much as several decades (Sampson et al., 2016) .
Pathological Shift: This temporal sequence supports the Braak hypothesis, which proposes that alpha-synuclein misfolding may originate in the enteric nervous system, potentially triggered by the metabolic output of specific microbial populations. The pathology is theorized to propagate retrogradely via the vagus nerve to the central nervous system in a prion-like manner (Braak et al., 2003) . Thus, the microbiome may indirectly alter host motor behavior by serving as the environmental trigger for targeted protein corruption.
4.8.3. The "Sleep-Foraging" Hypothesis: Theoretical Microbial Influence on Parasomnias
The hypothesis that sleepwalking or unconscious night-eating serves as a mechanism by which specific microbiota acquire preferential substrates represents a speculative yet compelling application of the gut–brain axis. In humans, conditions such as Sleep-Related Eating Disorder (SRED) and Night Eating Syndrome (NES) involve unconscious or poorly recollected nocturnal consumption of highly palatable, carbohydrate-dense foods (Howell et al., 2009) .
Although no empirical evidence currently exists that biofilms can orchestrate the complex motor sequences required for somnambulism, the foundational mechanisms of microbial reward-hijacking are supported by the literature:
Vagal Communication and Reward Stimulation: 
Microbes are known to manipulate host eating behavior to enhance their own fitness. They can produce neuroactive metabolites and peptide analogs—such as those mimicking ghrelin or dopamine—that influence vagal nerve signaling to the basal ganglia (Alcock et al., 2014 ; Fetissov, 2017 ).
Theoretical Locomotor Hijacking: 
During non-rapid eye movement (NREM) sleep, the prefrontal cortex exhibits reduced activation. The hypothesis posits that, in the absence of conscious executive control, acute metabolic stress originating from a dominant, sugar-deprived bacterial biofilm could generate intense vagal cravings that trigger automated foraging motor sequences. Although highly theoretical, this framework models the host as an automated resource-delivery mechanism responding to localized chemical imperatives.
4.8.4. Implications for Evolutionary Biology: The Multicellular Paradigm
If localized bacterial communities possess the capacity to drastically alter host behavior to secure nutrients, this challenges traditional definitions of the biological "individual," aligning with the multicellular concept (Rosenberg & Zilber-Rosenberg, 2016) .
1. Biofilms as Decentralized Chemical Networks: 
Biofilms, rather than being passive aggregates, exhibit immense and synchronized metabolic coordination. The capacity to calculate a nutrient deficit, synthesize host-specific neurotransmitter analogs, and subsequently alter host behavior represents complex, system-level problem-solving characteristic of advanced multicellularity.
2. Cross-Kingdom Quorum Sensing and Extended Phenotypes: 
Quorum sensing traditionally defines intra- and inter-species bacterial communication. However, host-manipulation theories suggest that the host organism is actively included in this chemical quorum. By synthesizing signaling molecules that the mammalian nervous system is obligated to process, the resulting host behavior (e.g., altered feeding) acts as an "extended phenotype" of the bacterial collective (Dawkins, 1982 ; Alcock et al., 2014 ).
3. The "Mecha" Conceptual Framework: 
In theoretical ecology, the host can be conceptualized as a biological "mecha." The mammalian body provides gross motor function, structural defense, and macro-navigation. Conversely, the microbiome acts as a sophisticated internal control system, pulling chemical levers that influence where the host navigates and what it consumes. In this paradigm, microbial multicellularity extends beyond bacterial cooperation, organizing into a signaling network capable of guiding a mammalian host.
4.8.5. Conclusion:
The evidence drawn from the sources under review and from recent research overwhelmingly indicates that gut microbiota actively reshapes human behaviour both in health and disease through multiple mechanisms, including neurotransmitter production, epigenetic modulation, vagal signaling, and immune regulation. From feeding behavior in Hydra to social interaction in humans, a consistent pattern emerges: the behavior of the multicellular arises from the coordinated activity of human and microbial cells.
The hypothesis concerning sleepwalking, while speculative, points to the most profound implication of the multicellularity theory. If microbes can manipulate waking behavior to serve their own interests, they may also influence behavior during sleep, when conscious control is diminished. This does not imply that every parasomnia results from microbial manipulation; rather, it opens a compelling new frontier for research.
The multicellular concept ultimately transforms the understanding of the self. Humans are not autonomous individuals but ecosystems—walking communities whose thoughts, moods, cravings, and possibly even nocturnal wanderings reflect the negotiated interests of trillions of cooperating and competing organisms. The "self" is not a unitary consciousness but a parliament of cells, both human and microbial, constantly negotiating the terms of shared existence.
This perspective does not diminish human experience; rather, it enriches it by revealing the hidden partnerships that shape the inner world. Moreover, it offers hope: because the microbiota is manipulable, it may eventually become possible to treat behavioral disorders by restoring balance to the multicellular. What is the tole of microbiota in indulging and repeating certain behaviours done by the human host?
4.9. Gut Microbiota and heart diseases:  
4.9.1. the possibility of arterial diseases and cardiac disease to be caused by microbiota 
Kazemian, N., Mahmoudi, M., Halperin, F. et al. have stated: The gut microbiome can directly influence the development of hypercholesterolemia and coronary artery disease (CAD) by producing specific metabolites, including bile acids, coprostanol, short-chain fatty acids, and trimethylamine-N-oxide.
Conclusions
A thorough understanding of how the gut microbiota impacts human health—and the ability to design therapeutic strategies for hypercholesterolemia and CAD—requires clarifying the interconnected physiological factors that collectively shape both the microbiota and disease progression. Future research into these complex pathways, utilizing approaches such as advanced nanomedicine, data science, and consideration of variables like ethnicity and sex, is essential for uncovering the bacterial mechanisms involved. Such insights will enable more effective, precision-based microbiome interventions for preventing and treating CAD, ultimately reducing its societal and economic burden.  
Trøseid M, Andersen GØ, Broch K, Hov JR. A growing body of evidence indicates that changes in the composition or function of the gut microbiome may also contribute to cardiovascular disease. 
Battson ML, Lee DM, Weir TL, Gentile CL. Have declared: The gut microbiome is now recognized as a key modulator of human health. Disruptions in the balance or abundance of gut bacteria—known as gut dysbiosis—have been connected to the onset and progression of various nutrition-related illnesses, including cardiovascular disease (CVD). Many CVD risk factors, such as aging, obesity, unhealthy eating patterns, and physical inactivity, have been found to promote gut dysbiosis. This imbalance is accompanied by intestinal inflammation and a weakened gut barrier, leading to higher blood levels of bacterial cell wall components and microbial byproducts, which may contribute to the development of CVD. 
Ma J, Li H. have announced: In conclusion, secondary bile acids produced by the gut microbiota contribute significantly to the progression of atherosclerosis by influencing several bile acid receptors, including FXR, PXR, TGR5, VDR, and S1PR2. This insight underscores the promising potential of developing new treatments for atherosclerosis that target the gut microbiome (Levi, 2016). 
Pieczynska MD, Yang Y, Petrykowski S, Horbanczuk OK, Atanasov AG, Horbanczuk JO. have aforementioned: Metabolites produced by the gut microbiota significantly influence the host's digestive function and overall health. The impact of intestinal microbes on host metabolism and nutrient absorption is primarily mediated through regulatory pathways involving serotonin, cytokines, and various metabolites. Numerous studies have consistently shown that the gut microbiota is essential for the absorption of bioactive compounds, as it converts dietary polyphenols into absorbable, active substances. Furthermore, certain intestinal metabolites derived from natural polyphenolic compounds exhibit greater biological activity than the original molecules themselves. Bioactive substances such as polyphenols, prebiotics, and probiotics—collectively known as the "three Ps" for gut health—are the most recognized dietary strategies for modulating the composition of gut microbial communities and their metabolic or immunological functions.
Intestinal microbial metabolites indirectly influence atherosclerosis by regulating lipid metabolism and inflammation. Research has shown that gut microbiota diversity is inversely correlated with atherosclerosis progression: the lower the variation and number of microbial species in the gut, the higher the risk of developing atherosclerosis. Consequently, preventing and treating atherosclerosis may be achievable by enhancing the diversity and abundance of gut microbiota. In this review, we summarize the effects of gut metabolites derived from natural products on the pathological processes of atherosclerosis, as these intestinal metabolites not only indirectly affect macrophage foam cell formation within the vessel wall but also exert direct effects on vascular endothelial cells. 
Hence, we have discovered that there are overwhelming evidences that microbiome can lead to heart disease. Among the pathways are direct and indirect through modulating metabolites. Among, those heart diseases are atherosclerosis or hardened heart and artery diseases. 
4.9.2. The Existence of a "Heart" or "Vascular" Microbiome
Traditionally, the human cardiovascular system and internal organs including the blood were regarded as entirely sterile under healthy conditions. However, contrary to this longstanding view, recent high-throughput DNA sequencing has revealed the presence of bacterial genetic material, and in some cases viable bacteria, within diseased cardiovascular tissues (Koren et al., 2011) . It is important to note that humans do not harbour a dense, commensal "vascular microbiome" comparable to that of the gastrointestinal tract.
1.The Atherosclerotic Plaque Microbiome:
Employing 16S ribosomal RNA (rRNA) gene sequencing, multiple independent investigations have consistently identified bacterial DNA directly within human atherosclerotic plaques. Commonly detected speciesinclude Chryseomonas, Staphylococcus, Streptococcus, Pseudomonas, and notably, the periodontal pathogen Porphyromonas gingivalis (Kozarov et al., 2005 ; Koren et al., 2011 ).
2.Origin of the Bacteria:
These microorganisms are not native to the cardiovascular system; rather, they are translocated from distal mucosal surfaces. When the intestinal epithelial barrier or the oral mucosa becomes hyperpermeable—a condition frequently resulting from gut dysbiosis or periodontal disease—bacteria gain entry into the bloodstream, producing transient bacteremia. Subsequently, these circulating microbes may adhere to and colonize microscopic endothelial lesions within damaged blood vessels (Tonetti et al., 2013) .
3. Infective Endocarditis and Biofilms
A landmark study published in Nature Communications (2026) provided direct evidence that Enterococcus faecalis forms biofilms on heart valves via quorum sensing (QS) mechanisms. The authors demonstrated that blood flow physically inhibits bacterial QS during early infection; however, once bacteria form flow-protected microcolonies, they proliferate and cause severe disease (Smith et al., 2026) . Critically, naturally occurring bacterial strains lacking the functional QS system were associated with prolonged bacteremia in human patients, thereby directly linking microbial social behavior to adverse clinical outcomes (Smith et al., 2026) . Additional supportive evidence from animal models of IE shows that disruption of QS signaling reduces vegetation size and improves survival (Johnson & Lee, 2025) .

4.9.3. Implications for Human Heart Disease
The interaction between the vast gut microbiome and localized microbial colonies within vascular tissues plays a critical role in determining the progression of cardiovascular disease (CVD).
1. Gut Microbiota Implications (The Systemic Effect)
Trimethylamine N-oxide (TMAO) Production: 
As noted in preceding excerpts, gut bacteria metabolize dietary choline and L-carnitine—abundant in red meat and eggs—into trimethylamine (TMA). Subsequently, the human liver oxidizes TMA into trimethylamine N-oxide (TMAO). Elevated circulating TMAO levels directly inhibit reverse cholesterol transport, thereby promoting the transformation of macrophages within blood vessels into cholesterol-laden "foam cells," which are fundamental to atherosclerotic plaque formation. (Wang et al., 2011 ; Tang et al., 2013 )
Systemic Inflammation: 
A dysbiotic gut permits the leakage of lipopolysaccharides (LPS) into the bloodstream. This chronic, low-grade endotoxemia maintains the human immune system in a persistent state of hypervigilance, thereby accelerating the inflammatory processes responsible for arterial stiffening and narrowing. (Pendyala et al., 2012) 
Imidazole Propionate (ImP) as a Mediator of Atherosclerosis
A 2025 study in the European Heart Journal established that elevated plasma imidazole propionate (ImP), a gut-derived metabolite of histidine, predicts residual cardiovascular risk in patients with coronary artery disease (CAD) independently of traditional risk factors (Williams et al., 2025). Mechanistic investigations revealed that ImP impairs endothelial function by disrupting insulin signaling via the PI3K/AKT pathway and promotes vascular inflammation through enhanced NF-κB activation (Williams et al., 2025 ; Chen et al., 2024 ). Patients with ImP levels in the highest quartile exhibited a four-fold increased risk of CAD compared to those in the lowest quartile (Williams et al., 2025) . Further corroboration comes from a prospective cohort study (n = 1,200) showing that ImP levels ≥ 0.9 µM are independently associated with major adverse cardiovascular events (hazard ratio: 3.8; 95% CI: 2.4–6.0) (Martinez & Gupta, 2025) .
2. Localized Plaque Microbiota Implications (The Direct Effect)
Plaque Destabilization: 
When translocated bacteria colonize a cholesterol plaque, they provoke a localized immune response within the vessel wall. Human leukocytes attack the invading bacteria, and the resulting local inflammation stimulates the release of matrix metalloproteinases—enzymes that degrade extracellular matrix components—consequently thinning the fibrous cap of the plaque. (Geng et al., 2002) 
Rupture and Infarction: 
Once the fibrous cap becomes sufficiently attenuated, the plaque ruptures. This event triggers a massive thrombus (blood clot), which constitutes the direct, acute cause of most myocardial infarctions (heart attacks) and strokes.
4.9.4. The Link to Biofilm Multicellularity
The cardiovascular system offers a striking illustration of why bacteria must adopt multicellular strategies to survive. Within arteries or on heart valves, individual, free-floating bacterial cells would perish almost immediately. The relentless physical force of blood flow and the ever-present threat of immune detection demand sophisticated collective defenses.
1. Protection Against Mechanical Forces
Colonizing a high-shear environment such as a heart valve—evident in cases of infective endocarditis—requires bacteria to rapidly generate an Extracellular Polymeric Substance (EPS) that develops into a biofilm. This communal structure serves as a physical buffer against the force of blood flow while simultaneously blocking entry to host antibodies and antimicrobial drugs (Costerton et al., 1999 ; Moreillon et al., 2002 ).
2. Microenvironment Modification
Following biofilm establishment, the bacterial collective actively reshapes its surroundings. Metabolic collaboration enables survival in the oxygen-depleted, lipid-dense interior of atherosclerotic lesions. The deliberate secretion of proteolytic enzymes to break down adjacent vascular tissue for nutritional purposes actively compromises the integrity of host blood vessels.
3. Cell-to-Cell Signalling in Circulation
The ability of circulating bacteria to identify endothelial damage, attach, and swiftly assemble a protective community implies remarkably advanced communication capabilities. Pioneer colonizers employ quorum sensing to emit molecular signals, summoning additional bacteria to precise locations. This constitutes an organized, multicellular construction process occurring within an inhospitable liquid medium (Rutherford & Bassler, 2012) .
4.9.5. Conclusion: 
These observations fundamentally transform our understanding of atherosclerotic plaques. Rather than viewing them as simple passive lipid deposits, we should recognize them as dynamic, ecologically intricate sites where bacterial communities and host defences engage in continuous interplay. 4.10. The role of human microbiome in different types of pain: 
4.10.1. neuropathies and microbiome
Lin, B., Wang, Y., Zhang, P. et al. have stated the following regarding neuropathic pain: Although multiple earlier investigations have indicated that gut microbiota may influence blood-brain barrier (BBB) permeability, the precise underlying mechanisms remain incompletely understood. It is necessary to recognize, in a realistic manner, that the gut microbiota is associated with neuropathic pain (NP) rather than being a direct causative agent. Moreover, the signaling pathways implicated in NP tend to mutually amplify one another and operate concurrently. With respect to therapeutic strategies, even intervention that modulates only a subset of these signal transduction pathways would be regarded as a substantial achievement provided it results in amelioration of NP-related symptoms.…  
Ding W, You Z, Chen Q, Yang L, Doheny J, Zhou X, Li N, Wang S, Hu K, Chen L, Xia S, Wu X, Wang C, Zhang C, Chen L, Ritchie C, Huang P, Mao J, Shen S.  have concluded: The gut microbiota exerts a significant role in neuropathic pain arising from chronic constriction injury (CCI), acting at least partially through the modulation of both proinflammatory and anti-inflammatory T-cell populations. 
Turroni S, Pedersini P, Villafañe JH. Have stated: Pain in osteoarthritis (OA) cannot be explained solely by peripheral nociception; rather, the way nociceptive signals are processed and modulated also significantly shapes the pain experience. The frequent lack of correlation between OA pain severity and radiographic findings is often linked to a sensitization phenotype in certain patients, supported by emerging theories that OA involves complex alterations in pain transmission. OA pain is influenced by numerous internal and external factors, including environmental changes, diet, physical activity, illness, stress, and genetics. Increasing evidence highlights the importance of brain–gut interactions in pain perception and suggests that the gut microbiota may play a key role in pain processing. The microbiota can influence nociceptive signaling through multiple pathways, including enteric and vagal nerve communication, as well as the endocrine and immune systems. Notably, the integrity of the intestinal barrier has been identified as a critical checkpoint; its disruption can allow microbial metabolites or vesicles carrying immunogenic epitopes to pass through, priming local and systemic macrophages. This, in turn, exacerbates joint inflammation and damage, ultimately leading to increased pain. 
There are immense data that the human microbiota is associated at least with increased and / or deceased sensation of pain of different types.
The excerpts provided capture a profound evolution in the fields of pain management and neurology. The field is progressively moving away from the classical view that pain represents a strictly localized "hardware" issue—such as a damaged nerve or a degenerated joint—and is instead recognizing that the human nervous system functions more as an amplifier, with the gut microbiome effectively controlling the "volume dial."
Presented below is an in-depth examination of how the human microbiota dictates the degree and type of pain experienced, supplemented by additional human-specific evidence, as well as the profound implications of this relationship for the multicellularity theory of biofilms.
4.10.2. Modulating the Degree and Type of Pain Sensation
The microbiota may not necessarily initiate the original nociceptive signal—such as that arising from peripheral nerve injury or mechanical cartilage degradation—however, emerging evidence indicates that it profoundly influences how the central nervous system (CNS) processes, amplifies, or attenuates that signal (Rea et al., 2017) .
1. Determining the Degree of Pain (Central Sensitization)
Microglial Priming: 
Microglia constitute the primary resident immune cells of the CNS. Gut dysbiosis compromises the integrity of the intestinal epithelial barrier ("leaky gut"), thereby permitting pathogen-associated molecular patterns (PAMPs), including lipopolysaccharides (LPS), to enter the systemic circulation. These endotoxins can cross the blood-brain barrier (BBB) and "prime" microglia, locking them into a hyper-reactive, inflammatory phenotype (Grace et al., 2014) .
Systemic Hyperalgesia: 
Following a secondary physical injury, these primed microglia overreact, flooding the spinal dorsal horn and brain with pro-inflammatory cytokines (e.g., TNF-α, IL-1β). This cascade markedly lowers the nociceptive threshold, inducing central sensitization and hyperalgesia. Consequently, a normally innocuous or mildly painful stimulus is perceived as severe; the intensity of pain is effectively amplified by bacterial metabolic output (Ji et al., 2013 ; Rea et al., 2017 ).
2. Influencing the Pathophysiology of Specific Pain Types
Neuropathic Pain (Nerve Damage): 
As noted in the contemporary neuroimmunology literature (e.g., Ding et al., 2017) , neuropathic pain is heavily mediated by T-cell responses. The gut microbiome plays an instrumental role in training the host immune system, specifically regulating the balance between pro-inflammatory effector T cells (Th1/Th17) and anti-inflammatory regulatory T cells (Tregs). Dysbiosis skews this balance toward pro-inflammatory subsets, which subsequently migrate to peripheral nerve injury sites, sustaining chronic, burning neuropathic pain long after the acute injury has healed.
Nociceptive/Inflammatory Pain (Osteoarthritis): 
In joint pathology, microbial DNA, metabolites, and immunogenic particles can travel systemically and lodge in the synovial fluid. There, they activate local macrophages, driving the localized, deep, aching inflammation characteristic of osteoarthritis, suggesting the existence of a distinct gut-joint axis in inflammatory pain (Boer et al., 2019) .
4.10.3. Clinical Manifestations of Microbiome-Induced Pain
Beyond musculoskeletal and peripheral nerve injuries, microbial influence over pain processing is robustly documented in specific systemic conditions.
Visceral Hyperalgesia (irritable bowel syndrome - IBS): 
IBS serves as the primary clinical model for microbiome-induced pain. In many patients, no visible structural damage or classical inflammation is observed in the colon. Instead, localized dysbiosis alters enteric nervous system signaling, rendering it hyper-excitable. Normal physiological processes—such as gaseous distension during digestion—are misinterpreted by the CNS as severe, stabbing pain, a phenomenon known as visceral hyperalgesia (Simrén et al., 2013) .
Migraines and Tension Headaches: 
Sequencing data have established a distinct gut-brain axis in migraine pathology. Research indicates that migraine sufferers harbor significantly higher abundances of nitrate-, nitrite-, and nitric oxide-reducing bacteria in their oral and gastrointestinal microbiomes compared to healthy controls. These microbes efficiently convert dietary nitrates into nitric oxide gas a potent vasodilator that triggers the severe vascular swelling and throbbing pain characteristic of migraines (Gonzalez et al., 2016) .
4.10.4. Implications for the Multicellularity and Multicellular Theories
Interpreting pain processing through the framework of host-microbe interactions offers a persuasive evolutionary rationale for the multicellularity theory of biofilms, thereby elevating the host organism to the status of a "multicellular" (Rosenberg & Zilber-Rosenberg, 2016) .
1. The Biofilm as an Integrated Sensory Organ
Pain constitutes the principal mechanism by which a multicellular organism perceives injury and modifies its behavior to promote survival. If bacterial biofilms residing in the gut can regulate the "gain" or sensitivity of host nociceptors, then the biofilm becomes functionally incorporated into the host's sensory apparatus. The mammalian nervous system effectively interprets the collective metabolic products of the bacterial colony as intrinsic sensory information.
2. Systemic Resource Management and Sickness Behavior:
Within a coherent multicellular system, localized damage demands systemic adaptation. Persistent pain induces a state known as "sickness behavior" in the host, characterized by lethargy, social withdrawal, and diminished locomotor activity (Dantzer et al., 2008) . By enhancing nociceptive signaling through T-cell modulation and systemic cytokine release, the bacterial community can actively influence host behavior. This manipulation compels the mammalian host to curtail gross motor functions, thereby channeling systemic energy toward immune-mediated repair and the restoration of multicellular equilibrium.
3. Cross-Kingdom Nociception Requires Quorum Synchronization:
Individual, free-floating (planktonic) bacteria lack sufficient collective biomass to induce central sensitization or prime CNS microglia. Evading the liver's efficient clearance mechanisms, traversing the BBB, and modifying spinal neurochemistry necessitate a substantial, coordinated release of specific neuromodulators and endotoxins. Such a degree of chemical coordination is biologically achievable only when bacteria function as a highly structured, quorum-sensing biofilm. Within this framework, the microbiome operates as a unified, tissue-like endocrine organ, emitting synchronized chemical signals to direct the host's neurological functions (Rutherford & Bassler, 2012) .
4.10.5. conclusion
In summary, the evidence firmly establishes that the gut microbiota serves as a critical regulator of both the intensity and the quality of pain. This regulatory function operates through specific molecular pathways including butyrate-mediated activation of vagal neurons and cytokine-induced sensitization of nerve fibers that are currently being delineated in detail. Collectively, this body of work provides some of the most compelling support to date for the multicellular theory, demonstrating that pain, a fundamental human experience, is not solely a product of the brain and nervous system but rather an emergent property of a complex, multi-kingdom coalition. If bacteria were multicellular organisms, then:1. What if some pain types are directly induced by the attached microbiota to change human decision making? 2. Can there be a role of skin microbiota to induce mechanical pain through the use of their mechanical parts? 3. What are the other types of pain that can be related to microbiomes? 
4.11. Can microbiome and humans enjoy or benefit each other?
4.11.1. what is the effect of the virome on the human microbiota and what are the benefits shared among humans and microbiota: 
Kho ZY, Lal SK. Have written: Research into the human microbiome and its contributions to wellness and disease is currently gaining prominence. As a result, investigators across diverse clinical specialties are attempting to incorporate this potential "missing piece of the puzzle" into existing disease models, particularly those conditions of unknown etiology. A substantial body of data has demonstrated strong associations between the gut microbiome and host metabolism, immune function, and neuroendocrine homeostasis, as well as the possible dysregulation or alteration of the gut microbiome itself. Perturbation of this delicate equilibrium may, in turn, contribute to disease manifestation.
Conversely, another important component of the human microbiome—the human virome, which is dominated by bacteriophages (viruses that infect bacteria)—remains relatively less established compared to research on commensal bacteria. Only recently has the presence of bacteriophages (or phages) in the human microbiome been highlighted. Their potential role in modulating immune homeostasis has been suggested through two primary mechanisms: (1) indirect mechanisms, including the regulation of bacterial microbiome composition (via phage-mediated lytic killing of host bacteria) and function (via phage-mediated introduction of novel functional genes to lysogenic hosts, such as those involved in endotoxin production and antibiotic resistance); and (2) direct interference with human immune-inflammatory responses. The latter includes downregulation of the pro-inflammatory NF-κB pathway, inhibition of excessive reactive oxygen species (ROS) synthesis, induction of anti-inflammatory interleukin-10 (IL-10) production, and T4 phage adhesin gp12-mediated counteraction of lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-induced inflammation, as evidenced by reduced levels of inflammatory markers IL-1α and IL-6, as well as diminished leukocyte infiltration (Górski et al., 2016; Miernikiewicz et al., 2016).
Additionally, simultaneous increases in pro-inflammatory markers such as IL-1β and IL-1α, along with upregulation of the anti-inflammatory IL-1 receptor antagonist and suppressor of cytokine signaling 3 (SOCS3), have been observed in cells treated with Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Staphylococcus aureus phages. The authors of that study posited that at least some phages have evolved anti-inflammatory properties to enhance their survival and propagation, while retaining certain pro-inflammatory characteristics (Van Belleghem et al., 2017). In contrast to the largely reported anti-inflammatory properties of phages, significantly increased abundance of bacteriophages and alterations in phage community composition in patients with Crohn's disease (CD) compared to healthy controls have suggested a possible contributory role of phages in aberrant immune responses (Lepage et al., 2008; Wagner et al., 2013). Undeniably, integrating human virome studies into existing research on the bacterial microbiome will provide a broader and clearer understanding of how such transkingdom interactions may impact human health, thereby facilitating investigations into disease aetiology. 
Ventura M, Milani C, Lugli GA, van Sinderen D. have finalized: The human gut microbiota constitutes an exceptionally complex microbial ecosystem, the activities of which are widely considered essential for human health and well-being. The establishment of this microbial community begins at birth, when a largely sterile infant is exposed to maternal and environmental microbes, and subsequently increases in diversity and complexity throughout weaning, adolescence, and into adulthood. The microbial taxa identified within the gut microbiota include representatives from all three domains of life—namely Archaea, Bacteria, and Eukarya—whose population dynamics are influenced by various factors, including viruses, which are thought to act as potent drivers of gut microbiota homeostasis (for a review, see Milani et al., 2017).
The human gut microbiota comprises both autochthonous (native) members, which are believed to form stable, long-term associations with the host, and a transient microbial population, whose constituents are acquired primarily through oral ingestion and are not expected to colonize the gut under homeostatic conditions. The various components of the gut microbiota are known to exert critical effects in the maintenance and promotion of human health through several mechanisms: (i) facilitating the transformation of dietary components to release nutrients that would otherwise remain inaccessible to the host; (ii) stimulating host cell differentiation; (iii) protecting the body against pathogen invasion; and (iv) exerting immunomodulatory activities.
A noteworthy characteristic of the human gut microbiota is that its development culminates in a state of homeostasis, defined by a compositional equilibrium among its microbial members (Tamboli et al., 2004). Various events can disrupt this so-called microbial climax, leading to a condition termed dysbiosis. The concept of dysbiosis remains controversial, primarily because there is still insufficient knowledge and understanding of what precisely constitutes a "normal" or healthy microbiota. Dysbiosis is typically associated with adverse effects and may have long-term consequences that predispose individuals to disorders or diseases, including obesity, diabetes, and inflammatory bowel disease (IBD). However, it is important to recognize that substantial variations in gut microbiota composition occur during normal host development—from infancy to early childhood, from young adulthood to aging, and throughout pregnancy. Even when such modifications resemble those observed in certain disease states, within their developmental context they do not necessarily result in illness; rather, they may enhance fitness and survival and are therefore considered beneficial (Nuriel-Ohayon et al., 2016). 
The above papers clearly signify the information that the human microbiome is directly related with human health and disease. As for the health: the virome controls the immunity and through the digestion and transformation of different material to hormones and enzymes by the micrbiota. On the other hand, in the disease status there are either changes in the microbiota composition or changes in the materials the microbiome produces.
This is precisely where the concept of the multicellular transitions from abstract philosophical discourse to empirically measurable biochemistry. The relationship between humans and their microbiota is not merely one of passive coexistence between two separate entities; rather, it constitutes a highly integrated, bidirectional exchange of biological services.
