When constructing arguments in favor of the multicellularity of bacteria and biofilms, defining this mutualistic exchange is of critical importance. It demonstrates that the biofilm does not simply reside within the host but is, in fact, functionally interwoven with the host's cellular machinery.
Presented below is a detailed examination of the specific chemical and environmental benefits exchanged between humans and their microbiota, along with an explanation of how this dynamic relationship reinforces the multicellularity theory.
4.11.2. Microbial Contributions to Host Physiology: The Metabolic Engine
The human genome is metabolically constrained. To maintain homeostasis, the host depends on the extensive genetic repertoire of the gut microbiome to produce essential enzymes, signalling molecules, and metabolic precursors.
Neuromodulation and Serotonin Regulation:
 Gut microbiota play a vital role in regulating the host's central and enteric nervous systems. Although bacteria produce some tryptophan, their primary contribution to serotonin synthesis involves the secretion of metabolites—such as short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs) and secondary bile acids—that chemically signal the host's enterochromaffin (EC) cells. In response to these microbial signals, host EC cells generate over 90% of the body's circulating serotonin (Yano et al., 2015) .
Incretin Hormone Modulation: 
Bacterial fermentation of dietary fiber yields SCFAs (e.g., butyrate, propionate, and acetate). These metabolites bind to G-protein-coupled receptors (specifically FFAR2 and FFAR3) on human enteroendocrine L-cells, thereby stimulating the release of glucagon-like peptide-1 (GLP-1) and peptide YY (PYY). This microbially mediated endocrine signaling is essential for host insulin regulation and satiety (Tolhurst et al., 2012) .
Essential Vitamin Synthesis: 
The mammalian genome lacks the enzymatic pathways necessary for synthesizing several life-sustaining vitamins. The colonic biofilm functions as an endogenous vitamin factory, producing vitamin K2 (menaquinone) and a range of B vitamins (including B12, folate, riboflavin, and biotin), which are continuously absorbed across the intestinal epithelium to support host cellular metabolism (LeBlanc et al., 2013) .
Carbohydrate-Active Enzymes (CAZymes): 
The human digestive tract secretes a limited array of enzymes for breaking down complex carbohydrates. In contrast, the gut microbiome encodes tens of thousands of CAZymes. Without this collective bacterial metabolism acting as an outsourced digestive organ, the host would be incapable of extracting energy from most plant-derived polysaccharides (Flint et al., 2012) .
Bile Acid Transformation: 
The human liver synthesizes primary bile acids to facilitate lipid digestion. However, it relies on gut bacteria expressing bile salt hydrolases to deconjugate and convert these into secondary bile acids. These microbially modified acids function as systemic endocrine molecules, binding to host receptors (such as FXR and TGR5) to regulate host lipid and glucose metabolism (Wahlström et al., 2016) .
4.11.4. Host Contributions to Microbial Fitness: The Engineered Biosphere
Symbiosis is inherently bidirectional. From the perspective of the microbial biofilm, the human host functions as a meticulously engineered, well-defended, and mobile biosphere designed to ensure the colony's survival.
Constant Nutrient Provisioning: 
The human host actively forages, delivering a consistent influx of diverse substrates. Importantly, during periods of host fasting, the human intestinal epithelium continuously secretes specialized glycoproteins known as mucins. These mucins serve as a dedicated, endogenous nutrient source, sustaining beneficial, mucin-degrading bacteria (such as Akkermansia muciniphila) during times of dietary scarcity (Derrien et al., 2004) .
Thermal and Anaerobic Stability: 
The mammalian host expends considerable metabolic energy to maintain a stable internal temperature (approximately 37°C). Furthermore, the spatial organization of the colon preserves a strict hypoxic (low-oxygen) gradient. This tightly regulated microenvironment enables obligate anaerobes—which would be eliminated if exposed to atmospheric oxygen—to thrive safely (Zheng et al., 2015) .
Targeted Immune Shielding (Secretory IgA): 
Although immunoglobulins typically function to clear pathogens, secretory immunoglobulin A (sIgA) in the gut serves a dual purpose. The host immune system secretes large quantities of sIgA that selectively coat commensal bacteria. Rather than destroying them, this binding anchors the bacteria to the mucosal layer, actively protecting the beneficial biofilm from competing pathogenic invaders and preventing immune-mediated clearance (Donaldson et al., 2018) .
Mobility and Geographic Dispersal: 
By optimizing the host's metabolic and neurological health, the microbial colony ensures its own geographic dissemination. The human acts as an advanced transport vector, shedding and spreading bacterial progeny to new environments, surfaces, and other human hosts.
4.11.4. Implications for the Microbial Multicellularity Theory
The sophisticated functional integration detailed above renders it conceptually challenging to regard gut bacteria merely as isolated, single-celled organisms.
1. The Biofilm as a Differentiated Organ: 
In classical eukaryotic multicellularity, distinct organs differentiate to perform specialized tasks (e.g., the liver processes toxins while muscles enable locomotion). The gut microbiome exhibits an analogous division of labor. It has differentiated its functions to execute complex biochemical processes (vitamin synthesis, CAZyme production), relying on the broader "superorganism" (the human) for thermoregulation and macro-foraging. Clinically, the microbiome is increasingly characterized as a "virtual endocrine organ" (O'Hara & Shanahan, 2006) .
2. Cross-Kingdom Quorum Sensing: 
For a bacterial biofilm to precisely stimulate host GLP-1 release or modulate host T-cell ratios, it must continuously monitor systemic host requirements. Evidence indicates that bacterial quorum-sensing networks are capable of "listening" to eukaryotic chemical signals, such as host stress hormones (catecholamines), and adjusting their virulence or metabolic output accordingly (Sperandio et al., 2003) . The bacterial collective operates as a synchronized tissue layer engaging in real-time communication with surrounding host tissues.
3. The Virome as an Integrated Defense System: 
Recent models propose that bacteriophages (the virome) provide a structural defense layer for the bacterial biofilm. The Bacteriophage Adherence to Mucus (BAM) model demonstrates that phages bind to host mucins, selectively lysing invasive competing bacteria while sparing commensals (Barr et al., 2013) . Furthermore, temperate phages transfer beneficial, fitness-enhancing genes to host-associated bacteria. Within this framework, the virome functions as an adaptive, decentralized immune system for the biofilm community.
4.11.5. Conclusion
When considered from the perspectives of metabolic codependence, targeted immune shielding, and inter-kingdom signaling, the physiological distinction between "host" and "microbe" becomes increasingly blurred. Although they originate from distinct genetic lineages, humans and their associated microorganisms have functionally converged into a single, highly coordinated, multicellular operating system. At the core of this unified entity lies a mutualistic contract: the human host provides shelter and nutrition, while the microbial community supplies essential metabolic and regulatory services. The virome adds yet another critical layer of regulation, and a developmental perspective reveals that this association constitutes a lifelong partnership. Taken together, these insights transform the traditional view of humans as isolated, single-species individuals into a recognition of humans as densely populated, multi-kingdom ecosystems thereby solidifying the concept of the human as a holobiont. If bacteria were multicellular from this perspective, then: 1. Are there humans where they do some types of rituals to increase the benefits they get from the microbiome? 2.To what extent the actions of the humans are benefits for the microbiome? 3.What about the other microbiota inside the human’s body, what are the extent of their benefits to the humans and vice versa?
4.12. What are other ways that microbiome affect humans other than infection and psychiatric diseases and direct effect?
4.12.1. some other diseases and the microbiome: 
Edry E, Lamprecht R, Wagner S, Rosenblum K, have discussed: Elucidating the mechanisms by which the central nervous system (CNS) operates represents one of the most formidable challenges confronting contemporary life sciences and medicine. The study of the brain is particularly demanding due to its inherent complexity, the heterogeneity of its cellular composition, and the significant changes it undergoes across the lifespan. The intricacy of adult brain neural networks also arises from the diverse properties and functions of neuronal cells, which are governed, among other factors, by temporally and spatially differential patterns of protein expression within mammalian brain cell populations. Consequently, investigations into the biology of CNS activity and its implications for human and animal behavior necessitate the application of novel scientific methodologies.
One source of such innovative tools is the discipline of molecular genetics, which has been increasingly employed in neuroscience research in recent years. Transgenic approaches in general, and gene targeting techniques in rodents in particular, have become fundamental instruments for deciphering gene function within the CNS. Although remarkable progress has been achieved over the past several decades through the application of these methods, it is important to recognize that they are subject to certain limitations. A principal challenge concerns the temporal and spatial regulation of introduced genetic modifications.
Viral vectors offer an alternative strategy for achieving temporally regulated, site-specific delivery of genetic modifications to neurons. This review describes the available technologies for gene transfer into the adult mammalian CNS, encompassing both viral and non-viral tools. The discussion focuses on viral vectors commonly utilized in neuroscience, with particular emphasis on lentiviral vector (LV) systems. The review considers the adverse effects associated with LVs, as well as their application for temporally and spatially controllable genetic manipulations. Notably, the significance of viral vector-mediated genetic manipulations in the study of learning and memory processes is highlighted, including their effective use in dissecting the distinct phases of learning: acquisition, consolidation, retrieval, and maintenance. 
Allen J, Sears CL. have elucidated: in recent years, the number of investigations examining the role of the gut microbiome in colorectal cancer (CRC) has increased substantially. Consequently, it is now established that various microorganisms and microbial communities are detected with greater frequency in the stool and mucosal tissues of CRC patients compared to healthy controls—not only within primary tumors but also in distant metastatic sites. Furthermore, evidence indicates that these microbes can induce tumor formation in diverse mouse models. However, relatively little is understood regarding their direct effects on colon epithelial cells (CECs) or how such interactions might lead to genetic and epigenetic alterations that initiate and sustain tumor progression.
The incidence of CRC is rising among younger adults, and the disease remains the second leading cause of cancer-related mortality worldwide. Therefore, a more comprehensive understanding of the contribution of gut microbes to CRC pathogenesis is urgently needed. This review summarizes recent progress in elucidating the impact of gut microorganisms on the genome and epigenome of CECs in the context of CRC.
Overall, numerous studies published within the past several years have conclusively demonstrated that gut microbes exert distinct effects on DNA damage, DNA methylation patterns, chromatin architecture, and non-coding RNA expression within CECs. Several of the genes and signaling pathways modified by gut microbes have been implicated in CRC development, most notably those involved in cell proliferation and WNT signaling. To advance the field, it is necessary to implement more standardized analytical protocols, integrate data across multiple studies, and utilize murine models of CRC to better evaluate these microbial effects, elucidate their functional significance, and translate this knowledge into improved patient care. 
Chen, C., Wang, M., Zhu, Z. et al. have revealed: Introduction
Since 2013, influenza A (H7N9) virus infection has been associated with high mortality rates. Elucidating the potential genetic variations that contribute to host susceptibility to infection is therefore of critical importance.
Methods and Findings
To identify genetic mutations that may increase host vulnerability to H7N9 infection, the researchers performed exon sequencing followed by validation of single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) using Sanger sequencing in a cohort of 18 patients confirmed to have H7N9 infection. Blood specimens were obtained from these 18 confirmed cases. Genomic DNA was captured using the Agilent SureSelect Human All Exon kit, sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform, and the resulting data were processed and annotated with the Genome Analysis Tool Kit. Identified SNPs were subsequently verified through independent Sanger sequencing. Bioinformatics analyses were conducted using the DAVID and DAPPLE databases.
Through exon sequencing and confirmatory Sanger sequencing, the study identified 21 genes strongly associated with susceptibility to H7N9 influenza infection. Protein-protein interaction analysis revealed that direct interactions among the products of these genes were significantly more frequent than expected by chance (p = 0.004). Furthermore, DAVID analysis confirmed that these genes are enriched for defense-related biological functions. The gene mutation profiles of patients who survived did not differ substantially from those who did not survive, suggesting that at least some of the genes identified in this investigation may be associated with H7N9 influenza susceptibility rather than with disease outcome.
Conclusion
Host-specific genetic determinants of disease severity identified through this approach may offer novel targets for the treatment of H7N9 influenza. 
The above papers show that there is another method by which microbiomes and biofilms affect disease appearance which is genomic translocation of their genetic materials.
This synthesis positions the field at the leading edge of host–microbiome research. The shift from metabolite-based chemical signaling to direct genetic modulation is recognized as a significant conceptual advance. To refine the terminology before proceeding: the phenomenon described is both real and well documented, though it is typically referred to by biologists as horizontal gene transfer (HGT), viral integration, or epigenetic modification, rather than as "genomic translocation." In standard genetic terminology, "genomic translocation" is understood to denote a structural chromosomal abnormality in which segments are exchanged between nonhomologous host chromosomes.
The Hologenome Paradigm: Microbial and Viral Modulation of Host Genetics
The conceptual framework of the hologenome posits that a host and its symbiotic microbial communities function as a unified evolutionary and physiological entity. Emerging evidence demonstrates that the microbiota and the virome actively modulate the host's genetic architecture and expression profile. By orchestrating trans-kingdom genetic interference, these microbial communities challenge the traditional dichotomy of "host" versus "pathogen," functioning instead as a sophisticated, multicellular consortium capable of reshaping host cellular programming to construct customized, nutrient-dense niches. This framework provides mechanistic explanations for the onset of various oncological and autoimmune conditions.
4.12.2. Mechanisms of Trans-Kingdom Genetic Interference
The modulation of host genetics by microbial and viral populations occurs through both direct physical integration of exogenous genetic sequences and indirect epigenetic or chemical manipulation of host DNA.
Direct Mechanisms: Viral Integration and Mutagenesis:
Viruses frequently act as vectors for genetic engineering within the host. Insertional mutagenesis, wherein viral elements physically splice into the host genome, is a fundamental driver of evolutionary biology. Notably, approximately 8% of the modern human genome consists of human endogenous retroviruses (HERVs), which are ancient viral sequences that integrated into the ancestral germline and became permanently fixed in the human genetic code (Stein & DePaola, 2023) .
Indirect Mechanisms: Genotoxins and Epigenetic Reprogramming:
While bacteria do not typically integrate their genomic material directly into human chromosomes, pathogenic strains deploy specialized secondary metabolites to alter host genetics. Certain bacteria secrete genotoxins, such as colibactin, a polyketide-peptide hybrid produced by Escherichia coli harboring the pks pathogenicity island. Colibactin actively induces DNA double-strand breaks in host cells, generating genomic aberrations and transformation phenotypes that precede malignant cellular proliferation (Iftekhar et al., 2021) .
Furthermore, the microbiome exerts profound epigenetic control over host transcription. Microbes dictate gene expression through the secretion of metabolites, such as short-chain fatty acids (e.g., butyrate), which act as histone deacetylase (HDAC) inhibitors, thereby altering chromatin accessibility and modulating immune and proliferative pathways.
4.12.3. Pathogenic Manifestations of Microbial Genetic Hijacking
When microbial communities deploy these genetic and epigenetic mechanisms, they frequently drive oncogenesis and severe chronic inflammatory states by overriding host regulatory checkpoints.
Genotoxin-Induced Malignancies:
The continuous localized secretion of genotoxins by polymicrobial biofilms directly precipitates cellular transformation. In colorectal cancer (CRC), pks+ strains of E. coli utilize colibactin to rapidly cause genomic aberrations in primary colon cells, circumventing proper DNA damage repair and leading to aneuploidy (Iftekhar et al., 2021) . Concurrently, enterotoxigenic Bacteroides fragilis (ETBF) secretes the B. fragilis toxin (BFT), a metalloprotease that induces the ectodomain cleavage of host E-cadherin, triggering the β-catenin/NF-κB signaling pathways and promoting extensive cellular proliferation and inflammation (Lee et al., 2022) .
Epigenetic Reprogramming in Oncogenesis:
 Pathogenic microbes also alter host phenotypes through epigenetic silencing. A classic example is Helicobacter pylori, which utilizes a Type IV secretion system to inject the cytotoxin-associated gene A (CagA) protein into gastric epithelial cells. Inside the host cell, CagA significantly downregulates the expression of tumor suppressor genes, such as MGMT, by upregulating DNA methyltransferase 1 (DNMT1) via the AKT-NFκB pathway, thereby inducing promoter hypermethylation and driving gastric carcinogenesis (Zhang et al., 2016) .
4.12.4. The Autoimmune Paradigm: Systemic Lupus Erythematosus (SLE)
Systemic Lupus Erythematosus (SLE) exemplifies how microbial dysbiosis fundamentally corrupts the host's immunological recognition software, precipitating an attack on native DNA and nucleoproteins. This process is driven by synergistic bacterial and viral mechanisms.
Bacterial Drivers: Molecular Mimicry and Translocation:
 Severe SLE flares, such as lupus nephritis, are frequently accompanied by significant alterations in the intestinal barrier and the expansion of specific pathobionts. Impaired intestinal barrier function and microbial dysbiosis promote the leakage of immunogenic bacterial components that trigger cross-reactive autoantibodies (Pan et al., 2021) . Moreover, specialized gut pathobionts, particularly Enterococcus gallinarum, are capable of breaching the impaired epithelial barrier to translocate into systemic tissues, including the liver and spleen, where they continuously trigger host autoimmune responses and drive the production of autoantibodies characteristic of SLE (Manfredo Vieira et al., 2018) .
Virome Drivers: Epigenetic Master Switches
The virome actively rewires the host's epigenetic landscape to induce autoimmunity. Epstein-Barr virus (EBV) is a well-established environmental trigger for SLE. The EBV nuclear antigen 2 (EBNA2) acts as a transactivating protein that physically occupies nearly half of the established genetic risk loci for SLE and other autoimmune diseases. By extensively rewiring the human chromatin landscape and gene regulatory programs, EBNA2 serves as an epigenetic master switch that establishes gene-environment interactions central to SLE pathogenesis (Harley et al., 2018) .
4.12.5. Synthesis: The Multicellular Biofilm Theory
The interplay between gut bacteria, the virome, and host genetic expression highlights that the physiological boundaries of the individual host are highly permeable. The translocation of E. gallinarum to hepatic niches, the epigenetic reprogramming by H. pylori (Zhang et al., 2016) , and the chromatin remodelling driven by EBV (Harley et al., 2018)  strongly suggest that human tissue operates in cross-kingdom symbiosis with its microbiome. Ultimately, the microbial biofilm, the virome, and the human host function as a highly entangled multicellular network, where alterations in microbial ecology dictate the fundamental expression of host genetics.
4.13. Summary of Chapter Three
Here in this chapter the reader will be briefed more regarding the effects of the attached microbiota on humans. This chapter systematically demonstrates that the attached human microbiome—comprising bacteria, archaea, fungi, and viruses—exerts profound, bidirectional influences across virtually every domain of host physiology, from cognition and emotion to cardiovascular function and genetic integrity.
Cognitive and affective domains (sections 4.1–4.8): Gut microbiota directly modulate memory (both short- and long-term), fatigue, sleep architecture, epilepsy susceptibility, paranoia, anger, depression, fear/phobia, and complex social behaviors. These effects are mediated by microbial metabolites (short-chain fatty acids, neurotransmitters, kynurenine pathway intermediates), vagal signaling, immune modulation, and epigenetic regulation. The evidence suggests that conditions previously considered idiopathic or purely neurological—including Alzheimer's disease, Parkinson's disease, autism spectrum disorder, Tourette syndrome, and epilepsy—have significant gut microbial components.
Cardiovascular and pain domains (sections 4.9–4.10): Microbial metabolites (TMAO, imidazole propionate, secondary bile acids) drive atherosclerosis and coronary artery disease. Bacterial DNA is found within atherosclerotic plaques, and quorum-sensing biofilms colonize heart valves in endocarditis. The microbiome also regulates pain sensitivity through microglial priming, T-cell modulation, and visceral hyperalgesia.
Mutualism and genetic integration (sections 4.11–4.12): The host-microbiome relationship is fundamentally mutualistic: microbes provide essential vitamins, neuromodulators, metabolic enzymes, and immune training, while the host supplies nutrition, thermal stability, and immune shielding. Critically, the microbiome and virome directly modify host genetics through horizontal gene transfer, viral integration, genotoxin-induced mutagenesis, and epigenetic reprogramming. These trans-kingdom interactions drive colorectal and gastric cancers, autoimmune diseases such as systemic lupus erythematosus, and susceptibility to viral infections.
Multicellularity synthesis: Collectively, the evidence reframes the human organism not as an autonomous, single-species individual but as a multicellular—a densely populated, multi-kingdom ecosystem in which health, cognition, behavior, and disease emerge from the integrated activity of human and microbial cells. The boundaries between "self" and "other," "genetic" and "environmental," "neurological" and "microbial" become fundamentally permeable. This paradigm shift carries profound implications for therapeutics: targeting the microbiome (via diet, probiotics, prebiotics, fecal microbiota transplantation, or phage therapy) offers novel strategies for preventing and treating a wide range of neurological, psychiatric, cardiovascular, oncological, and autoimmune conditions.


 
5. Chapter four: Good deeds and bad deeds affect the human gut microbiota 
Here in this chapter the direct aftermath of the actions of human and their behaviors and the relationship to microbiome would be discussed.  The shift to good microbiome and the worse microbiome would be noted with certain ongoing or chronic actions of the humans.
5.1. Fasting and the Microbiome: 
5.1.1. a positive shift in the gut microbiome and health:
The benefits of fasting are a lot and have been demonstrated in plethora of research. The Japanese scholar Yoshinori Ohsumi has won a noble prize demonstrating one benefit.  But, the benefits in regards to the microbiome is the point of our concern and focus. 
Maifeld, A., Bartolomaeus, H., Löber, U. et al have brieved in their paper: In a substudy of the clinical trial NCT02099968, the effects of fasting followed by refeeding on cardiometabolic risk factors induced by a Western diet were investigated. Patients with hypertensive metabolic syndrome underwent either a five-day fast followed by a modified Dietary Approach to Stop Hypertension (DASH) diet or the modified DASH diet alone. At three months post-intervention, the fasting group exhibited greater reductions in systolic blood pressure, antihypertensive medication requirements, and body mass index compared to the control group. Fasting induced compositional changes in the gut microbiome, specifically affecting bacterial taxa and gene modules involved in short-chain fatty acid production. Integrated cross-system analyses demonstrated positive correlations between systolic blood pressure and circulating levels of mucosa-associated invariant T cells, non-classical monocytes, and CD4+ effector T cells. Additionally, regulatory T cell levels were positively correlated with body mass index and weight. Using machine learning models based on baseline immunome or microbiome data, sustained blood pressure responses in the fasting group were accurately predicted. Key predictive features included CD8+ effector T cells, Th17 cells, and regulatory T cells from the immunome, as well as the bacterial taxa Desulfovibrionaceae, Hydrogenoanaerobacterium, Akkermansia, and Ruminococcaceae from the microbiome. Collectively, these high-resolution multi-omics data support fasting as a promising non-pharmacological intervention for managing hypertension in patients 
The connection drawn between Yoshinori Ohsumi’s Nobel Prize-winning work on cellular autophagy and the ecological dynamics of the microbiome is recognized as conceptually elegant. Just as fasting induces a human cell to clear and recycle its damaged intracellular components, fasting is understood to act as a profound ecological reset mechanism for the gut microbiome.
When the intake of external dietary nutrients is abruptly halted by the human host, the microbiome is not merely subjected to starvation; rather, it undergoes a fundamental structural reorganization, shifting toward a highly efficient, health-promoting compositional profile.
The precise mechanisms by which fasting reshapes the microbiome are described below, followed by an explanation of how this nutrient-deprived state is considered to provide some of the most compelling evidence to date for the multicellular organization of biofilms.
5.1.2. The Shift Toward a Homeostatic Microbiome
Dietary fasting is understood to induce a state of selective nutritional deprivation within the gastrointestinal tract. Bacterial populations that are obligately dependent on a continuous influx of dietary carbohydrates—frequently encompassing pro-inflammatory pathobionts—are observed to experience rapid declines. In the absence of these organisms, a specific consortium of resilient, beneficial microbes is found to proliferate, utilizing endogenous host substrates to maintain ecological stability (Earle et al., 2015) .
1. The Proliferation of Akkermansia muciniphila:
Upon the cessation of dietary fiber intake, the microbiome is forced to exploit alternative metabolic substrates. Akkermansia muciniphila, an autochthonous, mucin-degrading bacterium, is observed to exhibit a profound survival advantage during fasting by foraging directly on the host's endogenous mucosal layer (Derrien et al., 2004) .
Enhancement of the Mucosal Barrier: 
Counterintuitively, the degradation of the outer mucus layer by A. muciniphila is understood to act as a localized physiological stressor that stimulates host goblet cells to upregulate mucin synthesis. This feedback loop is ultimately associated with the generation of a thicker, more robust mucosal barrier, thereby reducing intestinal permeability (Everard et al., 2013) .
Microbial Cross-Feeding: 
The enzymatic breakdown of mucin by A. muciniphila is understood to yield oligosaccharides and short-chain fatty acids (SCFAs), predominantly acetate and propionate. These metabolic byproducts are recognized as serving as primary substrates for other commensal anaerobes, such as the butyrate-producing Faecalibacterium prausnitzii. Thus, fasting is seen to initiate a highly efficient, closed-loop microbial cross-feeding economy that sustains the broader beneficial community (Belzer & de Vos, 2012) .
2. Attenuation of Endotoxemia and Systemic Inflammation:
Western dietary patterns, characterized by high fat and sugar content, are understood to promote the proliferation of Gram-negative bacteria that shed lipopolysaccharides (LPS). Fasting is observed to rapidly deplete these populations, directly reducing the translocation of systemic endotoxins into host circulation—a condition known as metabolic endotoxemia. As demonstrated by Maifeld et al. (2021) , this precipitous drop in circulating LPS is associated with the downregulation of systemic host inflammation, as evidenced specifically by a reduction in pro-inflammatory Th17 cell populations, and consequently leads to reductions in host blood pressure and cardiovascular risk.
5.1.3. Implications for the Multicellularity Theory
Observing the microbial response to acute resource deprivation provides compelling evidence for bacterial multicellularity. A disorganized aggregation of planktonic cells would succumb rapidly to starvation. Conversely, the structured adaptation of the microbiome underscores a sophisticated, tissue-like resilience.
1. Biofilm Remodeling and Spatial Reorganization:
 During fasting, the bacterial biofilm is understood to undergo substantial structural and spatial remodeling. As luminal nutrients diminish, the biofilm is thought to rely heavily on quorum-sensing networks to coordinate widespread detachment, migration, and re-attachment closer to the host epithelium in order to access the mucin layer (Earle et al., 2015) . This spatial reorganization is recognized as requiring the precise, collective alteration of gene expression, thereby executing a tissue-level maneuver to ensure the survival of the greater colony.
2. Altruistic Programmed Cell Death (PCD):
 Analogous to the autophagic processes observed in nutrient-deprived eukaryotic cells, a starved bacterial biofilm engages in collective, programmed cell death (PCD) to sustain the colony.
Altruistic Apoptosis: 
Under severe nutrient restriction, specific sub-populations within the biofilm are understood to initiate genetically programmed lysis. By sacrificing themselves, these cells are observed to release intracellular proteins, lipids, and extracellular DNA (eDNA) into the biofilm matrix (Bayles, 2014 ; Webb et al., 2003 ).
Resource Reallocation: 
Surviving bacteria are subsequently understood to scavenge these released macromolecular resources. The colony is thus observed to effectively cannibalize a portion of its own "tissue" in order to preserve the core structural and metabolic integrity of the biofilm. This altruistic resource reallocation is recognized as representing a definitive hallmark of true multicellularity.
3. Coordinated Metabolic Downshifting:
In a cohesive multicellular entity, energy conservation is understood to require a high degree of synchronization to prevent internal ecological collapse. During a fast, the biofilm's quorum-sensing network is observed to instruct the entire colony to downregulate its basal metabolic rate. Rather than competing aggressively for dwindling resources—a behavior that would precipitate the collapse of the localized ecosystem—the biofilm is seen to enter a synchronized, highly efficient state of collective dormancy and cross-feeding (Kolter & Greenberg, 2006 ; Flemming et al., 2016 ).
Ultimately, by subjecting the microbial ecosystem to acute nutritional stress, fasting is recognized as stripping away the biochemical noise of active digestion, thereby revealing the underlying, highly structured, multicellular survival architecture of the biofilm.
5.1.4. Conclusion: 
For the multicellularity theory, fasting research is regarded as perhaps the most elegant demonstration to date. The multicellular is understood as a dynamic, co-evolved system that requires periodic environmental challenges—such as food scarcity—to maintain its health. When these ancient rhythms are disrupted by constant overfeeding, the entire partnership is understood to suffer. Conversely, when such rhythms are restored through fasting, the multicellular is seen to reclaim its balance.
5.2. Microbiome and meditation: 
5.2.1. view of a paper 
There is no direct paper that have investigated the relationship between Muslim prayer and gut microbiome. But we will take advantage of other types of mediations (although the writer is against those other methods).
Househam AM, Peterson CT, Mills PJ, Chopra D. have stated: Psychological stress is understood to typically trigger a fight-or-flight response, characterized by the production of corticotropin-releasing hormone and catecholamines in various parts of the body, which ultimately disturbs the gut microbiota. In the absence of stress, a healthy microbiota is observed to produce short-chain fatty acids that exert anti-inflammatory and antitumor effects. During periods of stress, an altered gut microbial population is found to affect the regulation of microbiome-mediated neurotransmitters as well as gut barrier function. Meditation is recognized as helping to regulate the stress response, thereby suppressing chronic inflammatory states and maintaining healthy gut barrier function. 
This research confirms that meditation will change the gut microbiota in a positive way. 
The inclusion of cognitive interventions such as meditation is recognized as introducing a compelling "top-down" dimension to the research. The progression from physical interventions—including fasting and dietary modification—to entirely psychological practices serves to illustrate the depth of entanglement between the human mind and the microbial ecosystem.
This premise is understood to be grounded in rigorous neurobiology: the conscious regulation of human brainwave activity is observed to physically alter the structural and metabolic composition of bacterial communities residing in the gut.
Presented below is the evidence demonstrating how meditation induces shifts in the microbiome, the biochemical mechanisms underlying these shifts, and the profound implications of these findings for the multicellularity theory of biofilms.
5.2.2. Empirical Evidence of Meditation's Impact on the Microbiome
Meditation is understood to effectively modulate the host's autonomic nervous system, shifting the physiological baseline away from sympathetic (fight-or-flight) dominance and toward parasympathetic (rest-and-digest) activation. This transition is recognized to occur primarily through the enhancement of vagal tone (Breit et al., 2018).
1. Longitudinal Evidence: The Tibetan Monk Cohort:
 A landmark 2023 cross-sectional study published in General Psychiatry evaluated the fecal microbiomes of Tibetan Buddhist monks engaged in long-term deep meditation. These were compared to those of age- and environment-matched non-meditating residents, a design that controlled for dietary and geographical variables (Sun et al., 2023).
Compositional Findings: 
The meditating cohort was observed to exhibit significantly enriched populations of Bacteroidetes and Prevotella, alongside substantial expansions of Faecalibacterium and Megamonas.

Clinical Efficacy:
 These compositional shifts were found to correlate with an upregulated metabolic capacity for short-chain fatty acid (SCFA) synthesis and anti-inflammatory pathways. Consequently, the meditating monks presented with significantly lower clinical biomarkers of systemic inflammation and cardiovascular risk (Sun et al., 2023).
2. Attenuation of Host-Derived Virulence Signals: 
 Psychological stress precipitates the adrenal release of catecholamines (e.g., epinephrine and norepinephrine) into systemic circulation and the gastrointestinal lumen.
Inter-Kingdom Signaling: 
Pathogenic enteric bacteria, including Escherichia coli and Salmonella enterica, are understood to utilize specialized membrane-bound receptors—notably the QseC sensor kinase—to detect host catecholamines (Sperandio et al., 2003).
Virulence Induction: 
Upon detecting elevated host stress hormones, these pathogens are understood to interpret the signal as an indication of host immunosuppression. This detection is observed to trigger rapid proliferation, biofilm dispersion, and the expression of highly virulent planktonic phenotypes (Hughes & Sperandio, 2008).
The Meditative Shift: 
By consciously mitigating systemic stress, meditation is understood to significantly reduce circulating catecholamine levels, thereby depriving opportunistic pathogens of the chemical cues required to initiate virulent gene expression (Lyte, 2014).
5.2.3. Engineering a Homeostatic Microbiome Environment
Mind-body interventions do not merely suppress pathogenic virulence; they actively engineer a gastrointestinal microenvironment conducive to commensal proliferation.
Restoration of Gut Motility: 
Chronic stress induces erratic intestinal peristalsis and chaotic transit times, which destabilize the mucosal interface required for commensal biofilm attachment (Mayer, 2011). Enhanced parasympathetic tone via meditation normalizes enteric rhythmic contractions, providing a stable physical scaffold for beneficial biofilms to mature.
Oxygen and pH Homeostasis: 
Sympathetic activation diverts splanchnic blood flow to skeletal muscle, inducing transient localized ischemia and altering luminal pH. Meditation restores homeostatic mucosal perfusion, maintaining the precise anaerobic gradients and pH parameters required by obligate anaerobes—the primary SCFA producers—to survive and effectively ferment dietary polysaccharides (Zheng et al., 2015).
5.2.4. Implications for the Theory of Microbial Multicellularity
Integrating mind-body interventions into the framework of bacterial multicellularity yields a profound conclusion: the human central nervous system acts as an overarching regulatory node for the microbial collective.
1. Cross-Kingdom Quorum Sensing (Top-Down Control):
 Quorum sensing constitutes the communicative framework of bacterial multicellularity. The capacity of bacteria to intercept and respond to host neuroendocrine hormones indicates that the "quorum" extends trans-kingdom to include the host (Hughes & Sperandio, 2008). Through meditation, the host consciously manipulates the systemic chemical signaling environment. This intentional regulation acts as a top-down form of quorum quenching—suppressing the host-derived chemical alarms that would otherwise induce the multicellular bacterial tissue to fracture into virulent, planktonic states.
2. The Multicellular Biofeedback Loop
 Meditation establishes a mutually reinforcing, bidirectional symbiotic loop:
1.	The host exerts conscious cognitive regulation to enhance vagal tone and attenuate sympathetic output.
2.	This stable, low-stress gastrointestinal environment permits the bacterial biofilm to organize into a healthy, structured multicellular entity.
3.	The mature biofilm subsequently upregulates the synthesis of gamma-aminobutyric acid (GABA), serotonin precursors, and SCFAs (Strandwitz et al., 2019).
4.	These microbial metabolites exert afferent signaling via the vagus nerve, chemically reinforcing the anxiolytic, meditative state within the host's central nervous system (Bravo et al., 2011).
In this paradigm, the multicellular biofilm and the human brain operate as an integrated, synchronized neuroendocrine circuit.
3. The Conscious Regulatory Override:
 Conceptualizing the human holobiont as a shared ecological matrix driven by a hologenome, meditation demonstrates that human consciousness retains a master regulatory capacity. While the multicellular bacterial colony exerts profound bottom-up influence over host cravings, mood, and nociception, the human host utilizes conscious awareness to manipulate the foundational physical parameters of the ecosystem (e.g., perfusion, motility, and endocrinology). This conscious top-down regulation forces the microbial multicellularity to adapt to a state of systemic homeostasis rather than pathogenic dysbiosis.
5.2.5. Conclusion: 
For the multicellularity theory, meditation research is regarded as perhaps the most elegant demonstration to date. The multicellular is understood to include not only the body and its resident microbes but also the mind. The conscious practice of meditation is observed to reshape the microbial ecosystem, which in turn is recognized to influence mood, stress resilience, and inflammation. This is understood as a true partnership—a "consensual we"—in which mental states, physiological responses, and microbial communities are continuously co-regulated.
As noted by one expert, the gut-brain-microbiome axis remains an emerging field of study. It is acknowledged that understanding of this relationship continues to grow and that such investigation is necessary given the considerable diversity of microbial colonies and the fact that their precise roles have not yet been fully characterized. Nevertheless, the direction of the evidence is recognized as clear: meditation is understood to offer a non-pharmacological means of nurturing the inner microbial partners.
5.3. Microbiota and physical activity:
5.3.1. brief review of the evidences:
Monda V, et al have summarized: Collectively, the available data are understood to strongly support that, in addition to other well-known internal and external factors, exercise appears to represent an environmental factor capable of determining changes in the qualitative and quantitative composition of the gut microbiota, with potential benefits for the host. Specifically, stable and enriched microbial diversity is recognized as indispensable for homeostasis and normal gut physiology, thereby contributing to appropriate signaling along the brain–gut axis and to the healthy status of the individual.
Exercise is observed to enrich microbial diversity; to improve the Bacteroidetes–Firmicutes ratio, which may potentially contribute to weight reduction and to the alleviation of obesity-associated pathologies and gastrointestinal disorders; to stimulate the proliferation of bacteria capable of modulating mucosal immunity and improving barrier functions, resulting in a reduced incidence of obesity and metabolic diseases; and to stimulate bacteria capable of producing substances—such as short-chain fatty acids—that are understood to protect against gastrointestinal disorders and colon cancer. Consequently, exercise is regarded as a potential therapeutic intervention for maintaining microbial balance or for correcting existing dysbiosis, thereby achieving an improvement in health status.  
Physical activities modulate the gut, skin and genital microbiota.
It has been suggested that this represents a remarkable angle for further exploration. When physical exercise is introduced into the experimental framework, the microbiome's response to intense, systemic physical and metabolic stress is examined. Exercise has been shown to alter the host's core temperature, blood flow, hormone levels, and mechanical movement.
The observation that bacterial ecosystems across the entire human body respond positively to host movement has been interpreted as some of the most striking evidence for a highly integrated, cross-kingdom metabolic grid. In the following analysis, a breakdown is provided of the specific microbiota affected, the manner in which this shift occurs across multiple body sites (including the gut, skin, and genitals), the supporting human evidence, and the profound implications for the multicellularity theory.
5.3.2. Gut Microbiota Enriched by Physical Activity
Regular physical activity has been consistently linked to increased alpha diversity in the gut microbiota. In more specific terms, exercise exerts selective pressures that favor the enrichment of bacterial taxa known to be specialized in energy metabolism, tissue repair, and mucosal barrier defense (Bressa et al., 2017) .
Veillonella atypica (The Metabolic Sink): 
Identified as a notable performance-related microorganism, Veillonella atypica does not rely on carbohydrate fermentation; instead, it uses circulating lactate derived from the host as its main carbon source. Through the metabolism of systemic lactic acid generated by active skeletal muscles, this bacterium serves as an essential metabolic sink (Scheiman et al., 2019) .
Akkermansia muciniphila: 
Often found at reduced levels in metabolic syndrome and obesity, this mucin-degrading bacterium has been shown to be more abundant in physically active populations. Its metabolic activity promotes goblet cell mucin secretion, which in turn strengthens the intestinal epithelial barrier and reduces systemic inflammation (Clarke et al., 2014) .
Faecalibacterium prausnitzii: 
As a primary producer of the short-chain fatty acid (SCFA) butyrate, F. prausnitzii provides direct energetic substrates to colonocytes while simultaneously suppressing mucosal pro-inflammatory cytokine pathways. Exercise reliably upregulates its population density (Mailing et al., 2019) .
Bifidobacterium and Lactobacillus: 
Regular aerobic conditioning expands these foundational lactic-acid-producing genera, which are strongly correlated with enhanced host immune surveillance, pathogen exclusion, and the modulation of the gut-brain axis, potentially mitigating host anxiety and depressive phenotypes (Bressa et al., 2017) .
5.3.3. Multi-Systemic Microbiome Modulation
The ecological benefits of physical activity are not confined to the gastrointestinal tract. Because exercise induces profound shifts in systemic physiology, it remodels microbial ecosystems across diverse anatomical niches.
1. The Cutaneous (Skin) Microbiome
The Mechanism: 
Physical exertion leads to thermoregulatory sweating, which fundamentally changes the physicochemical landscape of the epidermis. Sweat alters the local pH, raises salinity, and supplies host-derived antimicrobial peptides—including dermcidin—directly to the stratum corneum (Byrd et al., 2018). .
The Ecological Shift: 
This saline and antimicrobial flush functions as a selective force, inhibiting opportunistic pathogens such as Staphylococcus aureus while promoting the growth of salt-tolerant commensal biofilms composed mainly of Staphylococcus epidermidis. In response, S. epidermidis produces its own antimicrobial bacteriocins, thereby strengthening local barrier immunity and providing protection against pathogenic invasion (Byrd et al., 2018). .
2. The Vaginal/Urogenital Microbiome
The Mechanism: 
The vaginal ecosystem is highly sensitive to fluctuations in host endocrine activity—most notably circulating estrogen levels—as well as to systemic inflammatory status. Sedentary behaviors combined with central adiposity have been associated with elevated systemic pro-inflammatory markers, which can disrupt this delicate microenvironment (Ravel et al., 2011). .
The Ecological Shift: 
Regular physical activity mitigates chronic, low-grade systemic inflammation and supports homeostatic estrogen cycling. This systemic stability fosters an acidic, Lactobacillus-dominant vaginal biofilm, which acts as a formidable biological shield against opportunistic dysbiosis, including bacterial vaginosis and vulvovaginal candidiasis.
3. The Gastrointestinal Microbiome
The Mechanism: 
Exercise has been shown to increase vagal tone, which in turn enhances intestinal motility, and to promote the hepatic secretion of bile acids, thereby facilitating lipid metabolism. This exercise-induced elevation in peristalsis and bile acid flow helps prevent bacterial stasis and overgrowth (such as small intestinal bacterial overgrowth, SIBO) while selectively supporting the expansion of resilient, short-chain fatty acid (SCFA)-producing microbial networks (Mailing et al., 2019). .
5.3.4. Empirical Evidence in Human Cohorts
The clinical evidence linking physical activity to optimal microbial architecture is particularly robust when examining cohorts subjected to extreme physiological exertion.
The Elite Rugby Cohort (Clarke et al., 2014) : 
A landmark cross-sectional study compared the microbiomes of elite professional rugby players to those of size-matched, sedentary controls. The athletic cohort demonstrated significantly superior microbial diversity and marked expansions of Akkermansia muciniphila. This established that extreme physical training, coupled with dietary adaptations, fundamentally reorganizes host gut ecology.
The Marathon Endurance Discovery (Scheiman et al., 2019) :
Utilizing meta-omics, researchers profiled the microbiomes of athletes before and after the Boston Marathon. The analysis revealed a significant post-race bloom of Veillonella atypica. Mechanistic follow-up demonstrated that host muscle-derived circulating lactate crossed the gut lumen, where the Veillonella biofilm metabolized it into propionate. This microbially derived propionate then returned to the host bloodstream, actively enhancing the runners' muscular endurance.
5.3.5. Implications for the Multicellularity Theories
Integrating exercise physiology into the framework of biofilm multicellularity elevates commensal bacteria from passive symbionts to active, highly integrated components of the host's musculoskeletal and metabolic networks.
1. The Shared Metabolic Engine
 The discovery regarding Veillonella serves as the ultimate validation of a cross-kingdom metabolic grid. As the host generates a potentially rate-limiting metabolic byproduct (lactate) during locomotion, the bacterial biofilm functions as an outsourced, multicellular "liver." It filters the metabolite from circulation, converts it, and supplies a high-efficiency energetic substrate (propionate) back to the host's musculature. This illustrates that the biofilm is functionally integrated into the human energy cycle, operating as a specialized, multicellular organ dedicated to sustaining host motility (Scheiman et al., 2019  ; Rosenberg & Zilber-Rosenberg, 2016 ).
2. Mechanotransduction and Biofilm Adaptation
 During vigorous physical activity, the human gastrointestinal tract is subjected to intense mechanical perturbation, transient ischemia (due to the splanchnic shunting of blood to skeletal muscles), and elevated core temperatures (often approaching 39°C). To survive this severe mechanical and thermal stress, bacterial colonies cannot exist as disorganized, planktonic cells. They must utilize quorum-sensing networks to rapidly remodel their Extracellular Polymeric Substance (EPS) matrix—the structural scaffold of the biofilm—to maintain secure mucosal adherence (Flemming et al., 2016) . The biofilm adapts to physical stress as a unified, cohesive tissue layer.
3. The Evolutionary "Mecha" Maintenance Protocol
 From an evolutionary perspective, host locomotion is an obligatory requirement for foraging, hunting, and survival. When the host exercises, it signals to the resident biofilms that the biological "mecha" (the host) is active and functioning optimally. The multicellular microbial colonies respond to this locomotive state by upregulating the synthesis of health-promoting metabolites (e.g., butyrate, bacteriocins), which actively repair host tissues and suppress inflammation. This evolutionary feedback loop ensures that the microbes' mobile biosphere remains in peak physiological condition.
Ultimately, athletic endurance and physiological optimization represent an emergent property of the holobiont. The athlete's body is not merely a triumph of human genetics and training; it is a multicellular achievement, inextricably dependent upon trillions of microbial partners that metabolically and structurally adapt alongside their host.

5.4. Carnivores and herbivores microbiota:
5.4.1. An intro: 
Zoelzer, F., Burger, A.L. & Dierkes, P.W. have found:  Our study revealed notable differences in the consistency and diversity of fecal microbiota between carnivorous and herbivorous species. While Carnivora exhibited highly variable and inconsistent microbial communities both within and across species, Perissodactyla and Ruminantia showed greater similarity in microbiota across species boundaries. Additionally, low-abundance bacterial families displayed more pronounced fluctuations in fecal samples than high-abundance ones.
Conclusions
Our findings indicate that herbivores harbor significantly higher microbial diversity than carnivores. In contrast, carnivores show considerable variability in their microbiota—even among individuals of the same species. This high variability has methodological implications, emphasizing the need to analyze at least 10 samples per species for reliable results. When examining only three to six samples per species, we observed major differences in the presence of various bacterial families. However, starting from 10 samples, most within-species fluctuations stabilized, yielding consistent and dependable outcomes. 
O’ Donnell et al have found:  Our diversity indices revealed that, although the microbiota of ruminants was more diverse than that of hindgut-fermenting species, it was still less diverse when compared to other microbiota sequencing studies. Specifically, we observed lower phylotype diversity in hindgut fermenters, ruminants, and monogastric animals than that reported for the distal gut microbiota of other animals (Lamendella et al., 2011; Pitta et al., 2010). The phylotype estimates we obtained for the animal species ranged from 415 to 660, which falls within the range estimated for the human microbiota (Claesson et al., 2009; Nam et al., 2011) but is lower than our earlier estimates for hindgut microbiota (O’Donnell et al., 2013). 
The excerpts highlighted from Zoelzer et al. and O'Donnell et al. have been interpreted as touching upon one of the most fundamental ecological principles in microbiology: the architecture and stability of the gut ecosystem are dictated by the host's diet. The observation that herbivorous microbiomes are consistently more stable and diverse than their carnivorous counterparts has not been regarded as an anomaly; rather, it has been understood as a direct reflection of metabolic thermodynamics and structural biology.
When these animal models are extrapolated to human dietary patterns—specifically, plant-based or high-fiber diets versus meat-based or high-protein regimens—the available evidence has been shown to overwhelmingly indicate that plant-heavy diets foster a more robust and health-promoting microbial ecosystem. In the following analysis, a breakdown is provided of the specific differences between these microbiota sets, the reasons why the herbivorous pattern is widely considered to be "healthier," and the manner in which these dynamics offer foundational pillars for the theory of bacterial multicellularity.
5.4.2. Distinct Microbiota Sets: The Herbivore vs. Carnivore Divide
The ecological divergence between a plant-based (herbivorous) microbiome and a meat-based (carnivorous) microbiome is fundamentally dictated by the enzymatic machinery required to degrade the host's dietary substrates.
1. The Herbivorous (Plant-Based) Profile
The CAZyme Arsenal:
 Plant cell walls, composed of cellulose, hemicellulose, and pectin, are highly recalcitrant polymers that resist digestion by mammalian host enzymes. Consequently, a plant-based diet imposes a strong selective pressure for bacteria equipped with extensive arrays of Carbohydrate-Active Enzymes (CAZymes) (El Kaoutari et al., 2013) .
Dominant Taxa:
 In human cohorts, high-fiber diets consistently select for specific commensal genera, most notably Prevotella, Roseburia, Faecalibacterium, and Bifidobacterium (Wu et al., 2011 ; David et al., 2014 ).
High Alpha Diversity:
 Because plant polysaccharides possess highly varied and complex molecular architectures, they create thousands of distinct biochemical micro-niches within the gastrointestinal tract. This spatial and metabolic heterogeneity supports a high degree of alpha diversity, allowing specialized bacterial species to coexist without succumbing to competitive exclusion (Sonnenburg & Sonnenburg, 2014) .
2. The Carnivorous (Meat-Based) Profile
Bile-Tolerant and Putrefactive Selection:
 Animal proteins and lipids are predominantly digested and absorbed in the upper gastrointestinal tract via host enzymatic and biliary activity. The substrates that reach the colon are largely residual proteins and high concentrations of primary bile acids. Thus, an animal-heavy diet selects for highly bile-tolerant, putrefactive microbial communities (David et al., 2014) .
Dominant Taxa:
 Diets high in animal protein and fat trigger rapid structural shifts in the microbiome, characterized by blooms of Bacteroides, Alistipes, Bilophila wadsworthia, and Fusobacterium (David et al., 2014) .
Low Alpha Diversity:
 Because proteins and lipids are structurally less complex than varied plant fibers, they provide fewer discrete ecological niches. This leads to a narrower, more volatile microbial community structure, corroborating observations of reduced microbial stability in carnivorous mammals.
5.4.3. Metabolic Outputs and Host Health Implications
The systemic health impacts of these two distinct ecosystems are determined almost entirely by their metabolic effluents—the bioactive byproducts of microbial fermentation.
1. The Herbivorous Output: Barrier Integrity and Anti-Inflammation
Short-Chain Fatty Acids (SCFAs): 
The microbial fermentation of indigestible plant fibers yields high concentrations of SCFAs, primarily butyrate, acetate, and propionate. Butyrate serves as the principal energy substrate for colonocytes. Furthermore, it actively enhances intestinal barrier integrity by upregulating tight junction proteins and epigenetically induces the differentiation of regulatory T-cells (Tregs), thereby actively suppressing systemic host inflammation (Furusawa et al., 2013 ; Smith et al., 2013 ).
2. The Carnivorous Output: Endotoxemia and DNA Damage
TMAO Production:
 Gut microbiota specialized in meat digestion rapidly metabolize L-carnitine and choline—compounds abundant in red meat and eggs—into trimethylamine (TMA). The host liver subsequently oxidizes TMA into trimethylamine N-oxide (TMAO), a circulating metabolite that acts as a direct, causal driver of atherosclerosis and cardiovascular disease (Koeth et al., 2013) .
Genotoxic Metabolites:
 Putrefactive bacteria ferment residual animal proteins into highly reactive, toxic metabolites, including ammonia, p-cresol, and hydrogen sulfide. Hydrogen sulfide is particularly deleterious; it functions as a potent genotoxin that induces DNA double-strand breaks in colonic epithelium, significantly contributing to the elevated risk of colorectal carcinogenesis associated with high red meat consumption (Windey et al., 2012) .
5.4.4. Implications for the Biofilm Multicellularity Theory
Analyzing dietary impacts through the lens of evolutionary microbiology yields a profound argument for the multicellular nature of microbial biofilms. Specifically, a complex, plant-based diet compels bacteria to operate as a highly integrated multicellular entity.
1. Obligate Division of Labor (Metabolic Cross-Feeding)
 Complex plant polysaccharides are too massive and structurally diverse for any single bacterial cell to independently degrade. To extract energy efficiently, the gut biofilm must execute highly organized metabolic cross-feeding cascades.
•	Primary degraders (e.g., Bacteroides cellulosilyticus) secrete extracellular enzymes to cleave massive fiber chains into smaller oligosaccharides.
•	Secondary fermenters (e.g., Faecalibacterium prausnitzii) subsequently metabolize these smaller saccharides to synthesize end-products like butyrate (Flint et al., 2012) . This mechanism is not coincidental scavenging; it constitutes an obligate division of labor. The bacterial constituents rely entirely on the enzymatic output of their neighbors, functioning collectively as a multi-layered, interdependent digestive tissue (Rakoff-Nahoum et al., 2016) .
2. Structural Scaffolding for the Biofilm:
 Insoluble plant fibers provide more than nutritional sustenance; they act as physical architecture. High dietary fiber intake supplies undigested particulate matter that functions as a physical lattice within the intestinal lumen. Bacteria utilize quorum-sensing networks to attach to this lattice and construct mature, three-dimensional biofilms (Macfarlane & Macfarlane, 2006) . Conversely, a low-fiber, carnivorous diet deprives the colon of this structural scaffolding, forcing the microbiota into a chaotic, planktonic, and highly volatile state.
3. The Biofilm as an Outsourced Organ:
 In herbivores and humans consuming plant-predominant diets, the host and the biofilm have co-evolved a profound biological dependency. The host provides the raw substrate (fiber) and a controlled, anaerobic fermentation chamber (the colon). In exchange, the multicellular biofilm functions as an outsourced, specialized organ, executing complex metabolic programs (via CAZymes) absent from the host's own genome. This seamless physiological integration effectively blurs the taxonomic boundaries between host and microbe, substantiating the holobiont and multicellularity paradigms (Rosenberg & Zilber-Rosenberg, 2016) .
5.4.5. Conclusion: 
Within the framework of the multicellularity theory, this body of evidence has been interpreted as perhaps the most elegant demonstration to date that the multicellular organism should be understood as a co-adapted unit shaped by dietary niche. Herbivores and their associated microbes have been proposed to have co-diversified over millions of years, giving rise to specialized metabolic partnerships that enable the exploitation of plant-based diets. When this partnership is disrupted—whether by captivity or by evolutionary constraints—the integrity of the entire multicellular unit is thought to be compromised.
The tripartite mutualism among plants, herbivores, and microbes, as revealed by research on plant secondary compounds, extends the multicellular concept beyond a simple host-plus-microbes framework to include dietary plants as active participants in the shaping of the ecosystem. Consequently, multicellular organisms are not merely to be viewed as walking communities of host and microbial cells; rather, they are recognized as being embedded within larger ecological networks that span across biological kingdoms. 
5.5. Natural remedies as antibiofilm:
5.5.1. Natural remedies are good source for antibiofilms. 
The investigation into natural anti-biofilm agents represents a profound paradigm shift in antimicrobial therapeutics. Historically, modern pharmacology has prioritized biocidal antibiotics designed to target free-floating, planktonic bacteria. However, biofilms function as recalcitrant, organized multicellular tissues, rendering them intrinsically tolerant to conventional single-cell biocidal strategies (Flemming et al., 2016).
Plants and natural compounds have co-evolved alongside bacteria over millions of years. Consequently, the botanical world has developed sophisticated secondary metabolites that do not merely exert bacteriostatic or bactericidal effects on individual cells, but actively dismantle the multicellular infrastructure of the collective (Sánchez et al., 2016) .
Lu L, et al have confirmed the following: As noted in this review, natural resources provide a vast repository for discovering anti-biofilm agents. In recent years, increasing attention has been given to the health benefits of plants and natural foods. To date, numerous studies have explored how natural products can inhibit bacterial biofilm formation and progression, indicating their potential as alternative treatments for bacterial infections. Current evidence shows that most natural anti-biofilm agents yield promising preclinical results across various bacterial species. Their proposed mechanism of action primarily involves disrupting distinct stages of biofilm development or interfering with quorum sensing (QS) pathways. Over the past two decades, techniques such as chromatographic separation have facilitated the screening and isolation of effective anti-biofilm compounds. Nevertheless, many studies on plant-derived extracts with anti-biofilm activity fail to identify the molecular structures of the active compounds, highlighting the need for further research. 
Song, Xun & Xia, et. al. have found: Results: A total of 95 relevant references were identified. Phytochemicals, fractions, and extracts were categorized based on their general classes or putative active ingredients. Over 90 bioactive compounds with anti-biofilm properties have been isolated from various plant parts. The inhibitory concentrations of these natural products were also reported, and some were further studied for their potential anti-biofilm mechanisms.
Conclusion: This review provides strong evidence that plants serve as a valuable source of diverse natural compounds, offering potential for the development of preventive and therapeutic strategies against biofilm-associated infections. 
The following is a systematic categorization of potent natural anti-biofilm agents, delineated by their mechanism of disruption within the bacterial ecosystem.
5.5.2. Quorum Sensing Inhibitors: Disrupting Intercellular Communication
To maintain a cohesive multicellular structure, bacteria depend on highly regulated chemical communication pathways. Quorum Sensing (QS) inhibitors also referred to as quorum quenchers act as signal antagonists. By interfering with these pathways, they effectively silence intercellular communication, causing the multicellular collective to disintegrate into disorganized, planktonic states.
Garlic (Allium sativum) and Ajoene: 
While allicin is recognized for its broad-spectrum antimicrobial properties, ajoene, a sulfur-rich derivative of garlic, functions as a potent QS inhibitor. Research indicates that ajoene specifically targets and accelerates the degradation of key regulatory proteins (e.g., LuxR and RhlR) within the QS circuits of Pseudomonas aeruginosa and Staphylococcus aureus. This disruption significantly downregulates the genetic expression necessary for biofilm maintenance (Jakobsen et al., 2012) .
Curcumin (Curcuma longa): 
The primary bioactive compound in turmeric exhibits profound anti-biofilm efficacy. Curcumin acts as a competitive antagonist, physically binding to QS receptors and preventing the attachment of native autoinducers. Furthermore, under photodynamic therapy (blue light exposure), curcumin generates localized reactive oxygen species (ROS) that induce severe genetic and structural damage to the biofilm's signaling pathways (Loo et al., 2016 ; Packiavathy et al., 2014 ).
Cinnamaldehyde (from Cinnamon): 
This phenylpropanoid compound drastically downregulates the expression of operons associated with flagellar motility and the synthesis of the Extracellular Polymeric Substance (EPS) matrix. This effectively paralyzes the bacteria, preventing the spatial organization required to form a structured microbial colony (Brackman et al., 2008) .
5.5.3. EPS Matrix Degraders: Structural Destabilization
While QS inhibitors disrupt intercellular communication, other natural compounds act by dissolving the physical architecture—the EPS matrix—that binds the multicellular tissue together.
Manuka Honey (Methylglyoxal - MGO): 
Distinct from standard honey, Manuka honey contains exceptionally high concentrations of methylglyoxal (MGO). MGO is a highly reactive dicarbonyl compound that induces cross-linking of proteins and extracellular DNA (eDNA) within the biofilm's matrix. This biochemical interference destabilizes the three-dimensional architecture of the biofilm, thereby rendering the embedded bacteria susceptible to host immune clearance and conventional antibiotics (Lu et al., 2014) .
Proteolytic Plant Enzymes (Bromelain and Papain): 
Extracted from Ananas comosus (pineapple) and Carica papaya (papaya), these natural proteases effectively degrade proteinaceous structural components. Many pathogenic biofilms, including those formed by Streptococcus species, rely heavily on surface proteins and proteinaceous matrix strands to maintain physical integrity. Bromelain and papain enzymatically cleave these structural tethers, actively dissolving the biofilm scaffold (Khosropanah et al., 2012) .
III. Anti-Adhesion Agents: Inhibiting Multicellular Assembly
The critical initial phase of biofilm pathogenesis is irreversible cellular attachment. If planktonic cells are prevented from adhering to a biotic or abiotic surface, they cannot initiate the phenotypic differentiation required to establish a sessile, multicellular community.
Cranberry (A-type Proanthocyanidins - PACs): 
Cranberry extract is widely documented for its prophylactic efficacy against urinary tract infections, an outcome driven primarily by an anti-biofilm mechanism. The A-type PACs physically bind to the P-fimbriae (adhesion organelles) of uropathogenic Escherichia coli. By capping these fimbriated structures, the bacteria are rendered physically incapable of anchoring to the uroepithelium and are subsequently expelled via micturition (Howell et al., 2005) .
Ginseng Extract (Panax ginseng): 
Beyond its established adaptogenic properties, ginseng contains ginsenosides that have been shown to inhibit the formation of bacterial appendages necessary for surface attachment. This actively prevents the initial scaffolding required for Pseudomonas and Burkholderia biofilms from taking root (Wu et al., 2011) .
5.5.4. Membrane Disruptors: Essential Oils
Essential oils are highly concentrated, volatile hydrophobic compounds that disrupt the specialized lipid profiles of biofilm-associated cells, which differ significantly from those of their planktonic counterparts.
Carvacrol and Thymol (from Oregano and Thyme): 
These phenolic monoterpenes intercalate directly into the bacterial lipid bilayer. Because biofilm-associated cells rely heavily on specific membrane dynamics to secrete EPS, the incorporation of carvacrol forces the membrane to become hyper-permeable. This induces the leakage of intracellular ATP and essential ions, starving the collective of the energetic resources required for matrix synthesis (Nostro et al., 2007) .
Berberine (from Goldenseal and Barberry): 
Berberine is an isoquinoline alkaloid that not only disrupts the structural integrity of the lipid membrane but also intercalates directly into bacterial DNA. This halts the essential replication processes necessary for a biofilm to expand its ecological territory (Chu et al., 2016) .
5.5.5. Substantiating the Theory of Microbial Multicellularity
Framing plant-derived antimicrobial mechanisms in this ecological context provides a robust theoretical argument for the multicellularity of bacteria.
When botanical organisms successfully defend against bacterial infection, they rarely rely on agents that indiscriminately lyse single cells. Instead, plants synthesize highly specific, complex molecules (e.g., ajoene, A-type PACs) designed precisely to disrupt tissue-level organization, cellular differentiation (adhesion), and systemic communication (quorum sensing). The evolutionary reality that nature's most effective antibacterial defenses target the collective, rather than the individual cell, offers compelling evidence that bacteria exist and operate primarily as unified, multicellular organisms (Shapiro, 1998 ; Flemming et al., 2016 ).
In the context of the multicellularity theory, these findings reveal that nature has already evolved sophisticated mechanisms for managing microbial ecosystems. Plants produce targeted compounds that disrupt pathogenic biofilm architecture while arguably preserving beneficial or benign microbial communities a selective precision that broad-spectrum pharmaceutical antibiotics lack. This ecological insight, encoded within plant phytochemistry, provides a translational framework for developing therapeutics that modulate microbial multicellularity rather than attempting to eradicate it.
5.6. Mense and the uterine and cervical microbiota: 
5.6.1. A review:
Critchley HOD, Babayev E, Bulun SE, Clark S, Garcia-Grau I, Gregersen PK, Kilcoyne A, Kim JJ, Lavender M, Marsh EE, Matteson KA, Maybin JA, Metz CN, Moreno I, Silk K, Sommer M, Simon C, Tariyal R, Taylor HS, Wagner GP, Griffith LG. have stated:  Changes in hormone levels during the menstrual cycle are considered key drivers of the microbial environment in the reproductive tract. Throughout the cycle, rising amounts of estrogen and progesterone are associated with greater stability of the microbial community, whereas during menstruation, the microbiota becomes more susceptible to shifts in bacterial composition. While some women exhibit consistent microbial patterns across the entire cycle—especially those colonized by Lactobacillus crispatus—most experience fluctuations tied to menses. These changes often involve a transition from a Lactobacillus-dominant community to one characterized by Lactobacillus iners, Gardnerella vaginalis, Gram-positive cocci, or other bacteria linked to dysbiosis. Following menstruation, the original community structure tends to be restored.
The rise in L. iners and G. vaginalis during menstruation may be attributed to their ability to thrive under challenging conditions. For instance, although G. vaginalis cannot grow when iron is scarce, it produces vaginolysin, a toxin that breaks down host cells (such as red blood cells) to release iron. Meanwhile, certain Lactobacillus strains possess protective mechanisms that allow them to grow even in the presence of iron. L. crispatus, for example, carries an iron transport system. Similarly, L. iners produces a unique iron sulfur protein cluster that helps it capture iron from menstrual blood, giving it a competitive edge over Lactobacillus gasseri and Lactobacillus jensenii in the vaginal environment during menstruation.. 
Most women will have a change or shift of the uterine microbiota during menses. This as a result means that humans whom have intercourse during menses are more liable to change the microbiota of their counterparts to more harmful microbiota.
5.6.2. Menstruation-Induced Shifts in the Vaginal Microbiome
Empirical evidence consistently demonstrates that menstruation induces a transient ecological destabilization within the vaginal microbiome, frequently resulting in a shift toward dysbiotic, non-beneficial bacterial strains.
While some individuals maintain a highly stable, Lactobacillus crispatus-dominant microbiome throughout their menstrual cycle, a significant cohort experiences a temporary depletion of protective lactobacilli. This depletion is accompanied by a concurrent proliferation of obligate and strict anaerobes associated with Bacterial Vaginosis (BV), such as Gardnerella vaginalis, Prevotella species, and Lactobacillus iners (Gajer et al., 2012) .
Primary Mechanisms of Menstrual Dysbiosis:
Alkalization via Menstrual Effluent: 
A homeostatic vaginal environment is highly acidic (pH 3.5–4.5), a state maintained by the lactic acid production of commensal Lactobacilli. Menstrual blood, however, is slightly alkaline (pH ~7.4). The introduction of this effluent buffers and neutralizes the acidic mucosal environment. Dysbiotic pathobionts thrive in this elevated pH, whereas beneficial Lactobacilli experience suppressed growth (Eschenbach et al., 2000) .
Estrogen Depletion and Glycogen Availability: 
Systemic estrogen stimulates the accumulation of glycogen within vaginal epithelial cells, which serves as the primary fermentable substrate for Lactobacilli. During the perimenstrual phase, estrogen levels decline precipitously, effectively starving the commensal bacteria of their primary metabolic resource (Mirmonsef et al., 2014) .
Community State Type (CST) Transitions: 
Longitudinal genomic mapping reveals that during menstruation, women frequently transition from Community State Type I (CST I, L. crispatus dominant) to CST III (L. iners dominant) or CST IV (a highly diverse state lacking lactobacilli), before reverting to homeostasis following the cessation of menses (Gajer et al., 2012) .
5.6.3. The Impact of High-Frequency Sexual Activity
In populations characterized by high-frequency sexual intercourse, such as female commercial sex workers (FSWs), the microbiome dynamics involve similar mechanisms but often manifest as chronic dysbiosis rather than transient shifts.
Semen as a Chronic Alkalizing Agent: 
Similar to menstrual effluent, seminal fluid is highly alkaline (pH 7.2–8.0). High-frequency exposure to semen chronically neutralizes vaginal acidity, impeding the re-establishment of a Lactobacillus-dominant state and maintaining an ecological window conducive to the proliferation of G. vaginalis and other BV-associated bacteria (Borgdorff et al., 2014 ).
Pathogen Introduction and Microbial Exchange:
 Frequent exposure to exogenous microbial communities from multiple partners continuously challenges the native vaginal flora, driving ecological instability and increasing the risk of pathogenic colonization.
Chronic Baseline Dysbiosis: 
Epidemiological studies indicate that FSWs globally exhibit a significantly higher baseline prevalence of CST IV (highly diverse, BV-associated microbiota). Consequently, the menstrual cycle may not induce a drastic ecological shift in these individuals, as their baseline microbiome already exists in an entrenched, non-Lactobacillus-dominant state (Jespers et al., 2014) .
5.6.4. Advantages of Multicellularity and Biofilm Formation
